EOR THE RED PAGE.
To his poetic volume (“ In Middle Harbor
and Other Yerse ”) Thomas Heney added
the other day a volume of prose—“ The Girl
at Birrell’s ” —a story which takes at once
Australian classic rank, and a permanent
shelf-place beside “Windabyne.” Of liter-
ary or general interest it has, indeed, not
much; there is no direct attempt at humor
or pathos; and the style is simple and
straightforward, occasionally elevated, oc-
casionally awkward. Yet the book has
some claim to be considered the most
characteristically Australian book pub-
lished. Its theme is the not uncommon one
of station-life in western New South Wales
a score of years ago ; the plot is a natural
and familiar plot, with numerous pauses to
describe bush scenery and bush episodes—a
storm, a shearing, a tramp lost on the track ;
the men and women are bygone types which
you must now go far afield to find. And not
alone in the sombre story, in the stiff and
sombre style, but in a certain intangible
mental atmosphere which surrounds the book,
Heney has reflected the spirit of the Austra-
lian character and the Australian landscape
as perhaps they have never been reflected
before. Some of Lawson’s sketches leave the
same momentary impression ; but Heney has
the advantage of cumulus and climax, and
his note becomes as deep and fuller—a note
of grim monotony, abiding melancholy, of
alien and inexorable Fate.
* * * *
Mr. Heney is a remarkably close observer,
with a remarkably retentive memory, and his
work is in all essentials an exact transcript
from life. He paints his figures with minute,
patient fidelity, plodding persistently on till
the canvas is covered. He has a keen eye
for colour, and a fine command of colour-
language, yet his tone is a uniform grey :
“He must paint what lies before him.”
This method of accurate description causes
Mr. Heney’s book to be uncommonly real.
With better art, it could have been made
stronger, hardly truer. The old-time bush
parson, the Sunday in the bush, the shearers
at work and play, and kindred photographs,
are all admirably done ; there is some-
thing Australian in the very feel of them ;
they have the Australian instinct and ex-
pression—the Australian “ look-out,” as it
were. Pater has a remark that thought and
style should fit as soul and body—one im-
pressing the other, the other expressing the
one—a perfect harmony. The reticence and
severity of Mr. Heney’s style are so suited
to his thought, and harmonise with the
reticence and severity of the bush and the
bushman. And the title of his book might
have been “Man was Made to Mourn.”
* * • *
And now Mr. Heney may speak for himself
of the men whom he found in the shearers’
hut at the close of the shearers’ day :
They were men of many ages, though none were
old, while some were little more than lads. Most
of them incontestably were Australian-born and
country-bred, tall, lithe, well-made fellows, faces
tanned by an unsparing climate, dark-bearded and
moustached, big and hard of hand, long-armed,
sinewy rather than full at thigh and shoulder.
Some of them were handsome in a virile fashion,
with bold and massive features, calm eyes, closed
mouth, firm jaw. Nothing of the fopling, nothing
in any degree effeminate, attach e.d to the pick
of these stalwart fellows. They looked men as
they stretched out their clean and shapely limbs in
the genial glow of the fire, and the loose shirt, as
they changed position, sank on firm outlines of
breast and shoulder; the trousers fitted closely to
the muscular leg. These were the older men,
seldom more than 40, generally 30 or a little over
—men who had shorn sheep since they were
“ Kindchen ’’—bits of lads they meant, who liked
the life and kept up their reputation as ringers”
at the board, as “toffs” in the bush public-houßes
and hamlets, as thorough good workmen, wild
devils in a spree, staunoh friends and ugly foes.
* * * •
And bo Mr. Heney goes on to complete a
picture which space will not permit to copy,
closing his chapter with a glimpse of the shed
by moonlight
It is suoh a divine night as they have in Riverina
half the lunar month throughout the winter, yet
few trouble to notice, far less to enjoy its beauty.
High in a pale-blue sky the moon seems to halt,
while not a star is near, and only the greater con-
stellations can be perceived in the mild radiance.
There is absolute stillness; not even a vague, fitful
night-wind breathes discord. . . . The whole
impression of the night was a perfect and instant
peace, commanded by Nature’s very seii.
Here was the building where all day long the
confused and painful noises of sordid labour rose
and spread. What an enchantment! Flooded
with soft light, black shadow here and there cast-
ing out high light on roof and angle, it seemed some
fairy pavilion, so graceful, so unsubstantial, that
before another glance it might be dissipated into
the elements which bodied it forth. The kind
radiance touched all; the ground seemed level,
the bush symmetrical: all that could mar the
purity and serenity of this night had been touched
with magic so that it became invisible.
* * * *
There are many such poetical passages in
“ A Girl at Birrell’s”: in fact, you almost
get Thomas Heney’s poetry better in his
prose than in his verse : he is (or was)
cramped by rhyme and metre. Such a slow,
uncouth, lumbering Muse it is who leads
his ardent soul in the painful strife for
expression ! Mr. Heney has unquestionably
a fine poetic sense : his Nature-vision matches
Wordsworth’s ; but he is all eye and no ear,
with hardly a whisper of voice. He is a
corduroy poet —bump ! —jolt ! —bump ! —yet
you get along, and the road might be worse.
And in his verse, as in his prose, he relies on
literal, truthful description for his effects. If
he had only music ! But he blunders on
with his discords and doesn’t seem to care.
In the lines “For an Anniversary of All
Saints,” he remarks :
But the Church remembers her servants only,
And strictly stated in prayer and creed
Are clauses and terms of the heavenly oovenant
By which they have won the awarded meed.
And here is a specimen still blanker :
Five years ago, a boy, I went to make
Holiday with a brother living near ;
He, first a stockman in the north, went out
Into far Queensland, and thence many times
Had brought down herds of cattle half gone wild
In deserts on the Cooper or Paroo.
This is a weary world.
* * * *
About three-fourths of Mr. Heney’s verse
are of this calibre and little weightier ; the
other fourth has lines like—
The bridle reins hang loose in the hold of his lean
left hand—
verses like—
Back from his sweat-wet hair his felt is carelessly
placed,
Handkerchief at his throat, sagging shirt round a
lank firm waist;
True to the set of strong loins the belted moleskins
are tight,
Plain from forehead to stirrup a virile vigour in
sight—
and poems like “ Drought,” “ Found Dead,”
and “A Squatter of ’68.” “At the Tank
before Day ” is not so good as some others,
but it is quotable and characteristic :
AT THE TANK BEFORE DAY.
In the air the stars are dusk, yet the slow ap-
proach of day
Makes a wan dawn in a hand’s-breadth of the
sky, '
And the twilight like a mist seems to melt and
fade away ;
Darkness yonder, only dimness is anigh.
Here few nights bring dewy cool, and the dawn
air dry and hot
Holds the senses like a languor long and deep ;
Seeing, hearing, thinking nought; past and pre-
sent all forgot,
Thus to wake, to lie, is as a dream of sleep.
Through a dry smell of the earth from the piny
bush around
Slow faint savours as of incense rise and spread,
Out afar the hills loom dark, with dim lines their
mass is crowned,
And pale the stars together blench o’erhead.
Beyond the pallid mirror of the water in the
tank,
Shapes ’twixt earth and air in twilight came
and went,
Silent, ghost-like, swift, save that as they rose
and sank
In the sullen hush a heavy noise was sent.
* * * *
Crude and faulty enough, this; but poetic—
and true. Wherefore, may we not sum up in
some lines which Robert Richardson has
written for The Bulletin ?
PAN IS NOT DEAD.
Pan is not dead : he lives (9-day
Here in our South-land, far away
From Tempe’s streams in Thessaly,
Where once he reigned in pride and glee I
And ye who list may hear him play,
By creek and cove and sunlit bay,
His slender pipes, or sad or gay,
Touching his reed-stops piercingly—
Pan is not dead.
And if perchance his Southern lay
Be fashioned in a wider way
Than shepherds heard in Arcady,
Still is it fresh and strong and free,
And wider yet Bhall grow its sway—
Pan is not dead.
R eprints.
LNot much seems to be in Australia known of Arthur
Symons, and a reoent printing upon this page of a few of
his verses has aroused several importuning Olivers. He
is one of the pretty English minors who appear in limited
editions of five hundred or so, and are generally snapped
up to the last copy by a congregation of un- or discrimin-
ating worshippers. His prose (literary and critical; often
for the Academy) does not appear to have been collected;
his verse is titled “Days and Nights” (1889),
“ Silhouettes ” (1892), and “ London Nights ” (1895). He
is an English University product, 31 years old; and his
imagination seems warming as he ages. “London
Nights,” indeed, from which most of the following are
drawn, contains some things quite too warm to be printed
in a naughty paper like The Bulletin in a nice country
like Australia—where, as editors know, there still exists
a very provincial regard for the “proprieties” of Art.
(An improper Past has so heavy responsibilities.) Perhaps
there is in Arthur Symons some taint of modern decad-
ence. But he is dexterous, and a poet, and human, and
sweet . . . and ah Eros! and O Eros! and 0 Eros, Eros!]
THE OPIUM-SMOKER.
I am engulfed, and drown deliciously.
Soft music like a perfume, and sweet light
Golden with audible odours exquisite,
Swathe me with cerements for eternity.
Time is no more. I pause and yet I flee.
A million ages wrap me round with night.
I drain a million ages of delight.
I hold the future in my memory.
Also I have this garret which I rent,
This bed of straw, and this that was a chair,
This worn-out body like a tattered tent,
This crust, of which the rats have eaten part,
This pipe of opium; rage, remorse, despair;
This soul at pawn, and this delirious heart.
PARIS.
My Paris is a land where twilight days
Merge into violent nights of black and gold ;
Where, it may be, the flower of dawn is cold :
Ah, but the gold nights, and the soented ways !
Eyelids of women, little curls of hair,
A little nose curved softly, like a shell,
A red mouth like a wound, a mocking veil :
Phantoms, before the dawn, how phantom-fair !
And every woman with beseeching eyes,
Or with enticing eyes, or amorous,
Offers herself, a rose, and craves of us
A rose’s place among our memories.
KISSES.
Sweet, can I sing you the song of your kisses 1
How soft is this one, how subtle this is;
How fluttering swift as a bird’s kiss tha’t is,
As a bird that taps at a leafy lattice ;
How this one clings and how that uncloses
From bud to flower in the way of roses ;
And this through laughter and that through-
weeping
Swims to the brim where Love lies sleeping;
And this in a pout I snatch, and capture
That in the ecstasy of rapture,
When the odorous red-rose petals par|
That my lips may find their way to the heart
Of the rose of the world, your lips, my rose.
But no song knows
The way of my heart to the heart of my roses
ESCALADE.
Tenderly as a bee that sips,
Your kisses settle on my lips,
And your soft cheek begins to oreep
Like the downy wing of Sleep
Along my cheek, and nestles smiling,
As if Love’s truth were but beguiling,
Too utterly content to move,
Only to smile, only to love.
But if, to tease you, as I use^
I feign, unthankful, to refuse
Your dear caresses, and turn cold,
Then the shy lips, waxing bold,
Advance to vanquish my resistance,
And, with a passionate persistence,
Clinging closer, fold on fold,
They suok my lips into their hold.
And if, still feigning, I resist,
Fondly feigning, to be kissed,
They wax still bolder, and begin \
Hungrily to fasten in
Upon my neck, as they would gloat
On the protesting veins that tingle
As they and your deep kisses mingle,
Your kisses burning in my throat.
But, ah ! if, lastly, I should hear
Your sudden lips upon my ear
Set my brain buzzing, and my blood
Beating the measure of your mood,
And pouring over me and under
Scented billows of soft thunder,
I yield, I’ll love you, but let be :
I yield, ere you quite murder me.

T. O O K!
BEFORE YOU
T. E A P!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and 0,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
G-eorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
fihe ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A French scientist, by aid of an electric
furnace, is producing a compound of boracic acid
and carbon, so hard that it will actually cut dia-
monds.
A recent death of a young farmer was from
blood-poisoning, caused by some artificial manure
getting into a scratch on the hand during scatter-
ing. Moral: use gloves when manuring.
Vic. Government, with a view to encour-
aging fig-culture, has imported a large number of
young fig-plants direct from Smyrna, and dis-
tributed them free. Smyrna fig-trees often grow
to be over a yard in trunk-diameter.
“GANYMEDE.”
My dear, brave love —much lovelier than woman
Could ever be—is “more than common tall” ;
His eyes (the light in them is hardly human,
More like the stars) are deep and true ; and all
His body sweet is firm with outlines strong
And not too round, with short lines here and
there
To break monotony of straightness. Long
Are his straight limbs, and slender-long his feet
And nervous fingers ; and his curling hair
Is brown most soft; and his strong heart’s strong
beat
Rings out the fearless note of health. And see—
That floating thistle-seed falls not more light
Than his firm touch that sends fierce thrills through
me.
This grass knows how his heels—this grass dew-
bright—
(First of his footfall) lightly-sternly strike
And light as his free heart, rise up again.
Not lounging Paris is my hero like ;
But Mars’ rude strength, with Phoebus’ power to
pain
Or cheer with tender art, are his ; as rain
His voice is soft, as rain in August falling
On flowers softly—clear as magpie calling
Unto his mate when sunrise comes again.
I would iny tongue as Sappho’s tunefid were
To sing this lithe young native, or that air
Of his sweet breath inspired me now and near—
Then might I rhyme as Spenser of my dear.
Adelaide.
LALAGE.
HIPPED.
The white clouds lie on a sullen ridge
And gloomily watch the dirt-gray town ;
The creek has swollen a grimy brown,
And swirls ungainly below the bridge ;
The sky’s a swamp, and the roads are mire,
And I sit by myself and kick the tire—
Damn it !
A draught crawls up from the leaky floor ;
The damp creeps out from a mouldy wall;
The wood’s burnt black, and the stuff they call
“Best Newcastle coal ” is but one fraud more ;
My nerves are stretched to the breaking-point,
And all creation is out of joint—
Damn it !
The water’s tepid, the whisky raw,
The landlord shuffles in squeaky boots ;
A long, dull blundering nerve-pain shoots
From aching temple to aching jaw;
The fire is taking a “Smoke ho ! ” spell—
I wish to God I was warm in hell,
Damn it!
J.S.S.
A REPLY.
“ Man wants but little,” said the sage,
And who will say he said amiss
That finds upon the printed page
His warm and fragrant Bower of Bliss ?
The backlogs in the fireplace hiss,
The pipes are full; the friends are true.
My summum bonum comes to this :
A pipe, a book, a friend or two,
Some rush round half the world to find
What found but Fairy gold may prove,
While others, with unflinching mind,
Toil, iron gates of Power to move.
But those who keep my quiet groove
Nor wealth nor power aspire to woo ;
But have for their impartial love
A pipe, a book, a friend or two.
“ Selfish ” you say, and bid me write
“ Songs that with self-denial glow.”
No scorner I of those who, bright
With smiles, to cheer the wretched go.
All sorts to make a world you know !
To his own kind let each be true ;
I keep to mine, dear girl, and so—
A pipe, a book, a friend or two.
Sydney.
EOXLEY.

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 22, 1896.
The Proposed Sydney County Council.
About eight months ago, in its issue of 28th
December, 1895, The Bulletin first seriously
raised the question of establishing a County
Council to govern Sydney and its environs.
The question was again raised at greater
length in its issue of J une 6, and since then
the mattter has been taken up in other quar-
ters, until it really looks as if the movement
might result in some good for the over-
burdened city of the 40 municipalities and
the 396 aldermen. So far, however, it is
almost entirely, if not entirely, a newspaper
movement.. There is no sign of much popular
interest in the matter, and no evidence that
the public has begun to realise the advantages
of municipal federation, or is thinking about
it to any great extent. The cornstalk, unfor-
tunately, does not take his municipal institu-
tions seriously ; nor does he understand of
what immense value they might be under
better circumstances. And the people of
Sydney in particular are, in the main, placidly
contented with their ugly, tangled, common-
place city, where there is hardly one street
which keeps a straight line for 400 consecu-
tive yards ; and are blankly unconscious of
its defects, because most of them know no
other city, and have no distinct idea of what
it might be made if there was any central
authority with power and intelligence and
energy enough to undertake the work. In
short, the County Council idea falls flat
with the mass of the community, because it
hasn’t begun to realise the great want which
that institution would be especially qualified
to fill.
* * * *
The financial aspect of the case has been
already dealt with. In Sydney and its im-
mediate surroundings there are 40 munici-
palities, and 40 mayors and town clerks and
staffs of municipal officials, and 396 aldermen,
and 35 municipal debts, and 27 municipal
bank overdrafts, and almost as many separate
town-halls as there are municipalities ; and
the waste involved under this system is
necessarily enormous. The 40 councils spend
among them about £35,000 a year on salaries,
at least one-third of which might be saved by
consolidation. There is no central authority
which can undertake the lighting of the
whole area by gas or electricity supplied
from municipally-owned works ; a private
syndicate can get power to supply the whole
40 municipalities from one central establish-
ment, but if the work is undertaken—as it
should be—by the municipal authorities it
means, under the present system, the estab-
lishment of 40 separate works and the erec-
tion at ruinous expense of 40 separate plants,
therefore the existing state of things offers a
big premium to the private, syndicate and
handicaps municipal enterprise almost hope-
lessly. The debts of the suburban munici-
palities bear interest at an average rate of
over per cent., whereas a County Council
could easily consolidate them into a uniform
per cent, stock and thereby save nearly
£16,000 a year in interest alone. Moreover,
the new institution would be an affair of some
weight and dignity, and would attract the ser-
vices of able men who now hold aloof from
the squalid circumstances of suburban parish
politics. These are a few of the more imme-
diate reasons for municipal consolidation,
and they should be sufficiently weighty to
command attention. The County Council
system should save at least £50,000 a year
in useless expenses of one kind and another,
and it would open up great possibilities in
the way of municipal socialism, and make
the municipal government an institution of
almost as much value to the Democratic party
as Parliament itself.
* * * *
In addition to these immediate advantages,
there are other things to be considered. The
people of New South Wales have contrived,
by some process of perverted ingenuity, to
build probably the most crooked and dis-
reputable city of its age that can be found
upon this earth. There is hardly one street
of decent width in Sydney. There is not
one street, so far as can be gathered by an
inspection of the city map, which takes a
reasonably straight line to anywhere in par-
ticular. The shortest way from anywhere
to anywhere else is round 47 corners
and via several dozen unexpected crooks
and angles. Even in the best parts of
the city the highways wander round and
round each other in an irritating tangle.
The principal thoroughfare is so narrow that
the proposal to construct tramways in it
meets with powerful resistance on the ground
that there is no room. Among the parallel
thoroughfares, Castlereagh-street, Elizabeth-
street, College-street, Sussex-street, Clar-
ence-street, and York-street are of practic-
ally no use to relieve the pressure of traffic,
because they all come to an abrupt end at
nowhere in particular. There is not one
street which follows a straight line—or any-
thing even vaguely approaching a straight
line—east and west to give access from the
crowded wharves of Darling Harbor to the
eastern suburbs. Sydney was mostly laid
out by the primeval bullock-driver. He
made his own highways as he went, and
where he took a detour to avoid a stump 100
years ago the road was laid down, and the
principal streets of the city have been
dodging that same stump ever since. Nothing
but destruction on a large scale and exten-
sive rebuilding will ever make Sydney any-
thing better than a blot on the landscape
and a disgrace to one of the finest sites upon
this continent.
» * » *
Fifty years ago the evil which the old,
besotted pioneers had done began to be re-
cognised, but it was decided that the matter
was then beyond remedy, and things were
allowed to take their course and keep on
growing worse indefinitely. Now it is" also
recognised, but the general feeling is that
any serious attempt at amelioration should
have been made 50 years ago, before the city
attained its present dimensions, and that it
is too late to make any improvement worth
mentioning. If the present state of things
is allowed to remain, in another 50 years
Sydney and its suburbs may have 800,000, or
anything up to li million inhabitants instead
of 400,000, and the bewildering tangle will
then have spread many miles further, and
the general consensus of opinion will be that
the matter should have been seriously dealt
with in 1896, when the County Council idea
was first mooted, and that in 1946 it is
too vast and too incurable to be mended.
There is no local authority which has
any power to order the abolition of
the reeking slums, or to open up the
narrow, unwholesome alleys to the sun-
light, or to prevent the construction of
new slums, or to do anything in particular
—there was one, but it dropped to pieces a
few years ago, and even at the best its
power was too limited to count for much.
And it is in this matter that the County
Council should be of most advantage. An
authority which covered the whole metro-
politan district could compel new streets
that are opened to bear some relation to the
old ones already existing, whereas at present
the municipality which makes a new street
is under no obligation to keep it in line with
the one already existing in the adjacent
municipality, but generally goes off in pure
cussedness at an angle of its own. It could
enact that all new streets should be of liberal
breadth; and it could enforce a cast-iron
building law which would limit the height of
the towering Yankee insurance-office, and
prevent the further multiplication of wooden
and galvanised-iron and kerosene-tin struc-
tures in the suburbs. And it could make
some uniformity about the paving and light-
ing arrangements, and otherwise prevent the
great casual metropolis becoming any more
crooked and misshapen and casual than it
already is.
* * * *
In addition to this, it could seriously
undertake the work of straightening up the
worst features of the disreputable city. The
undertaking is certainly not one to be done
in haste—it will probably require to be
spread over at least a century, but the fact
that it is long and arduous is no reason why
it should not be commenced at once and
carried on steadily thereafter. The County
Council, having control of the whole metro-
politan district, could make a comprehensive
plan for the improvement of Sydney, where-
as the existing councils cannot, because in
whatever direction any one of them moved it
would presently be brought up short by the
boundaries of one or other of the remaining
39 local authorities. By economies of ad-
ministration and consolidation of the muni-
cipal debt the proposed council should be
able, without any increase of taxation, to
command a revenue of £50,000 a year for
improvement purposes, and it could by a
very small increase of taxation, raise this to
£lOO,OOO. Also, it could borrow at a very
low rate, and its works of improvement, in
many cases, could be made almost self-sup-
porting. If it gradually resumed the whole
peninsula of ancient slums about Dawes’ and
Miller’s Points, and rebuilt the district with
wide, straight streets and modern habitations,
the increased rental values thus created would
probably supply a sinking-fund that would
recoup the entire outlay in a comparatively
short time. The extension of Elizabeth
and Castlereagh streets to Circular Quay,
the extension of York and Clarence
streets, the opening up of streets ex-
tending directly from Darling Harbor
to the Domain, and the straightening
of the northern end of George-street, where
the Chinese fan-tan emporiums are—all these
are works in which the additional values
created would go a long way to cover the
expense. The creation of a few wide,
straight thoroughfares through the tangled
wilderness of the western and eastern
suburbs would also be possible for an enter-
prising and well-managed central authority ;
and even the widening of George-street,
though it would necessarily be a work of
stupendous cost, is not impossible. A muni-
cipal body deriving its revenues from a popu-
lation of over4oo,ooo people, which population
may increase four-fold before the last of the
people now living have passed away, could do
wonders with the City of Dreadful Angles if
it went at the work patiently and economi-
cally, and kept continually doing something
without trying to do too much at once.
* * * *
The reconstruction of Sydney would cer-
tainly be a work of time, but as the County
Council would have all the time there is to
do it in that objection is not worth consider-
ing. If it can be shown that the work could
be done in anything short of eternity, then
the possibility of it is sufficiently demon-
strated. Unfortunately, however, the move-
ment for municipal union—if it is worth call-
ing a movement —is being run so far on small
parochial lines, and the small municipality
which only owes £ll6 3s. 4d. is already
rising to ask how much it would lose by com-
bining on even terms with the one which
owes £12,637 19s. 7d. The most important
aspect of the case—that municipal union
would open up a possibility of reconstructing
the miserable, crooked, unkempt metropolis,
and converting it by degrees into something
worth calling a capital has hardly even
begun to be heard of. Yet that, in The
Bulletin’s opinion, is the great reason why
the County Council should be created, and,
if it isn’t to be created with that end in view,
its existence really doesn’t matter very much.
“The King 1 of Lecturers.”
Dr. Brigham Brown sends this to The Bul-
letin on “Rev. Charles Clark and the
Critics ” :
Ed. Bulletin, —I am one of a numerous band of Aus-
tralians proud of our Bulletin— exulting in its merits
hating it for its holes, cracking it up all the time as the
best approximation to an articulate national voice we
have ever had to shout for us. So your puff of a
lecture-agent (I forget his name, but it doesn’t matter)
galls me. This particular agent, when I saw him at Rev.
Clark’s door the other evening, looked to be a kind of
animated Butting Billy, with a d d calculating eye.
Very useful sort of person, doubtless ; but why allow him
to publicly peacock that enormous tale of Advt., as if
the clever men whose admission-tickets he has collected
were ciphers sans his parasitic I ?
* * » *
The par. goes on to dub Rev. Clark “ first and best of
the lecturers.” Now, misled by press notices, I wasted
an evening on Clark not long ago. (Subject: Dickens-
one of my earliest and dearest friends.) I heard him to
the bitter end : couldn’t get away ; for I was hemmed in
by a protuberant fat woman whom the lecturer amused
so vastly that I sat in constant dread of annihilation—
every moment expecting that she would burst her corsets
and slop over and overwhelm me. And—having escaped
by the mercy of the fat woman’s Providence—l walked
home asking myself is this the Rev. Charles Clark ? is
this “the king of lecturers”? is this the honored of
critics ?—this affable elderly gent, in dress-suit and deco-
rative watch-chain ; this amateur actor with one voice
one set of mannerisms, one ditto attitudes, one cheerfully
inflexible countenance, striving to pourtray a dozen char-
acters in monologue ; this repertory of flat observations
and stale allusions ; this mnemonic machine ; this Bore ?
Did I sleep ? did I dream ? or had fear of the fat woman
paralysed my intelligence ?
* * * *
I didn’t know ; so I wasted another evening. Again
Dickens ; but I dodged the fat woman. And the esti-
mate ? the result ? Precisely the same. I eyed the lec-
turer narrowly ; heard his every syllable ; weighed his
lightest comment; and came away convinced that Rev.
Clark is a Presence, a Voice, a Memory, and not an atom
more. Of course, he knows his authors and their com-
mentators—that is a matter of so many days’ reading at
so many hours a day. But he never, in my hearing
rose once from the respectable rut of tradition ; he never
made one original remark, never illuminated his subject
by one gleam of thought or insight : his enthusiasm was
tame, his criticism unenlightened, his platitudes Awful
A priori, to be sure, one might have guessed as much
Would people crowd so to hear a really brainv lecturer?
Did they so crowd to hear Proctor— successful though
Proctor admittedly was ?
* * * *
But pass brains : admit the monstrous proposition that
“ the king of lecturers ” should be a mere phonograph •
and Clark fails again. For he isn’t a phonograph, and
he isn’t an actor : lie is an intruding Presence who comes
between you and the author, between you and the charac-
ters, and complicates the Idea of Dr. Marigold and the
Idea of Bob Cratciiit with the Idea of a stout and rather
festive old gentleman with plenty of watch-chain. Now,
a lecturer—not a critic, that is, or a thinker, but aglorified
penny-reader of the Clark type—should be either Water
or Wine. He should either seem an absolutely colorless
medium, through whom an author’s dramatis personae are
made visible to your mind without the least distortion or
refraction ; or he should lose himself altogether, and
actually be th e personae to the last tittle, so that for the
time the illusion is perfect, and the talent of the lecture!
completes the author’s expression, transfigures his pic-
ture, and brings the audience, as it were, into the arcana
of his mind to see his creations as himself sees them—
warm with color, palpitating with Life.
* * * *
This, of course, is asking that a lecturer shall win the
actor’s effects without the actor’s mask and buskin—
seemingly an impossible feat. Yet I have known several
raconteurs and reciters who could represent two or three
characters to the life —merely by voice, and gesture, and
facial expression—so that you wholly forgot the medium ;
and I have known one mimic who could represent seem-
ingly an endless succession of characters with a similar
result. But Clark halts half-way ; he destroys the illu-
sion of Dickens’ words without substituting an illusion
from his own personality. His attempts to imitate Sophy’s
voice, or to screw his jolly red face into the similitude of
old Scrooge, are merely grotesque. Yet he is “ the king
of lecturers ! ” Bah !
* » * *
When you read Dickens— so strenuous is his vitality, so
nn e his faculty of picturing things with words—you can
often get into absolute mental contact with him : the
printed page, the vehicle of transmission, releases its
images at a glance : the epithets sing and flash : the
sweet alive sentences come trooping’ past, cinctured with
sound, now a gay figure, now a grave—here a tumbled
Bacchante, the imprint of a wine-kiss on her mouth;
there a Seer august and slow ; yonder a merry Child, yet
with eyes troubled by the primal melancholy of Being.
And from each to each is tossed the leaping Thought,
pregnant with sad Joy or smiling Sorrow, till you too,
forgetting Time and Space and the barriers of Souls,
become one with the thronging multitude, are swept
hither and thither by the breath of the Writer’s
genius, and merge every rill of self in the Ocean of his
magnificent ecstasy. But Clark !— Clark takes the
Bacchante’s arm, and straightway she is a draggled Mag-
dalen to be prayed over at a Night Refuge ; lie kneels
before the Seer, who trips and falls headlong ; he speaks
to the Child, and all its grace of childhood goes. And the
tender Thought, escaping from the desolation of this
change, falls into a thicket of platitudes and is mired out
of knowledge or vanished utterly. Yet Australian criti-
cism has never whispered this. Wherefore I rage.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Social Purity Item.
One “ Richard Arthur ” —understood to be a
physician—tackles “ the social evil ” from a new
standpoint in Sydney Telegraph :
Sir,—The statement made by me at the Centenary Hall
yesterday afternoon that there were probably 10,000 girls
and women seduced in this colony every year may be
thought a gross exaggeration, and, therefore, I would ask
space in your columns to explain why I am afraid it is not.
This estimation is based on a calculation of probabilities,
for, of course, it is impossible to obtain accurate know-
ledge on the matter. The figures for the starting point of
the calculation are found in the number of illegitimate
births. These amounted in 1894 to 2437. Now, it might
be hastily concluded that this also represents the number
of cases of seduction ; but a little consideration will show
the error of this. First of all, a certain number of births
are not registered. This number may not be very large ;
but it comprises all the infants who are done away with
immediately after birth, and the greater proportion of
those who are handed over to another person for a similar
purpose. As stillborn children are not registered, they
also go to swell the total. But a much more important
consideration is the number of children who are destroyed
before birth. Here again it is impossible to arrive at an
accurate estimate, but the number may be set down as
very large.
I feel certain that if these various causes which lead to
non-registration were not at work, the illegitimate births
every year would amount to over 3000.
The next step is the most important, and perhaps the
most difficult of all. It is this —that only a small propor-
tion of those seduced become mothers. It is impossible
here to go into the reasons, medical and otherwise, which
lead to this conclusion. lam confident, however, that it
is a low estimate which states that only a third of those
who run the risk of maternity actually incur it. This at
once brings the numbers to about 10,000.
As the great aggregate dirty saint will pre-
sently rise up with the Social Gimlet in his
hand and begin to howl over this theory, and to
talk more than ever about the age of consent,
The Bulletin respectfully asks Mr. Richard
Arthur to consider his own figures a little and
see where they get to. In the first place, the
New South Wales illegitimate births for ’94
are returned at 2393 instead of 2437—n0t that
the difference matters much. Then Mr. Arthur
reckons only one illegitimate child to each
seduced female, and he also reckons seduced
females to each illegitimate child. Therefore,
when that frequent individual John Smith
states in the court, as he often does, that he is
living with Sarah Jones, to whom lie isn’t
married, and they have six children, Sarah
Jones thus becomes 20 seduced females in one
act. If they have three children then she is 10
seduced females; if they have eight it follows that
she is 26 and a fraction fallen women all at once.
On a high estimate 10,600feinales attainthesedu-
cible age in N.S. W. every year—taking that
age as an average anywhere between 15
and 25 and the statement that 10,000 of
them are seduced leaves only 5 per cent, of
moral women in the province. Any saint who
says that he believes this, and yet ventures to
marry in N.S.W., is evidently a person who
doesn’t care much what sort of person he
marries. Further, illegitimacy applies almost
wholly to unmarried women ; the married
woman’s child may not be legitimate, but the
fact seldom comes to light. Allowing for this,
if 10,000 girls and women are seduced in
N.S. W. every year it really would seem that
more girls are seduced than are available. At
all events, 40 years of this sort of thing would
lead to the seduction of 400,000 females, which
is a truly dreadful thing in a province where
there are only 360,000 females over the age of
10 years. And considering that there are
only about 10,600 girls, and only 10,200 un-
married girls, every year attaining the
age (17) when seduction becomes common,
and 10,000 of them are seduced, it seems a
pity that Richard Arthur, Esq., didn’t con-
trive, if only for the sake of symmetry, to write
the whole lot down as seduced. The balance
seems hardly worth preserving when the case
might have been made so much stronger by
running them all in together.
“The Burns Myth.”
“ Tom Collins ” writes to The Bulletin :
This hypercritical disparagement of Robert Burns— a
healthy reaction in the first place, but now tiresomely
conventional—has far overstepped justice. It is scarcely
reasonable to compare the motley word-garb of his pro-
vincial lays with a century-end standard, which the nobly
rebellious spirit of that poetry has done much to make
popularly attainable. If his verse is often slovenly his
thought is not so. To his present critics, “ A man’s a
man tor a that ’ may appear as a platitude clothed in
doggerel ; but the song, when first written, embodied the
greatest discovery of modern times. The magnitude of
that discovery may best be apprehended by considering


what a terrible thing it was to be brought up under
Cotter’s Saturday-Night conditions —brought up in the
literal and hair-raising fear of an anthropomorphic
Phantasm such as Paganism has seldom evolved ; a Ruler
whose arrangements for the eternal future were aptly
prefigured in the present life by that hopelessly - in-
scrutable dispensation which had told off a few actors in
the world-drama to extract the third nettle and call it
rent, and a few million others to work in sleet and muck
and humility for 16 hours per diem on the rascalliest
variety of brose, with the option of being transported
with thankfulness, or of sulking in the collar. It
was something for Burns, of his own initiation, to
detect, impeach, and laugh to scorn this devil-worship
which, as the mawkish saying g'oes, he had learned at his
mother’s knee. Booth’s cab-horse standard stands out in
sinfully luxurious contrast with the lot of the Lowland
agriculturist in an era which, according to Thorold
Rooers, witnessed the nadir of the peasant’s condition ;
and, of those squalid millions, Burns alone gave articulate
challenge to the system he had been taught to revere.
Across the border, his contemporaries, Oowrer and
Crabbe, both exceptionally humane men, and both writing
copiously of the poor, have no protest against the un-
natural social-economics of the day. It is the Lord’s
doing, and it is marvellous in their eyes —that’s all.
Burns’ sister (Isabel, was it not?), who lived far into the
present century, could not read or write; and this —not-
withstanding the equivocation of glozing biographers of
the Morris type—because the right-thinking guid-man,
in a spirit of child-sacrifice reminiscent of BAAL-worship,
had seen to it that his plebeian daughter should have no
notions above her station. Think, now, through what
thickets of moral obstruction, and through what sulphur-
fumes from the Gehenna of his fathers this warm-blooded
prophet burst, seeking the light of day ! If he sometimes
felt, and oftener affected, loyalty to an order of things at
which his higher thought revolted, it was because no
man can be always at his best. If he lunched with Sar-
good whenever lie got the chance, it was because in his
day there was no democratic etiquette—no democracy,
indeed—and the resilient manhood which equally resented
insolence and condescension was well balanced by a frank
camaraderie toward man as man. If his light no longer
dazzles, it is because the torch taken from his dead hand
has found worthy bearers. But whilst he lived, the fitful
inspiration of the fearless truth-seeker was upon him.
Appealing to the emotions, as his contemporary, Paine,
appealed to the reason, he stands in history as one of
society’s redeemers. And if he did flirt with two vocabu-
laries, and manufactured rhymes ad arbitrium, the
licentia vatum, though admittedly malum in se, was. in
reality no more en dehors de saison than our own weaving
a soupgon of Latin or French into our text, as per pi esent
example. Scribendi reete sapere est et principium et funs,
as we say when we try to show off.
The “ Humorous Side of the Business.”
The visit to Australia of certain agents of a
great English co-operative society has moved
the Australasian to let off a few remarks
about the general eussedness of Protection :
But there is a humorous side to the business which will
probably strike the delegates from Manchester, whether
they speak of it or not. Men are found admitting that it
is a fine thing for us to be able to dump as much produce
as we like on the shores of Great Britain, and at the same
time denying it to be a good thing that we should take
her commodities in an equal spirit of free interchange.
The fair play which is said to be bonny play is absent
here. But our protectionist absurdities hurt ourselves
much more than they can hurt the mother country. Every
business man must know that our exports can be paid for
only in imports. If we will persist in taxing our imports
heavily through the Customs we receive less for our ex-
ports than we should otherwise obtain ; the loss falls on
ourselves, and especially on the producer, who must be
able to live cheaply if he is to compete successfully in the
markets of the world. If we send produce to Man-
chester, can it be a good thing wilfully to diminish the
value which Manchester sends us in return ?
This sort of thing, which the Argus and Aus-
tralasian are constantly getting off, is good
enough in a large, loose sort of way, but how
does it look when we get down to particulars?
Britain levies an enormous tax on Victorian
wine, brandy, raisins, and other fruits, and
calls it Freetrade; why should not Victoria
levy duties on British produce in return and
call it Protection or anything else she pleases ?
Britain raises almost as large a proportion of
her revenue from Customs duties as Victoria
does, though the duties are differently distri-
buted ; why then should she be held up as a
bright and shining example ? Also, where does
the want of fair play come in, and what is all
the row about? Also, how, or by what possi-
bility does “every business man know that
our exports can be paid for only in imports,”
when in Victoria alone about £2,000,000 worth
of exports per annum are paid for simply by a
receipt for the interest on the public debt ?
What kind of a business man is it who doesn’t
know this ? Moreover, Victoria does take
British goods quite as freely as Britain takes
Victorian goods, provided Britain sends the
kind Victoria most wants —that is, specie.
Britain also only takes the kind of goods she
wants, so the two things are equal. Further,
leaving specie out of the question and dealing
only with other goods, Victoria takes very
nearly as much from Britain as Britain takes
from her, so again, what is the row all about ?
And if “ the loss falls on ourselves,” what has
Britain got to grumble at ? Again, if exports
are paid for only by imports, then supposing Vic-
toria sends £IOOO worth of goods to England it
naturally follows that England sends £IOOO
worth back, whether the country they are sent
to is Freetrade or Protectionist. So unless the
Australasian really means that the English
exporter looks up the tariff, and —when he finds
that Victoria is a Protectionist country—posi-
tively refuses to send more than £BSO worth of
goods or specie back, and that Victoria stands
this kind of thing, the statement that a Protec-
tionist country receives less in exchange than a
Foreign-trade one is sheer imbecility. And, in
any case, if there is to be “fair play” and
“ free interchange ” and sich, shouldn’t Britain,
which advocates free interchange, set the ex-
imple—not Victoria, which doesn’t advocate it ?
The Desolation of Sydney.
In view of Premier Reid’s cheerful assurances
of a very few weeks ago as to the diminution of
poverty in New South Wales, and the general
improvement in prospects, and the revival of
trade, and his assertion that the unemployed
now amount to less than 1500 all told—in view
of all these things the special report presented
the other day by the manager of the Benevolent
Asylum is doleful reading. Its general tenor is
that, in all the many years during which the
institution has existed, things have never been
so bad or so hopeless as during the last six
months —when the policy that was to make
New South Wales “ jolly and fat ” began to
get really into operation. At the end of the
half-year the institution had on its books some-
thing like 1880 persons (845 registered as “ un-
employed,” and 1035 miscellaneous destitutes)
and reckoning the families of these people the
total number receiving outdoor relief was 7330.
This takes no account of indoor relief, and it also
takes no account of the fact that there are over 30
other benevolent institutions in the county of
Cumberland alone, so that these figures repre-
sent merely a fraction of the aggregate misery
of the metropolitan district. The report fur-
ther adds that “there is every prospect of a
further increase ” in the number of the Asylum s
dependents. Since this was written the fore-
cast has been fully verified ; for, whereas the
half-yearly report sets forth that things had
never before been so bad in Sydney since the
institution existed, the monthly report for July
states that they have since become a great deal
worse, and that the persons receiving outdoor
relief and their families have suddenly increased
to about 9400. In the face of these statistics
it is about time to throw up the ghastly pretence
that things are getting better, or even that they
are not getting a good deal worse, in the Golgotha
by the Beautiful Harbor. Last year the popula-
tion of Sydney anditssuburbs decreased by 15,100
people —the first decrease recorded for more
years than this generation can remember. The
value of city and suburban property, as shown
by the municipal valuations, went down with a
thud such as even Melbourne in its burst days
hardly excelled. The aggregate amount of em-
ployment decreased woefully; all the faked
statistics of the rotund Premier can’t dissipate
the evidence on this head contained in the
Benevolent Asylum reports. The record suicide
year, so far back as Cogiilan’s statistics go,
was 1895 ; not only was it the year when the
actual number of suicides was largest, but it
was also the one when a larger proportion of
the population suicided than ever before —in
other words, New South Wales was evi-
dently a better place to get away from in
’95 than at any other period of its recent
history. And 1896, so far as available
evidence goes, is even worse than ’95. There
has been a liberal shutting-up of factories
through the beneficent operation of the Foreign-
trade system. Pauperism, as shown by the
Benevolent Society’s figures, has largely in-
creased. The sugar-industry has decided to
gradually peg out. The State revenue for the
first month of the new financial year reveals a
large decrease. The trade reports in the Sydney
morning papers tell the same invariable story
that “ business shows no marked improve-
ment,” and have been telling it for many
months past. About one-third of Sydney is
for sale at greatly reduced prices, and more of
it is to let, probably, than ever before. Alto-
gether, if there is any bright spot in the imme-
diate future of New South Wales it is nowhere
apparent, and even the most flippant politician
seldom looked out over his vast stomach at a
more dreary prospect.
The Coolgardie Water Problem.
Apparently the Westralian Government must
tackle the question of a water-supply for Cool-
gardie and its surroundings, because Coolgardie
is the centre of the great boom which keeps
Westralia alive ; yet the scheme bids fair to be
one of the worst white elephants on this con-
tinent. It will cost about £2,500,000; and the
annual outlay for interest, maintenance, &c., is
estimated at £320,000. To earn this the
Government expects to sell five million gallons
a day at 3s. 9d. per 1000 gallons, equal to
£329,000 a year. But there are awful diffi-
culties in the way. It is estimated that two
million gallons a day will be bought for domestic
purposes, which will require a population of
nearly 100,000 ; and that three million gallons
will be taken by the mines, which estimate is
based on the assumption that 300 batteries of
20-heads each will be going night and day.
But, at present, there is no sign of more than
one-tenth of that battery-power being wanted,
and if the batteries don’t come the 100,000
population can’t live there, and, between these
two circumstances, it is just possible that the
revenue will be nearer £30,000 a year than
£329,000. If this should come to pass, and it is
quite possible, then the shortage will swallow
up one-sixth of the Westralian revenue in one
act. Moreover, it is quite possible that the
abundance of water will destroy the roaring gold-
boom for good and all—most of the mines live on
the fact that they can’t crush and thereby prove
their utter barrenness, for want of water ; and
as the pound water-supply comes in
the people who are to pay for it may simply
rise and go out. Apparently they can’t live
decently without it, and very likely it will
make it practically impossible for them to live
at all. Moreover, if the requisite 100,000
people are forthcoming to use the water they
will be much more than half the population of
the province, and they must get adequate repre-
sentation some day, and when they do they
will almost certainly insurrect against the tre-
mendous charge of 3s. 9d. per. 1000 gallons, and
insist on charging a large part of the expense to
the general revenue. Therefore, if Coolgardie
and Kalgoorlie don’t succeed the old identities
will have to pay the bill for water-supply to the
hated t’othersiders. And if they do succeed,
the old identities will probably have to pay a
large part of the bill. Perhaps the final use of
the great water-supply will be to irrigate the
plains so that Chinamen may grow onions there,
to be humped by an insolvent railway to Perth
and thence exported to Sydney.
A Northern correspondent to The Bulle-
tin :
The town of Bundaberg (Q.) is the scene of many
, strange sights. It may be described geographically as
bounded on the north, east, west, and south by r.he genial
Kanaka. At every roadside corner the black brother is
in force—especially on Sundays—waiting with his bow
and arrows, ready for fight. The Kanaka is the pride and
victim of the sky-pilot of every denomination, barring the
Papal one. lie generally finds at the end of the year that
he has left to him more religion than sense and less money
than either. If by chance he gets free from the heavenly
harpies, he has to face the harpies in the town. These
volunteer to keep his cash in safe custody profitable
business. The mortality amongst Kanakas in the Bunda-
berg district is so high, and the “morality” is so
low, that it is a safe thing to borrow any
nigger’s money at ten per cent, (promised). Nine
out of ten take out principal and interest in grog,
women, or other accommodation, or die before they de-
mand it. In one recent month two Bundaberg store-
keepers were committed for trial for alleged larceny
as bailees of kanakas’ money. The niggers, of course,
have open to them the Govt. Savings Bank ; but, like
their white fellow-residents, they seem to dislike trusting
Q. Govt. They seem to think Govt., Polynesian Dept.,
Savings Bank, and missionary stations are all run on the
same lines as parts of the same comprehensive swindle.
The other day, at the Polynesian Office at Bundaberg-,
about 1)0 kanakas presented themselves to be paid. One
“ boy ” “ no-saveed ” his name. lie was stupid in his
speech and appearance. A brother kanaka explained that
there was a man on the .verandah of the office outside
vending liquor to the niggers departing with their money
The middle distance of the picture was made up of the
vile white women plentiful in Bundaberg, pleading for
their share of the golden harvest reaped by the Polynesian
saviours of Queensland. In the background hovered a
black-coat. Doubtless the kanakas found all appropriate.
To them, Rum, Harlots, and Religion are Queensland s
blessed trinity, with a “good-fella Guv’ment” paternally
smiling over all.
In the latest Alert case of Ponting v. Huddart
PARKEKandCo. threeof the jurymen were, admit-
tedly, seen drinking with two of the defendants’
lawyers at Menzies’ Hotel. Doubtless there
was nothing deplorable in this incident, but it
reminds The Bulletin of another Melbourne
law-case —a famous will-suit —tried by a special
jury, whose foreman invited one of the counsel
engaged to lunch with him at the same Menzies’
Hotel whilst the struggle wa,s proceeding.
Also, in that instance the jury gave a verdict
for the friendly counsel’s client—such a “bad”
verdict that the winner made a modest com-
promise with the loser to avoid a new trial.
Melbourne barristers and solicitors —especially
the latter—show an undue affection for jurymen,
anyway. In the course of Speight v. Syme a
juryman died. His last illness was soothed by
much chicken and jelly and things from the
hands of at least one legal gentleman engaged
upon the joh. The dainties used to be brought
every morning, with best wishes for a speedy
recovery of the patient and an honest verdict to
follow. And when at last the juryman went
where there is no more jelly both of the rival
legal parties mourned for him as a brother.
One of the saddest things The Bulletin has
seen in print for a long while is this item from
Sydney Evening News :
There is good news from various sources pointing to
the probability of considerable industrial revival in the
near future. For one thing it is announced that Mr.
Joseph Mitchell has formed an English syndicate for the
establishment of ironworks in New South Wales, and, for
another, the British co-operative delegates just arrived
here have been talking in a way that seems to foreshadow
increase of business.
The British co-operative delegates are looking
for supplies of all manner of food products to
sell to their customers. And N.S. W., which
imports great quantities of cheese, flour,
eggs, fish, fruit, barley, maize, oats, wheat,
hops, jams, jellies, cattle, malt, bacon, hams,
milk, molasses, potatoes, onions, salt, straw,
timber, tobacco, and even vegetables, because
it somehow can’t, under the Freetrade system,
grow its own supplies, reckons that their visit
will somehow open up a profitable business in
something—no one knows what. And the
other cause of joy is that an English co. is
thinking of starting iron works in N.S. W., and
the cornstalk will probably be allowed to hump
the heavy loads at a low wage, and be a beast
of burden in his own country for an English
master. And he is down so low that he calls
this “good news.”
The Parliamentary pressure exerted upon
N.S.W. Government to extend the period
allowed for the re-appraisement of “special
areas,” chiefly on the plea of ignorance of the
law, and to abolish the 10 per cent, fine upon
such holders of such areas as are behind with
their payments, has to collide with the legal
axiom that every citizen is supposed to know
the law, every law, every bit of every law, and
that ignorance is no tenable excuse. Besides,
as “ Lands ” Carrutheks has pointed out, there
has already resulted, by re-valuation of lands, a
loss of £900,000 to the N.S. W. revenue. The
clamor of the agitators is tolerably comprehen-
sive : (1) District surveyors, who make valua-
tions, are not experts in valuing; (2) land
boards decline to receive expert evidence as to
values ; (3) the expert evidence that is received
is unreliable. Yet it only means, on behalf of
the proposed beneficiaries : Holders don’t want
to pay anything more than they can help, and
don’t want to pay that much until they are
forced to do so. But they hold on to the land
and din noises into the ears of local members.

Writes “ Treble B. ” :
Dear Bulletin, —In his note on the N.S.W. land ad-
ministration “ Stump-Jumper” touches the spot, and his
conclusion, “the land administration is rotten,” will
receive an emphatic endorsement from thousands who
are galled by the lack of ordinary business courtesy and
the bureaucratic indifference to the public convenience,
characteristic of that administration. Little is heard
publicly of the matter, for the simple reason that it
emphatically doesn’t pay the man settled on the land to
incur the ill-will or animus of any part of the administra-
tion, and grievances can only be ventilated anony-
mously. “ Stump-Jumper ” is of opinion that things
will be righted when we get practical men at the
head of the departments. Rather an optimistic opinion,
I fear. A strong man might do somewhat, but a man
strong enough to tone down the arrogance and remove
the inertia of the Lands Department might be Premier.
In the conduct of public business the Post Office Depart-
ment and the Lands Department may stand as examples
of how to do it and how not to do it, and this notwith-
standing that the P.M.G. is always the lame duck of the
Ministry, whilst the Minister for Lands is just as invari-
ably the second in charge. This would go to show that
the difference in the two departments arises from some
factor within the departments, and is not the effect of, or
affected by, Ministerial control. Which factor may be
that the P.O. is administered by the common people for
the common people, and that the Lands Department is
administered by a professional class with the usual regard
to their own convenience and arrogance to outsiders so
often characteristic of members of the professional
classes when beyond the control of direct public competi-
tion. Be this as it may, the Minister for Lands who can
convert his Department to “civility, punctuality, and
despatch ” will confer a lasting boon on the State tenants.
In the meantime the administration is rotten.
Practically, the N.S. W. law of divorce recog-
nises that a marriage under false pretences
comes within the recognised statutory ground
of “desertion.” The other day, Cohen, J.,
granted a decree nisi, returnable in two months,
to a wife who supported her allegation of
“ desertion ” (according to a daily paper’s re-
port) by stating that her husband, before the
marriage, had styled himself “a general ser-
vant ” ; but he did not seem to be able to do any-
thing, and never worked at all. She supported
him by going out as a general servant, and he
never found her a home.” If this constitutes
“ desertion,” a wide field is open to divorce
lawyers. Male Impecuniosity frequently
marries Foolish Female Competency, and never
works for her, nor provides her with a. home.
In fact, Female Hunger buys its Male Food at
a price. If the law for the rich be theoretically
the law for the poor, then must also the Coiien-
ese law for the poor, as m the case illustrated
above, be the Cohenesk law for the rich. Here
is a large hitherto unexploiled field. Only the
other day, when a domestic fracas was ventilated
in a Sydney suburban police-court, the male
defendant admitted airily: “I married her
because she settled ,£2OO a year on me.” This
case is not on all-fours with that under con-
sideration, but the possibilities of married con-
ditions coming easily within this latest inter-
pretation of divorce-law should be interesting
to a lively legal imagination.
Graham was asking in Yic. Assembly the other
night whether the acting railway commissioners
had employed stevedores on the Murray and
elsewhere; whether shippers were charged
with the cost; whether a certain carrying firm
(named) was favored re said charges ; whether
what they paid, if they paid anything, was
refunded ; whether favored individuals as far
as wool-freights are concerned exist in the
neighborhood of Linton ; what the reasons are
for the seeming favoritism, &e. All which, if
soundly based, points to the danger of our
importing the baneful American system of
preferential freights, which are so effectually
crushing out the American farmer and com-
pelling him to fly to Populism to get some
alleviation. Possibly a dividend-mill might
justify itself ; but can a popular Government?
Victorian Public Service needs overhauling
and re-classifying—just as does every big ser-
vice every few years. Promotion by seniority
means that many men get billets they can’t
manage, and a re-arrangement of duties follows
as the night the day. Further, the tide of
public business changes and leaves men stranded
without work, or overflows them. The boom
meant expansion of the Titles office work—
complete alienation of the land means an idle
Lands Department—&c., &c. Even M’sP.
know this. But does the Vic. Public Service
Board commence attacks on service anomalies—
taking stock of wasted energies? No. It
issues an order that lunch-time must be strictly
limited to half-an-hour, institutes time-books
for the lunch-time, regulates that the officer
who carries a sandwich in his pocket must note
the time lie begins eating and the time he stops ;
that another officer must certify the fact, and
that the time-book shall be sent for inspection !
The result is a flutter for a fortnight, and one
civil servant is threatened with the sack. O
monstrous ! but one half-pennyworth of sack to
this intolerable deal of lunch-time.
In its broadest sense “ local option ” is soundly
democratic. It is, in fact, a mere extension of
the principle of “ local government,” a prin-
ciple that has scarcely an audible opponent. But
teetotallers run their “ local option ” movement
in the narrowest instead of the broadest sense.
Their case is ex-parte Cold Water. They only
seek local option votes as to whether public-
house licenses shall be numerically decreased or
standstilled. Bung, in S.A. and N.S.W. is
now moving in a counter-direction. Bung is
agitating for a “ local-option ”,vote upon “ Sun-
day-opening,” as against the adopted (but con-
temptuously-fractured) law of the land. Bung’s
position is at least as logical as Pop’s. A free
people should have a right to say what it may
or may not drink at any time. The case, how-
ever, is not merely one of Bung v. Pop. It is
the People v. Bung and Pop. And the local-
option vote should be possible, in certain locali-
ties, concerning the question of Night Licences.
Ex-Governor Norman’s Queensland dinner
speech in London, regretting that Federation
will dwarf the Australian provinces into lieu-
tenancies, contains that kernel of unpalatable
fact which makes numbers of provincially-pro-
minent people oppose or throw cold water upon
every practical attempt to constitute an Aus-
tralian nation. - First, as General Norman
says, away will go the Governors. Next, away
will go all but one of the Chief Justices. Then
the Premiers will dwindle to something the size
of mayors, and away will go half the Parlia-
ments, their officers, and belongings. In fact,
considering the shrinkage of dignity and emolu-
ments which must necessarily take place in
each province on the coming of Federation, the
wonder is that the self-protective instinct of
mediocrity does not inspire a fiercer hostility.
And perhaps it would, were not mediocrity in
general so fatuously vain as to think that it
will be as big a man in Australia as it is in
Hog’s Gully.
There are some suggestive points in the follow-
ing, addressed to The Bulletin by a legal
correspondent :
When a prisoner has been convicted after trial, the
record of his previous convictions, if any, is duly read
out, ostensibly for the purpose of guiding the sitting
Rhadanianthus in passing sentence. Wilson or Butler
had just pleaded “ not guilty ” to a charge of highway
robbery under arms, when an arraignment for simple
larceny was straightway made against him, to which he
pleaded “guilty.” Thereupon the Crown got its little
chance, and chucklingly proved a record of convictions in
Victoria and elsewhere involving 63 years of sentences —
some, of course, concurrent. Jurymen in court listened
to this damnable iteration, and must thereby have been
more or less prejudiced against the prisoner when im-
mediately afterwards he was put on trial for the graver
offence with the pistol. The record of convictions
should in all fairness have been withheld pending the
hearing of the more serious charge. Even a ruffianly
criminal like Butler should have fair play when the stake
is his life. The shadowy case against him (it now
appears that he was guilty all the same) was quietly
brushed aside by the prisoner, who ably defended himself.
Nor did Judge Holroyd’s impartial summing-up at all
injure the man’s common-sense defence of want of reason-
able identification, which the jury without hesitation
accepted. Here let it be said, however, that Victorian
Judges lay themselves open to an accusation of unfairness
when they look at the “ convictions attached to the pre-
sentment” while prisoner is on his trial. The jury is
not allowed to see these, but it may be pertinently asked
why the Judge should see them either. If the Judges con-
fined themselves to an exposition of the law bearing on the
case, there would be little harm in this proceeding. But,
unfortunately for themselves and for the fair spirit of an
impartial community, most of them usurp the undoubted
functions of the jury by debating the evidence step by
step in their charges. Knowing of those previous convic-
tions, prejudice infallibly steps in, and warps, insensibly
it may be, the judicial deliverance to the jury. When it
is laid down as a legal axiom that the jury should know
nothing of the antecedents of a prisoner before delivering
the verdict, in order to secure absolute justice, should not
the same abstinence be demanded of the Judge, and
especially of the Judge who comments freely upon the
facts, acting indeed as 13th juryman ?

The Australian Star put up a record
last Tuesday. On page 6 appeared a lurid shriek
concerning the death of one Joseph Ryder, with
big black headings, “ Kicked to Death,” “ Brutal
Outrage at Waterloo.” On page 5 appeared a
later version of the same story in which the Star
speaks in a tone of great pain as follows :
Systematic inquiries by the police into the circumstances
surrounding the death of the hoy Joseph Ryder at Water-
loo yesterday do not tend to support the theory that he
was brutally assaulted by larrikins. Every time a man is
found bruised and battered a certain section of the press
immediately put it down to a “ push,” although there
may not be the slightest evidence of the assault being
so caused. The facts in this case point to a fall
from a horse. Imaginative writers, flourishing in
certain newspaper circles, have been wont to create
mysteries where facts are not sufficiently blood-curd-
ling. It is now almost impossible for a man to
be found in a battered condition without the intro-
duction of this mystic element. What possible founda-
tion existed in the boy Ryder’s case to give rise to weird,
eerie shrieks of secret brotherhoods and sworn vendettas ?
It is not long since a man named Rae was found seriously
injured. A chance utterance in rambling delirium, as in
this case, furnished an inspiration, which was forthwith
clothed in denunciation of imaginary pushes and brutal
larrikins. This is the species of so-called journalism which
has been brought to bear in the Waterloo case.
This is a case of grabbing the difficulty with both
hands and making the best of it.

Athenssum Hall Ovide Musin.
The Potter-Bellew season in Sydney com-
menced with a brief attack of “ La Tosca,” which
elegant and gentlemanly horror was well supported
by the public and received with all the enthusiasm
that such a well - appointed horror deserved.
“Tosca” is magnificent tragedy and gory and awful
murder in evening dress —not twopenny butchery
done in a cellar with an axe and the corpse left on
top of the coals, but scented homicide and the
remains afterwards set to slow music. And as the
very Nicest People like their murder, provided it
is done in a high-toned fashion, “ Tosca ” should
stand a good deal of casual revival. The drama
was gorgeously staged, and its five short acts and
four rather long intervals somehow suggested the
small flower-plots and the big, glaring paths in the
far den of a newly-enriched pawnbroker. Bellew’s
laron Scarpia was received in Sydney with
general approbation. The flour-bag-colored visage
and the vividly - painted lips of that gaudy
Sicilian produced an unnatural effect, but
Bellew’s rendering of the character was
otherwise altogether commendable. He was the
subdued and cat-like functionary that the public
expected, and whether his version of Scarpia was
or was not the best one possible, at all events it
was strictly consistent, and, having started to
crawl in one direction, it kept on crawling that
way to the bitter end. In a number of small, re-
flective ways Bellew filled in the character of his
ghoulish Scarpia and made him an artistic ruffian
of the deepest dye. Scot Inglis’s Cavaradossi was
a fine, manly character, and he and Scarpia set off
each other. No one at present on the Australian
stage could improve much, if at all, on Inglis’s
rendering of the hero whom Mrs. Potter hugs so
desperately, and a smaller man would have been
cracked in that embrace.
* • * »
As regards Mrs. Potter, the general feel-
ing, so far, is one of bewilderment. When that
lady made her last appearance in Sydney, some
years ago, the impression she left behind was that
she was wildly different from anybody else, and
she is more different now than she was
then. She spoke her Tosca with a strange
and wonderful accent (except when she for-
got the accent) which was possibly meant for
broken Italian, though why the pure Italian of the
original Tosca shouldn’t simply be translated into
ordinary English passes comprehension. The
other characters were also Italian, but none of
them struggled along in the extraordinary Potter
dialect. Her attitudes were artificial and labored
and affected ; her painted visage, with a great
mass of black at.the outer corner of each eye and
a splash of red at the inner one, was an extra-
ordinary piece of Impressionist art —the lady
looked as if she had been facially decorated
out of a bucket by the eminent Mr. Sid. Long;
she rolled her eyes painfully, and made
fearful and wonderful mouths, and had strange
adventures with her hair ; and her Tosca
was an artificial creature who couldn’t have lived,
because she would have wriggled herself to death
at an early age. And with it all there is a decided
fascination about Mrs. Potter, though her amazing
hat did strike one in the eye like a colored rocket;
most of the details of her are objectionable, but
about the whole Potter there are many undefinable
attractions. If she painted less, and struggled
not quite so desperately with herself, and gave the
amazing hat to the poor, and trusted a lot more
to Providence for small details, and wasn’t so
painfully affected and so much aware of her own
existence, The Bulletin has an idea that some-
where or somehow Mrs. Brown P. might be a
really good actress, or something near one. Apart
from the people above-mentioned, Miss Polly
Emery deserves a mention as a wild-haired boy
who was done admirably so far as he went; and
that fine old veteran, J. H. Cathcart, gave a per-
fect sketch of a casual old nobleman. The noble-
man was wholly casual, but he was a notable indi-
vidual the way Gathcart did him.
** # *
Her Majesty’s resumed business with
Harry Hodson as lessee and manager, and Charles
Holloway, brother of the well-known Billolloway,
as star. So far as the first nights go, things are
looking well with the new firm, and it is just
possible that the curse which has hitherto rested
on Sydney’s biggest theatre has begun to be used
up. The “King of Crime,” which was revived
for the occasion, is a rather deserving melodrama
in which many people are cut off in their prime.
The pathetic and beautiful blind woman gradually
falls down dead when she finds that her beloved
husband, when he isn’t leading a pastoral exist-
ence in their cottage, is head of a band of thugs
of the most gory description. The miser has pre-
viously died by justifiable insecticide, and the
wrong man has been promptly and ably run in for
the crime. Then the traitor is obliterated by a
pair of large hands—the vengeful hands of C.
’Olloway—which appear at midnight from behind
a curtain ; and the avenger is himself bombarded
to death by a large quantity of troops immediately
afterwards. Also the will, after being three times
stolen and once burnt, turns up again in the pre-
sence of a gorgeous and exceedingly wicked ballet
with pink fleshings on.
* * * *
In the “King of Crime,” vice is flattened
out with a desperate and picturesque energy which
is beyond praise ; it is not only flattened, but it is
killed and utterly smashed up with a club, and
belabored heavily after it is dead. C. Holloway
himself is the most notably weak part in the
show. He is desperately earnest with a comedy
hat on and a pair of unconvincing top-boots, and
his attitudes are mostly wooden and very depress"
ing. Still he sometimes rises to a big occasion
with some semblance of success. The co.
has a clever and very pretty leading
lady in Miss Eugenie Duggan, whose rendering
of the blind woman is an excellent piece of work
throughout. There is not very much of the blind
woman, but she appears at many critical moments,
and hears the breathing of the assassin in the
dark, and she always turns up gracefully, and
does her little bit of work in a most commendable
fashion. Willard makes a striking and very tall
villain, who sins at a great altitude like a demon
on the top of a high mountain ; and Inman’s small
Sart as the hero is sufficiently good; also J. P.
'’Neill, as a fat Irish person, has many points
about him. In fact, there is no very bad person
in the co.; the trouble is that, except for the lady
above-named, it keeps a respectable dead level
throughout, and there is no star in it worth men-
tioning. Still, the mounting is good, and the
tights of the ballet are surprising enough for any-
body.
* -* * *
Maggie Moore’s version of the improper
person who sat for the altogether will run at
Sydney Royal till the end of next week. The
public hasn’t begun, in any sense, to weary of
the partially unclothed heroine, nor does it tire
of Svengali and Gecko; and the Rev. Bagot—
who snuffles through the nose of J. P. West—and
Zou-Zou also maintain their hold on the affections
of the community more or less. But, all the
same, there is so very little of “ Trilby,” and so
much of what there is consists of very moderate
variety business, that three weeks should about
exhaust the possibilities of the tawdry little show
in Sydney, and but for the magnetism of Maggie
Moore and the wild hairiness of Svengali three
days would have been enough. The author didn’t
write Svengali’s hair or Maggie Moore’s feet,
therefore The Bulletin feels itself justified in ex-
pressing the opinion that that person should be
slit up lengthways, and the germs of literature
should be picked out of him and carefully smashed
with a club on a board. He hasn’t got the right
kind of germ, apparently.
** * *
Harry Rickards’ people come and go and
come again in a bewildering fashion. Sydney had
just fairly begun to regret the vanishment of the
wonderful Frantz Family from the Tivoli, and
now that amazing combination of bounders is
back again, and bounding as if nothing had hap-
pened. While they are there they comprise about
as much astonishment as the public can hold, and
the fact that Mons. Provo is also on the premises
seems hardly worth mentioning, while the pre-
sence of William Crackles is too small a circum-
stance to be alluded to. Crackles and the
others may assume their former dimensions when
the Family is gone, but at present they are more
or less eclipsed.
ifc
The new Palace of Varieties in Pitt-street,
Sydney, is to open somewhere about November
next. Phil. Goatcher is sole lessee, and Arthur
Garner manager, and G. L. Goodman will repre-
sent. the front of the establishment. Under such
management the Palace, which has been built by
“Tattersall” Adams on lavish principles, should
put up a record. Arthur Garner, who is probably
about the best-qualified man for the work who
could have been procured, has been ranging over
Europe and America gathering up the best artists
of all kinds, regardless of cost, and it is intended
that the new enterprise will go to the front on its
very first night, and stay there permanently.
