The spirit of Richard B. Sheridan hovered
reproachfully around Melbourne Princess’ until
Tuesday last, when “ The Rivals ” passed away
and Mrs. Malaprop’s solemn chant was turned off
at the meter. On Saturday the modern American
rendering of old English comedy was preceded by
an alleged idyll, in one act, taken from the
French. The Bulletin’s remarks upon “Grin-
goire, or the Ballad Monger,” are tempered by the
belief that the idyll will not trouble Australia any
further. Hence there is no reason for protesting
against poet Gringoire’s doggerel wig and grue-
some countenance, or for dealing harshly with
Louis the Eleventh, although these and other
matters were very trying at the time. The
idyll is so feeble in its motive, dramatic
construction and dialogue, that fine acting would
be thrown away upon it, which possibly explains
why Nat Goodwin and Co. confined themselves to
an inferior performance. The dejected bard and
the rampageous Louis were equally unreal crea-
tions, and a few courtiers didn’t appear to know
whether they were concerned in a serious under-
taking or a Gilbertian joke.
* * * *
“In Mizzoura,” the simple, straightfor-
ward yarn of rough ’Murkan life, is having a
three-nights revival this week. On Saturday the
final week of the Goodwin season in Melbourne
will start with “ The Nominee,” and another idyll,
entitled “ Romeo’s First Love.” “ The Nominee,”
a farcical comedy, is said to be one of Goodwin’s
most popular successes, and it will introduce the
beauteous Miss Maxine Elliott to this country.
** * *
George Rignold, having wearied of wait-
ing for ‘ ‘ Cheer, Boys, Cheer ” to catch on at the
Royal, changed his bill to “ Romany Rye ” on
Monday night. The previous productions of
“Romany Rye” in Melbourne date back to the
early dawn of boom-time, when the city was com-
paratively virtuous, so it is quite likely that G. R.
Sims’ melodrama will rank as a bright memory of
the past and do large business.
* *- * *
Ovide Musin, likewise Madame Musin and
Edward Scharf, the most genuinely admired
trinity of music-makers that ever travelled these
parts, were heard again at the Athenaeum Hall on
Saturday night. The entertainment given by these
unassuming artists is more delightful than before in
the instrumental numbers, and as regards the
lady’s showy vocalism, it might be said that her
high notes have improved sufficiently to make up
for what seems to be a new and faulty production
of the lower ones. Ovide Musin commits none of
the unpleasant noises which other great violinists
are apt to scrape as evidence of their technical
skill. He carries out the intention of Providence
by playing beautiful music in the sweetest possible
way. His fiddle talks to the soul of his audience,
and Edward Scharf turns the piano to the same
good account. Any sane person who has heard those
instruments tortured and thumped respectively by
Camille Urso and the furious Russian boy who
visited us last year, will feel all the better for lis-
tening to Musin and Scharf.
** * *
Carl Hertz’s new tricks and illusions are
reminiscent of old tricks and illusions performed
by the same prestidigitateur in days of yore, and
the best of them are rather too ingenious to excite
much active curiosity as to how they may have
happened. Carl Hertz got a warm reception at
Melb. Opera House last Saturday, but at the
finish of his long turn there was no burning desire
for an encore. Next Saturday the Rickards com-
pany will enlist another important recruit in
Beautiful Jessica, described as Hingland’s Queen
of the Wire. Already there is a pretty little wire
dancer hereabouts, so Beautiful Jessica will need
to be something extra special.
sfc * * *
Revd. Charles Clark, who lectured in the
echoing Town Hall a few weeks back, is about to
make his very last appearance in Melbourne at the
Athenaeum Hall, commencing on Saturday (22nd)
with his original Dickens entertainment. The
advertisement mentions that Revd. Clark will also
repeat his two new lectures which were not easily
heard in the Town Hall. At the smaller building
they will be quite audible to everybody who gets
inside, but considering that the chances of hear-
ing the old favorite are so limited in number his
admirers had better arrive early lest they should
get left outside on the mat.

At Poverty Point,
The American rights of “ Joseph of Canaan” have been
bought by Mrs. Brown-Potter. The beauteous Bellew
would make a far more convincing Joseph than George
Rignold.
In “ Cheer Boys Cheer” Rignold makes up the part of
the Marquis of Chepstowe as Frank Madden, the jingoistic
boss Tory of Vic. Assembly, little brother of his ex-wax-
cellency bruiser Madden, and the especial white-haired
boy of the Argus. In the absence of any Australian
House of Lords, Limelight George was forced to model
his Marquis on the most Conservative person available.
Hence, no doubt, Madden.
“The Rivals,” as represented at Melb. Princess’s by
“ Gnat ” Goodwin and his compatriots, is very remindful
of Mark Twain’s “ Yankee at the Court of King Arthur.”
Goodwin’s Bob Acres is as great an anachronism, cavort-
ing amongst Sheridan’s phrases and characters, as is
Twain’s hero amongst the people and in the atmosphere
of the Arthurean legends. And the other people, with
their twang and up-to-date American oddities, are far out
of the picture, and each scene suggests a corner at a New
York masquerade. Nat Goodwin should have taken for
his motto during the seasons in these parts : “ Of Ameri-
cans, by Americans, for Australians.”
Haresfoot: “ ‘ The Rivals,’ the best of English comedies,
yet ‘ dates,’ as the saying is. The moral is that a piece as
good ought to be written up to the present day. Coppin,
a coarse and slightly vulgar actor, had more "the hang of
Acres than Goodwin, or even Jefferson. Besides, Copp.
would be supported by Lambert or Rogers, Brooke, and
Mrs. Phillips, Hans’ mother. There was a swing which
might, after all, be only an echo of the real old time, but
now there is only the shadow of a shade. Performers
have lost the grand style, so called, in its artificiality, and
are merely the Collins-street Block masquerading in 18th
century clothes, powder and patches.”
English place-names in Sheridan’s “Rivals” as pro-
nounced by Nat Goodwinites : Derby becomes Durrby
instead of Darby ; Bath becomes Bath with a short a
instead of Barth. Old comedy translated into the Yankee
language becomes quaint stuff.
There are more ways of giving an affirmative answer
than saying “ Yes.” Pretty Blanche Walsh, of the Nat
Goodwin Co., has a way of her own. It is mutely elo-
quent. Her “Yes” is a kiss-shaped pursing up of the
lips and a wrinkling of the chin combined with a sideling
of the head, very charming to observe. Miss Walsh is
one of the few actresses who act with their faces when
they are not speaking.
Melb. :“ ‘ Cheer, Boys, Cheer,’ is a failure. Rignold,
now about Irving’s age, is magnificently preserved, but
this piece might have been saved with Scot Inglis as the
hero. George must understand he cannot be beautiful for
ever. Youth has to get a show. Besides, he has not
Melb. by the wool, like Sydney.”

“ A Passion Flower laid on his grave.” London World
on Tristan and Isolde, by Augustus Harris’ opera co.
English musical critics agree that Wagner’s “ Tristan
and Isolde ” equals any opera ever composed. Yet it was
written in 1859, and has never been properly done in
London till this year. Time’s revenge.
New “The Rivals” is done in three acts, the American
sensible three-act “ Hamlet” may as well be tried, knock-
ing out the flummery, and playing in two hours.

London Novelty Theatre, was exactly paralleled by ama-
teurs, in a Manchester theatre two years ago when
Romeo killed Tybalt.
Fitzgeralds’ Circus took £436 on opening night in Rock-
hampton (Q.). The big show lost one night only on the
whole of its Q. tour. That was caused by the lighter
which carried their traps going aground in the Fitzroy.
When it is remembered that the Brough co. were playing-
to a packed house on the same night as the above sum
was taken, it is fair to assume that good shows will pay in
the North during winter.

The Quid Nunes of Melb. Pav<s were asking why Comique
Godfrey was so mildly starred, at the Opera House, for
the first part of his last week. The explanation was to be
found in a current rumor that Godfrey had taken St.
George’s Hall, and was going to open in opposition to his
beloved ’Arry. However, towards the end of the week,
the capitals turned once more in Godfrey’s favor, where-
fore speculators concluded again that his ardor had
slackened in re running alone. The right conclusion is
that he has made up his mind for a dive, but will proceed
fair and softly.

A lady at London Royal Aquarium jumps from a height
of 150 feet on to solid ground for the sake of thrilling a
bloodthirsty public. Were the jump bona-fide, the re-
sult would, of course, be eternal smash for the jumper,
so she is aided by wire mechanism. The thrill of the
public therefore springs either from the thought of the
“ might have been ” or the hope of “ what may be.”
Music, which hath power to soothe the savage breast,
hath no power to soothe the savage music-teachers
of Melb., who are at each other’s throats, in press
controversies and private vilifications, and various
libel-actions are incubating. Meanwhile, Prof. Marshall
Hall declares that there are not a dozen private and
school music-teachers in Melbourne who are fairly compe-
tent to teach the five-finger exercise.
Re critic Clement Scott’s desire to visit Australia on a
lecturing-tour, overtures on the subject were made to
R. S. Smythe a year or two ago, and the Much-Travelled
declined. He considered that London D.T.’s theatrical
reporter had no money out here. Australians would
rather see plays than hear a man talk about them.
Two theatrical notices from Melb. Age. Firstly: “A
spectacular melodrama is perhaps scarcely to be judged
from a lofty literal standpoint, but ‘ Cheer, Boys, Cheer,’

is, moreover, deficient in situations.” Secondly : “ ‘Cheer,

the London press on the occasion of its production.”
Death extinguished “the Vital Spark” (Jenny Hill)
early last month, and that remarkable little woman was
duly buried. Also, a chaplain said over her grave “She
was bright, social, kind, and loving. The good memory
dwells deep down in the hearts of those nearest and
dearest to her.” Rorty Jenny, whose strong point was
female coster impersonation at the ’alls, had played a
season in burlesque at the Gaiety, and run her own pub.
Three or four years back W. & M. tried to get her for their
Melb. panto.
We were at Balranald the other week, writes a poor
player to The Bulletin. Success had thawed the
managerial heart, and one night we passed in about a
dozen aboriginals, who seemed, from the boards, to
thoroughly enjoy the performance. After the show, we
found the nigs waiting for us at the stage-door : “ You
gib it thickpen’ ? ” asked one, apparently the boss.
“What for?” inquired the manager. “Whaffor? We
bin werry good. We sit an’ listen all you yabber, not
say anything. We think it deserve him thickpen’.”
Mrs. Kendal on American theatre-goers, apropos their
appreciation of the “ naughty ” bits in “ Mrs. Tanqueray ”
to the exclusion of the intended “ moral ” I go
through the streets not looking at the spittings on the
pavements, but at your beautiful blue sky. Your audi-
ences come to the theatre with never a look at the sky,
but everyone looks at the spit.”
Finn : Many burlesque and song-and-dance artists
quickly weaken and, in some cases, quite lose their voices,
because, in response to an encore of a song and dance,
they reappear when breathless and tired and, instead of
repeating the dance, repeat the last verse of the chorus,
thus straining their voices. Two well-known exceptions
to this failing are Maggie Moore and Alice Leamar. When
either of these artists is encored for a song and dance
she repeats the dance only.
A curious explanation is given of the reason why Augus-
tus Harris acted for a few years after taking Drury Lane.
Every Drury Lane manager, who acts three years, has a
right to a pension of £365 a-year out of the fund.
Wary Harris, knowing the ups-and-downs, would not even
despise this, but made it safe.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The N.S.W. Land-tax Department has fairly
started now to wrestle with a stupendous difficulty. The
trouble is created by the mortgage provisions of G. 11.
Reid’s Act. Land which is not mortgaged pays Id. in the
£ per annum on its capital value. Land which is mort-
gaged pays Id. in the £ on the capital value that exists
over and above the mortgage, while the mortgagee pays
income-tax on the interest derived from the mortgage.
This works out in a lot of remarkable ways. Leaving out
the exemptions, which are a very small matter, £96,600
worth of land with no mortgage on it would be taxed £4OO
a year. On the other hand, £96,000 worth of land with a
mortgage of £48,000 on it at 5 per cent, would pay land-
tax on £48,000, equal to £2OO a year, and income-tax (6d.
in the £) on the interest (£2400) of the mortgage, equal
to £6O, making the total of land and income-tax £260.
Therefore, there is a clear gain of £l4O by fixing up a
bogus mortgage on the property. If the bogus mortgage
is for £72,000 at 5 per cent, then the total land and
income-tax will be £l9o—-a saving of £2lO, or more than
half the tax. Again, if the same property was mortgaged
in the boom-time for £96,000 at 5 per cent., and owing to
depreciation the mortgage now covers the whole value,
and it all comes under the income-tax provisions, the tax,
instead of being £4OO, will be only £l2O. Therefore, the
law offers a solid inducement to every Fat Man to put a
large bogus mortgage on his property, and he is doing it,
and the loss to the revenue is likely to be considerable.
A’so, the labor of trying to bowl out the Fat transgressor
will be heavy and expensive. That is why they are
“lending” Public Service Commissioner Coghlan to the
Land-tax office.
* * * *
The financial situation in Maoriland throws
some light on Tasmanian Treasurer Fysh’s great con-
version-scheme. For some years past the M.L. Govt, has
been gradually converting its high-priced loans into long-
dated 4 or 3£ per cent, stock. As it gives the present
market-value of the old stock in new securities, the pro-
cess has added £2,038,973 to the public debt, and at the
same time it has reduced the annual interest by £159,644.
If about £35,000 a year of this amount was put away in a
sinking-fund it would accumulate, by the time the new
stock falls due, into enough to pay the two millions of
extra debt incurred through the conversion—always pro-
vided it could be put away in some place where no hungry
Treasurer could get at it; and the revenue would still be
£124,000 a year better off. But as not a single penny of
the saving has been put into a sinking-fund the debt is
permanently increased, and the fact that the interest has
been reduced by increasing the debt is only made an ex-
cuse for floating another big loan and increasing the
debt still more ; and, though the burden on the revenue
is £159,000 less, no one suggests putting that amoant into
the public works fund and dispensing with further bor-
rowed money. Treasurer Fysh’s scheme, showing how
the Australasian debts can be converted, and a huge
saving in interest effected, and part of the saving put to
a sinking-fund to pay off the increased debt, and all the
rest, is very beautiful on paper —but who is going to hold
that sinking-fund? *
* • * *
The most amazing balance-sheet The Bulletin
Has come across for a long time is that of Montgomerie’s
Brewery 00. (Melbourne). For the half-year ended 30th
June the receipts were £6477, and the working expenses
£7587 ; therefore, there was a loss for the six months of
£lllO. The co. commenced the half-year with £2661 to
credit of profit and loss, and ended with a credit of £1551
only, the rest being swallowed up. And this £1551 is
actually called “net profit” in the balance-sheet.
The previous half-year Montgomerie’s Brewery made
a loss of £1661 oil its ordinary trading, and wrote-
off £2OOO for “contingent losses,” therefore it went
£3661 to the bad. And it then put down “ Net profit,
£2661,” which was about the finest piece Of bluff on
record. This concern, which is making a big loss
and shows a serious falling-off in its business
and is loaded down with a stupendous over-
draft, includes among its alleged assets “ Goodwill
£25,000” —which is evidently based on the assumption
that any capitalist would naturally pay £25,000 for the
privilege of losing at the above rate. The co.
has no reserve fund; it has seemingly not a penny of loose
cash it can call its own ; its overdraft is apparently up to
£68,667, which is a little worse than last half-year; its
profit and loss balance is down to £1551, so another year
at the present rate will begin to make a large hole in its
capital ; its liabilities to shareholders and creditors are
£214,000, and its visible assets, even at its own valuation,
are less than £190,000 (this is not reckoning the goodwill,
which is wholly invisible); it does a small business
which seems to be getting smaller; and, judging by the
“ net profit” item, it seems to be run by a most wonder-
ful set of financiers.

Hudson Bros. (Limited), which Premier Reid
quoted in his budget speech as a great example of the
alleged revival which has set in, reports that it has suc-
cessfully made a big reduction in wages, and that: “ The
amount of business transacted for the past half-year is
barely equal to that of the corresponding period of last
year, but your directors have the satisfaction of reporting
they have since secured a fair contract for rolling stock,
and that more work is offering.” Apparently, therefore,
whatever revival there is consists mostly in Government
orders to be paid for with borrowed money.

* * * »
The Mildura mortgagees have decided to reduce
interest to 5 per cent, (it formerly averaged 9 per cent.)
and not to call up any principal for five years. Under
these conditions Mildura should have a fair chance of
success. So should the mortgagees ; if they had demanded
the letter of their bond the only possible result would
have been the practical abandonment of the settlement,
and then the securities would have deteriorated to next to
nothing. It was a case of throwing 4 per cent, overboard
to lighten the ship and prevent the principal and the 9
per cent, sinking together.
* » * *
The State auditor has come uppermost in his
trouble with the Victorian Local Government system.
For a long time the local bodies, when the State auditor
showed that the cash had been misappropriated, or that
the bloated councillor had done what he oughtn’t, simply
refused to pay audit-fees, as a warning to the Government
official not to discover unpleasant things in the future ;
and owing to a leakage in the Act it seemed doubtful if
the latter could recover. The doubt has been settled by
a Supreme Court order to Winchelsea Shire Council to
pay £3B 10s. audit-fees, and the law expenses incurred in
trying to scare off the audit-man amount to about £lOO.
* * * *
N.S.W. Supreme Court decides that the busted
municipality of Lambton needn’t pay the £7OOO it owes to
the Commercial Bank, because the aldermen, when they
borrowed it, didn’t advertise the purposes for which the
loan was intended, as required by the Act. In other
words, the aldermen aren’t, fined for breaking the law, hut
the bank is fined heavily because the aldermen broke the
law !
* * * •
Someone sends along the balance-sheet of the
municipal district of Moruya (N.S.W.). It sets forth that
the Government grants and endowment for the half-year
are £ll7, and that the whole expenditure on roads and
bridges and other works is £26 13s. sd. Out of £173 ex-
pended, £lO7 goes in salaries, rent of council chambers,
election expenses, legal ex.’s, printing and frill. There
are some quaint municipalities in N.S.W. For the year
ended Feb., ’95, Marsfield spent £8 on public works and
£29 on salaries and office expenses to see that the £8 was
properly expended ; Grenfell spent £260 in salaries, &c.,
to see that £174 was properly laid out on works ; Hill End
spent £56 watching the expenditure of £67 ; and Gundagai
spent £l2l in keeping an eye on £ll2 worth of contracts.
On the other side, Ulinarragot through £1456 of contracts
with only £63 worth of supervision, and Berry spent only
£B4 in bossing £1037 worth of work. Some of the muni-
cipalities, however, seem to be only man-traps for the
State endowment.
* * *
Some Victorians are starting a new woollen mill
at Footscray (Melb.), and the capital has all been sub-
scribed locally. N.S.W. is thinking of starting a beet-
sugar industry at Tenterfield, and a gathering of prospec-
tive beet-growers cheered lustily when the local member
talked of going to England to persuade John Bull to put
up the money for building the mill. That is the difference
between the two provinces. The Victorian trusts to him-
self—most of the time, at all events. The cornstalk
hardly ever does—he can’t be found dead without the
assistance of British capital.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent:—Negotiations have
for some time been pending between an agent of a certain
bank-official and the family of a man, since dead, con-
cerning a large sum of money said to be lying in one of
the banks in the deoeased’s name, about which the family
knows nothing, but which, as heirs to their father’s estate,
they could legally claim if they knew exactly where it
was. As it means to the clerk the loss of his billet, he
refuses to part with the information under a stiff figure.
At any rate, the clerk has as much right to levy blackmail
on the family for a portion of the money as the bank has
to rob them of the whole—which, all the same, is morally
no right at all. When is that Unclaimed Money Act
coming ?
* » * •
Awhile back the estate of a certain eccentric old
Australian identity was sold outright to some smart
financiers at a price that the executors had very good
reasons to congratulate themselves upon —at the time.
So very smart indeed were the buyers known to be that
the executors were at a loss to understand how they ever
came to make much a palpable mistake in valuing the
property. The mystery, however, was cleared up long
afterwards, when the executors learned that one of the
financial syndicate had all along held a bank-deposit
receipt of the defunct eccentric’s for £l7,26o—the exact
figure at which they bought the whole estate. No record
of the deposit was found by the executors in deceased’s
books ; its existence was known only to the man who by
some means had dropped across it, and the bank that
held the money. And both the man and the bank were
interested in keeping things dark; the bank because, in
the absence of a badly-wanted Act of Parliament, it is
allowed to annex for its own use all such unclaimed
moneys, and the man because of his contemplated deal
with the executors for the estate. Consequently, the
£17,000 odd was not taken into account by the executors
when they closed with the smart financiers’ offer for “ the
sale of the whole estate, absolutely.”

Tas. railways made a profit last year of £29,291.
“ Profit ” here doesn’t mean interest on cost of construc-
tion, £3,521,956. Were interest charged to the railways
account instead of to general revenue, the annual loss
would be over £120,000.
* * * *
Vic. apiarists are making great improvements
in honey-production, and will soon ship largely to London.
Under expert tuition, Vic. honey has improved in value
from 1-jd. per lb. to 4d. One apiarist in the Wimmera
district (he is also a wheat farmer) last year netted £3OO
from honey alone.
• * * *
The S.Q. cane-crop was terribly damaged by
the recent frost, and ruin is writ large over the farms
around Bundaberg. In Gingin district the crop has
actually rotted off large areas, the new Central Mill not
being quite ready in time to take over the cane —which
sad state of affairs was caused by the delay in obtaining a
locomotive from England for use on the tramlines. In
the Isis district a similar state of affairs prevails. Despite
clerical protests, Sunday work prevails at all the available
mills.
» * * *
One of the Smellbourne accountants who
administer insolvent estates filed his schedule the other
day. Which raises the suggestion that an insolvent
trustee is the proper person to deal with his own assets,
if he be qualified to handle other people’s.
* • * *
Alleged letter from Longmuir to Fink, dated
July, ’92, produced in the City of Melbourne Bank prose-
cution :—“ My dear Fink, —I suppose it is no use remind-
ing you that you have still not returned the documents
re General Assets Company. Their being executed cannot
affect your position in the slightest to the extent of a
penny, and it is rather galling to find that the prophecies
of all my friends have come true, and you persistently
refuse to assist me, although doing so costs you so little.
If I was stupid enough to think that you were a man of
your word, and would not go back on your many pro-
mises to do anything in your power to put me right with
the directors, yet you need not be so d d disobliging
when you lose nothing.—Yours faithfully, Colin M. Long-
muir.”
The assistant-manager of the bank swore that
he never saw or heard of this extraordinary letter before.
It is a beautiful letter so far as concerns the directors on
trial, inasmuch as in it Longmuir—the man that’s dead —
professes to tacitly admit that he and not the directors
was responsible for whatever Was wrong with Fink’s
account; therefore, he wanted Fink to put him right
with the directors. And Longmuir, being dead as afore-
mentioned, can’t deny the letter, or repudiate the respon-
sibility, or explain anything connected with it. It appears
more and more that Longmuir was the only man who
knew anything, and the fact that he is dead —if he is still
dead—can’t be too much regretted.

* * * *
Anticipated that next year’s Vic. butter-season
will completely wipe out the majority of the minor butter-
factories and creameries now cumbering the country dis-
tricts. Already collapses have not been few, and the
probable low prices of next year will leave the industry in
the hands of the big co’s and monopolists. As matters
are and have been, wherever a dozen or so cookies are
gathered together, so surely will be found a butter-factory
in charge of a heaven-born expert, whose salary generally
runs into a couple of pounds weekly, and whose lack of
experience generally leads to the waste of all material
committed to his charge. Very few of these small con-
cerns do any good.
• * * *
Korumburra miners are petitioning Vic. Parlia-
ment to have an import-duty of 2s. 6d. per ton put on
coal, so as to shut out the Newcastle article. The claim
of the owners of the Gippsland coal-fields to consideration
at the hands of Victorians is not great. A section is
under suspicion for compulsory sales of land to employes,
and all of them deserve blame for th'> cu-u 1
fisnness shown during the progress of the recent N.S.W.
coal-strike. Instead of seizing the opportunity to popu-
larise their coal, they raised prices to the highest limit,
and delivered it very imperfectly screened. If they had
been smart business-men, they would have put forward a
big effort to capture the Victorian trade. Gippsland coal
is, for household purposes, generally superior to N.S.W.
coal. It does not produce “ smuts ” and ‘ blacks and
pungent discoloring smoke. It is, as a rule, a good house-
hold coal, and therefore a bad gas coal. But under the
special circumstances of the case, the coalfields being
Fat-Men monopolies, a protective duty would be a source
of danger, unless the State took stringent precautions to
give the miner ai)d the public their fair share of protec-
tion from the monopolists.
* * * *
A point in Vic. licensing-law : A publican
before opening a private-bar in his house must apply for
and get permission from the licensing-court. It has for
years been the legal belief that this permit applied only
to the then licensee, ceasing when he or she left the pre-
mises ; however, a smart hotel-broker, who had evidently
swallowed the Act, parchment and all, thought differently.
He advised a client that a private-bar which hadn t been
used for years could be re-opened, as the. right had never
been cancelled or withdrawn, and applied to the license
of the house, not to the licensee. She accepted the hint
in defiance of legal opinion, and was prosecuted ; but the
broker’s view was upheld. Now publicans who have been
fined for similar alleged offences, having found they have
unrevoked private-bar licenses, are besieging Govt, for a
return of the fines wrongly inflicted.
• * * •
The policy of the Sydney liquor-interest bosses
just now is to grant long leases for public-houses under
their control. “Rents are still coming down,” they tell
one another in confidence, “and won’t rise again in our
time at any rate.” Moral: If you want a pub. take it on
a short lease.
* * * *
The shareholders in the B.H. South who have
accepted the published statement that the co. had some-
thing like £20,000 in hand must have felt bad when they
read the directors’ report and balance-sheet. The cash in
hand is shown to be £2O odd, while the liquid assets are
less than they were six months ago. Profit and loss
account shows that whereas £10,453 was brought forward
to this last half-year, only £8394 remains to be carried for-
ward. Outstanding liabilities are about £BOO less, but
liquid assets are also £7OO less. On January 1 there were
201 tons of concentrates on the mine, and on July 1 there
were 529 tons. Assuming that advances against ship-
ments of concentrates were as satisfactory during this
last half-year as in the preceding period, it seems, then,
that the co. has got 328 tons of concentrates to the good
and £2059 to the bad on the half-year’s operations. As
concentrates are not worth more than about £5 per ton,
it would thus appear that the co. has actually made a
small loss on the term.
For this result perhaps not one in thirty of the
shareholders was prepared—the present writer being one
of the 29. And the grounds appeared good enough. In
the last report a profit of two or three thousand was
shown, and the general manager, after saying that the
mine never looked as well as it then did, promised better
things when certain developmental works, then near com-
pletion, were finished. Since then the production of ore
has been sustained, and likewise the output of concen-
trates, whose value has also remained fairly constant.
The only explanation to be derived from a singularly in-
conclusive report and statement of accounts is that the
ore has taken more getting lately; at any rate, £17,073
has been spent in winning 29,951 tons this last half as
against £14,102 in winning 29,745 tons in the preceding
half.
Allowances possibly ought to be made for the
alleged desire of certain influential people to effect a
transfer of this mine from present shareholders —or from
some of them, at least. And somehow the name at the
foot of the directors’ report does not just now inspire con-
fidence. In these circs, it is a great pity that the com-
munication to the shareholders and the public was not
made particularly ample. As it stands, and placing upon
it the only construction which seems possible, few of
greater importance have been issued by any B.H. co. The
South’s ore bodies are confessedly poor in silver, but they
are rich in lead, large and uniform above the average.
No large profits could be expected ; but on the basis of
the returns for the last half-year of ’95, when, as before
said, a small profit was made, it was expected that with
improved working appliances and the steady advance in
metal-saving by concentration larger profits would accrue
in the future. *No better work in any direction is, how-
ever, set out here ; and the work for the latter half of
1895 was not as good as it was in the first half. Consider-
ing the additions to the plant, &c., the results have there-
fore grown steadily worse. The manager says he has now
1100 tons of concentrates in the crude slimes ; and these
are to be operated. Further, the rock-breaking capacity
is to be increased, this having become necessary in conse-
quence of the increased hardness of the ore at the lower
levels. So far, at the best, more than half of the silver
has “ remained unrecovered ” (managements object to the
term “ lost,” and perhaps rightly); and this and the con-
stant additions and amendments made to the concentrat-
ing plants prove that, after all, sulphide-treatment, as
practised so far, is little if anything beyond experiment.
And it isn’t particularly comforting to reflect that these
experiments are consuming very considerable quantities
of what at present are reckoned the best ores to operate.

E. A. Ashcroft, of the Sulphide Corporation
(Ashcroft’s process) Ltd. : “The co. had purchased 360
acres of land at Cockle Creek, near Newcastle, and the
works were now being erected there at a cost of £250,000.
. . . When in operation these works would find employ-
ment for about 5000 men. . . . They had enough ore in
the Central, which they had also purchased, to keep the
works going for at least twenty years.” Ashcroft is in
Broken Hill, supervising the erection of concentrating
plant on the Central. But it isn’t on a scale to keep 5000
men going.
* * • *
The Broken Hill fire is slowly burning itself
out. To keep it down last half-year cost £3OOO ; to put it
completely out is now regarded as impossible. Fires in
very big mines will not be put out.
♦ * * *
It is significant that the draining of the Junc-
tion mine’s deep western level has drained also the North.
This seems to prove quite conclusively that the North
already has the Junction lode. In which case the Junc-
tion North gets it only in a corner, though the underlay
is into it all the way. But it’s bad for the Junction Con-
solidated and Silver Hill, and all that recently much-
boomed run of little shows. And, besides, it strengthens
the case for a contention, not always popular, put forward
in many Bulletin pars, concerning the course and charac-
ter of the B.H. lode at the north end.

Editor Bulletin, —Cobarites are not “ chagrined
and amazed that the phenomenal richness of the Peak
has not given them another boom.” Rather the opposite.
They are satisfied to let the world keep its ignorance of
the rich blue and brown lode while they rake in the spoil.
They have nothing to sell, and certainly “ their energies
are devoted to the mines nearer home.” They want the
ground for themselves, and that very desire, 1 think,
proves the value of their mines as compared with any
which are being stocked and exploited by the boomtide
press.—D. C. Green, Cobar.
• • * •
The opal-miners at White Cliffs (N.S.W,), out-
side the recently-floated English co., propose to send to
Europe their own agent, in the hope that thus they may
dodge the middleman, whom they suspect, with much
reason, of having mercilessly fleeced them hitherto. Several
hundreds have been subscribed, with the object of sending
over a pushing man. All attempts to get rid of small
individual parcels have up to date utterly failed ; and on
the field (which Father Curran is just now visiting), buyers
are offering prices so ridiculously low as to set at defiance
the current statement that opal is in good demand again.
* * * *
Honesty is not the West policy.
Deeble, the owner of the Bendigo United
Pyrites works, is about to establish branch works at
Wyalong. The completion of the Climo chlorination
works on that camp has been long delayed by legal com-
plications among the owners.
According to Minister Foster, the new Vic.
Mines Bill will include provisions for ventilation. Foster
thinks that when “ holing through ” is practicable it is
the best plan, and quotes for an example the New Chum
and Shenandoah in Bendigo, each at present with its
separate winze. The abolition of royalty, he declares, is
only delayed by the fact that certain agreements per-
petuated by the Act of ’B5 are still in force; but, when
they expire, he says that the Assembly “will take precious
good care that payment of royalty to private individuals
never creeps into the law again.” Tribute, he admits, is
perplexing, but he finds two cardinal principles in security
of tenure and the substitution of “ net” for “gross in
the percentage of the proceeds given to the working
miner. That word “ net,” he says, would lighten a lot of
hardship for the tributor.

Dear Bulletin,— Apropos the many tales of
weary marches through hot and dusty regions in the West.
I have just received a letter from my brother, who has
finished a ten-davs’ tramp from Deptford, Gippsland(Vic.),
to Batlow (N.S.W.), covering 210 miles through snow not
less than 9in. and in some parts 3ft. 6in. deep. Refreshing
reading for parched Australians. —W.M.
“ A start is to be made to open out on Bickford’s
lode in the Croesus mine (W.A.) at a depth of 20ft. It is
expected the lode will be richer than on the surface.”
Very deep sinking. It would be a striking novelty to
hear for once that some lode or other was expected to be
poorer with depth. That is the fact with nine out of ten
lodes all over the world, nevertheless.
* * * *
The special commissioner of the Age and Sydney
D.T., writing on Westralian mines, avers that one alleged
yield of 400 oz. a week was kept up for months by the use
of purchased gold till the mine was taken over by Bull
and Co. for £250,000, when the returns dropped—to
lOdwt. They bought bullion, and J. Bullion bought the
mine. The commissioner himself, at Kalgoorlie, was
offered 100 guineas, and another £lOO if the mine floated,
“for a favorable report.” The confidence-trick. For
match-box, read mine.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The writer of those Age and
Sydney D.T. articles on W.A. makes a few mistakes, and
I hasten to correct him. Burbank’s isn’t a cement show,
and, furthermore, a circle of 12 miles doesn’t represent
all the W.A. fields. Most of the men who write of W.A.
only see Bayley’s and the Derry and the Loch, and then
size up an enormous area of country on the sampling of
half-a-pound of dirt. One duffer doesn’t damn a field,
any more than one Boulder proves that all mines are
Boulders. The Age writer compares W.A., quite a
newly-opened country, with Vic. Well, then. How
much capital has been slung away on frauds in Vic. since
1854? How does W.A. compare with N.S.W., which is
the biggest duffer gold-mining country on record ? The
matter written weekly by one Sydney mining-expert—may
God give him modesty ! —is immeasurably worse than the
bulk of W.A. scribes’ reports in its vast promises which
are never fulfilled, and its statements that some rotten
N.S.W. mine “will start crushing shortly.” Westralian
mining is solid, and W.A. will be a field when London is
in ashes. If the Western mining reports are more fraudu-
lent generally than truthful, it follows that for three
years over 50,000 people have been engaged in a con-
spiracy to play the confidence-trick with the whole world,
And that is an impossibility.—Randolph Bedford.

* * • *
The Coolgardie death-roll is a long one. Reuben
Ricard, who died in the country, was one of the most
competent, best-known, and best-loved of American
mining men, and it is a pity he “went over” before the
world was told what he thought of the country. Captain
Vaudery, recently found dead in his tent, was a well-
known Ballarat manager, who opened several copper-
mines in N.S.W. He did well in the West, having the
luck to be conveniently near when Coolgardie got a new
life in the transformation of the Great Boulder. He re-
presented English money, and made it for his employers
as well as himself. In the death of Fitzgerald Moore
Australia has lost perhaps its boss boomer, and that is
saying a great deal. Moore was generally the first in a
boom and the first to leave it. He once boomed Cobar and
scooted ; he also boomed poor little Tasmania almost to
the verge of the madhouse. Amidst all his talk—and he
could talk!—he was remembered more on account of a
certain historical nod than anything else.
* * * •
One W.A. newspaper—the Menzies Miner —
thinks the time has arrived to admit that a great number
of the mines floated are wild-cats, and to warn the public
that many of the men who profit most by the boom are
adventurers. It has determined to expose some of the
brazen schemes of plunder, but admits the “ task is
perilous, unpleasant—perhaps unprecedented. It would
be easier and no doubt temporarily more profitable for the
Miner to preserve a discreet silence, but, desirous of the
permanent prosperity of the community, the Miner
accepts the risk which attaches to duty.”

The prospectus of the Mount Lyell Golden Flux
Supply Co. appeals for a modest £3OOO (in 30,000 shares of
two “ bob” each), of which £2400 will represent working
capital, the vendors settling all flotation exes, out of the
remaining £6OO. Another 30,000 shares—a half-share in
the spec.—will go as payment for the property, and, inas-
much as the Mount Lyell mine can do with heaps of flux,
and the-property aforesaid is conveniently situated for
supplying that want, the Supply co. should float—if the
Mt. Lvell Co. can’t make better arrangements. The
prospectus calls attention to the success of the Tarra-
wingee Flux Co., which has paid 37 divs. up to date.

mysterious family.

COOLALTA
CAMEO
At a Sydney missionary meeting, the other
night, to farewell a young Sydney lady who had
“set herself apart for the Master’s work in Han-
kow, China,” Rev. Dr. Lawes said “ he was sorry
they hadn’t been able to avail themselves of the
services of willing sisters in New Guinea. He
was anxious to have them, though, of course, the
climate and the moral condition of the people
placed a great responsibility upon those taking
lady missionaries there.” Which is distinctly
funny, considering all we know about the moral
condition of Messrs. Hung Fat and Kow Tow, in
whom Australian women seem to be taking such a
lot of interest. “After the dedicatory prayer”
the young lady remarked that “she was going
forward because the Master had said ‘Go,’ and
QUESTIONABLE DIET.
FIRST DOMAINER: “ Wot d’yer think the parson advised me to do? W’y, to fill meself with the
’Oly Spirit!”
she was simply obeying. She loved the Chinese
because the Master loved them. (Applause).”
Judging from the fuss women make over the
bland and obsequious Chow, the latter is re-
garded in Australian missionary circles as God’s
Chosen. The pietistic cant talked about the
sensual Chow is absolutely sickening to the average
white man. It is increasingly plain that the
moving spirit in this woman-missionary business
is Adventure and not mere benevolence—else why
don’t all these exalted young people go and soothe
the dying hours of the miserable white wretches
in the Liverpool Asylum cancer-ward and such
places? Any woman of truly nun-like soul who
wants a field for her charity can find it at Liver-
pool Asylum, or in the Parramatta homes, where
there are hundreds of slowly-dying old people who
can’t rise out of bed.
* * * *
The banquet given by Lee Yan at Cairns
(Q.) to celebrate the birth of his son, the first
locally-born full-caste Chinese baby, was attended
by 400 guests, including many whites. The baby
“was first presented by his mother to Mr. Mae-
alister, our worthy Police Magistrate, who handled
it with evident care and delight, and finally be-
stowed a sort of ‘ bless you, my boy ’ kiss On the
infant’s innocent little face. Mr. Forbes followed
suit, and each of the guests did likewise.” (Cairns
is the only place in Australia where it is en regie
for whites to kiss Chinese babies and take off
their hats to the infants’ fathers.) Champagne
flowed freely, and, as a special mark of favor,
the only press representative present was unosten-
tatiously handed a jug of draught beer. The
first dish served at the banquet was stewed shark
fins, and the next baked dog, served with boiled
bamboo sprouts and white sauce. Two helpings
bore testimony to the fact that the P.M. enjoyed
this course immensely, and he embraced the
opportunity presented by the waits between the
helps to point out tb his fellow guests that the
Chinese as a nation were decidedly far in advance
of the rest of the world in matters of cookery.
Priam, a splendid St. Bernard dog, the property
of Mr. Hollis, at this stage of the festivity left the
room. Bird’s-nest soup, a most appetising delicacy,
was next brought forward ; then followed rat a la,
Russe, which was found to fully bear out the “affee
same chicken ” recommendation of the host.
Stewed duck and bacon, fat pork stewed with
giblets, Chinese preserved chickens (eggs hatched
for 19 days and then pickled), beche-de-iner soup,
preserved shrimps served in castor oil, sun-dried
oysters boiled in vinegar, fried catfish, seagull pie,
jelly-fish pudding, and innumerable other dainties
followed in rapid succession. Then came dessert
and the speeches, in which the P.M. was described
as “Welly good man.” The “good-luck” cash-
offerings made to the saddle-colored infant totalled
£250.

Latest Sydney case of leprosy (unreported).
Some time ago a lady living in the suburbs, when
in town, bought a bag of bananas—presumably the
popular “fourteen for sixpence ”-—at “a foreigner’s”
shop. When she got home, portion of a human
hand vvas found amongst the fruit. The police
have since removed the owner to the lazarette.
Hands and bananas ! Ugh !
The English, Irish, Scotch, and Australian
corps are, it is rumored, to be shortly reinforced
by a natural! sed-Chinese corps. And all the time
the poor Australian blackfellow, who is the one
man specially justified in repelling invaders, is left
without a captain, a rifle, or a pontifical blessing.
♦ * * *
The Chinese in Tasmania are almost
entirely confined to the East, where the tin-
mining has gradually dropped into their hands.
Of the 1000 Mongolians tolerated in Tasmania,
many are wealthy bosses employing white men,
and even the common or garden-variety of China-
man is far more patron kingly arrogant than any
native white. Apart from the West Coasteis,
Tasmanians would sooner think of objecting to
Bishop Montgomery than to Chinamen.
* * * *
It is recorded that the wife of a certain
Australian Governor, much boomed for his
generosity, officiated at the opening of some
charitable institution, the other day, and after the
ceremony had concluded H.E. turned to the ex-
pectant secretary, and producing a fiver from her
dainty purse, said: “Take a guinea from that,
Mr. Blank. And have you sovereigns for change ?
For I have to call at three other hospitals this
afternoon.” The Governor himself is contracting
the pleasing habit of telling everybody promiscu-
ously to “ put him down ” for various amounts,
which have, so far, failed to be forthcoming not-
withstanding repeated applications.
* * * *
Queensland has its weekly leper, N.S.W.
its weekly Ereetrade triumph, and Victoria its
weekly committal to gaol of some senile pauper.
The latest comes from Castlemaine, where Rev.
Hume Robertson objected to the “ sentiment ”
talked on the subject, and said that “gaol was a
very comfortable place.” Queer teaching for a
minister of the gawspel. “ I was a stranger, and
• —ye brought me before the Bench.”
By the way, a brilliant idea has struck the
Castlemaine bench aforesaid. If a man is charged
with being too poor to live they remand him for
three months. Thus he is disposed of in gaol, and
“no stigma is attached.” E. D. Williams, M.L.A.,
claims to be the father of this remarkably ingen-
ious solution.

Gov. Gorman ston is not very popular with
the Hobart “gods.” When he arrived at the
opening performance of “Trilby ” the band played
“God Save,” and the “loyal” subjects arose as
usual. The gallery, however, vented its opinion
in loud unanimous groans and hoots. It has not
forgiven H.E. for refusing to contribute towards
the funds of the local regatta, which a large
section of Slow-bart people look upon as their one
annual festival. Of this event the Governor has
always been patron since the days of Sir John
Franklin, but on last occasion the committee, on
account of H.E.’s refusal to subscribe, did not
invite him to attend. It must, however, be set
down in Lord Gormanston’s favor that in accept-
ing his present appointment, at an unprece-
dentedly small salary, he distinctly bargained that
he should not be expected to “part.”

The old order passeth. The old women must give
way to the New.

The eternal fitness of things at the Buxton
marriage was symbolised by a beer-van which per-
sistently followed the wedding procession. That
beer-van represented the skeleton in Gov. Buxton’s
cupboard, the apparition that won’t be laid, the
Worm which Dieth Not, the fly that eternally
gets into the gubernatorial cup. The Buxton
greatness was built on beer ; the family pile was
made out of coarse, common swipes ; the reason
why Buxton is a Governor and a baronet and
many other things is lush—booze—tanglefoot—
shypoo, the Accursed Drink ; and if he swore off
dog’s-nose and all its works he would have to
drop back into obscurity. When a beer-van
follows a man like that life becomes a mockery.
* * * *
Captain Wallington’s vice-regal secretary-
ships include Carington and Duff, Hopetoun and
Brassey, and now he takes Gov. Buxton, of S.A.,
under his wing. When he has given Brisbane
and Perth a turn, he will be an absolutely unique
authority on Australian society. The journeyman
private-secretary !

The appeal of a parent against his liability
for the support in a lunatic asylum of his insane
daughter, whose husband had become financially
crippled, on the ground that said husband alone
was liable, was dismissed with costs the other day
by the Sydney courts.

thought the bar loafers were fooling when they

How the “ Three ” fad rides N.S. Wales :
Three Railway Commissioners, three Land-and-In-
come-tax Commissioners, three Electoral Commis-
sioners, three Public Service Commissioners, and
various others, existing or extinct. Is it merely
Rory O’More’s “luck-in-odd-numbers” theory
that is at the bottom —though, then, one would fill
the bill? There were three Royal Commissioners
in the Dean case, and there is but one in the
Parkes Inquiry case, and it will be hard lines if
the latter doesn’t prove itself as a Commission as
wise as the former. There is but one Railway
Commissioner in Victoria and but one in Queens-
land, but N.S. Wales sticks happily to the magic
“ three.”. Why not, if there’s luck in the number,

* * * *
Revised definitions re exemptions from
“ habitual drunkenness,” by Williams, J. (Vic.):
“ The man who is sober five days in the week, and
drinks his wages on Saturdays and Sundays, is no
a habitual drunkard.” That is to say, the man
who keeps sober in order to earn wages so that
lie can get drunk till turn-to time again, is only an
intermittent drunkard. Let him get fair justice,
please. He is only an intermittent teetotaller.
He can’t help his inability to keep habitually
drunk. Corollary : Intermittent teetotallers are
the bone and sinew and mainstay of the country.
Can’t see the corollary, can’t you ? Well, it’s
there in ellipsis.
* * * *
The other day three people met on a Syd-
ney harbor ferry-boat. Two of them were a
handsome and elegantly-attired young couple who
had with them a yellow-leather handbag; the
other, a man who had no one to love him but a
large, fierce dog, also bore a yellow handbag—in it
he was carrying home to Lion aforesaid a not-
over-fresh calf’s heart which he had that day ac-
quired at the butcher’s for twopence. Also, having
taken a whisky or two he did not notice the slight
change of weight when, on leaving the boat, he
by mistake picked up the young couple’s bag and
carried it off instead of his own. On reaching
home he was duly welcomed by his expectant
hound, and on opening the bag to bring forth
Lion’s meal he found instead of ti.e calf’s heart—
a perfumed silk nightdress, a suit of silk-corded
pyjamas, and three pairs of delicate canary-colored
gloves. And that bright young pair to whom the
world was a poem—well, when they opened their
bag they of course found in it the smellful heart
of a young bullock that had gone before. Talk
about Disenchantment and the blistering satire of
Fact! Love’s trappings turned into two penn’orth
of dogsmeat!
* * * *
Outside Sydney Y.M.C.A. rooms (con-
trolled by an executive composed chiefly of Low
Church Anglicans and Methodist lay-preachers,
whose abomination is Rome and all her works)
appears an advt. in the shape of a cross. Why ?
By the way, a prominent “religious” organ,
inveighing against Ritualistic practices, spoke of
violet altar-cloths as “an old Pagan symbol for
Lent.” Now, did old Pagans trouble themselves
about Lent ? The same journal referred to the
sign of the cross as the “ brand of Cain.”
* * * *
A Sydney paper remarks that “ more prac-
tice before Judges only would certainly raise the
tone of the bar.” But just look at N.S. W. Equity
Court, where a Judge sits alone. Practice in that
court has distinctly lowered the tone of the bar—
the Equity Court has, in fact, come to be known
as “the whispering gallery.”
* *
In a down-South city there is a home for
Governesses. Recently young ladies called to
enquire terms for a friend —a country hospital
nurse—desirous of taking her holiday-fortnight in
the city. Said the manageress: “Our terms are
so much, and if the boarder does not attend
prayers the charge is a shilling more.” This,
surely, is inverting the order of things !
* * * *
Sydney Presbytery finds Rev. Geo. Hay
guilty— inter alia —of “ erecting a church within
the charge of Canterbury in opposition to the two
churches already existing therein.” The rival
Gospel-shops! “No connection with the alleged
soul-saver over the way ! ” Yet the parson says
he isn’t a tradesman !
* * * *
Since the latter part of last year post offices
throughout N.S.W. have been systematically
visited by burglars who leave no clues. Hillgrove,
whence .£139 was lifted, is the last up to date.
A little time back Katoomba was honored, also
with success. Somewhere about a dozen P.O’s
altogether have been levied on so far, and the
method in each case is practically the same, show-
ing an exact knowledge of departmental routine

* * * *
On Taranaki (M.L.) beach the other day
two wanderers picked up a lump of ambergris
(481 b.) which they were advised to send to Paris
addressed to Roger and Gallet, the perfumers.
By return mail they got a cheque for £3OOO.
Wagga corporation, The Bulletin hears,
pays the princely sum of five shillings per diem
for street-watering, the lucky recipient of the ss.
finding the man, horse and harness.
* * * *
The reports of cyclist “church parades”
in Melb. Monday morning papers are mainly ad-
vertising-lists thinly disguised. “Spiel, Bust,
and Scorcher rode Messrs. Crank and Pearl’s
triplet,” &c., &c. “The Boom Co.’s quad was
steered by Spoke, Tyre, Sprint, and Boliver,”and
so on. The riders in the church parade of the
future will carry sandwich-boards, and the pre-
siding parson will point out that Jones’ Diamond
bike is breaking all the records in the New
Jerusalem. The bike may yet endow the church
with a real avocation.
A man lately stopped the mail-coach
between Mitchell and Augathella, shot the horses
and robbed the mail-bags. For this offence the
maximum penalty is penal servitude for life.
Sentence —two years with hard labor —suspended
under the First Offenders’ Probation Act. Judge
Cooper did it.

* * * *
A South Melbourne sand-carter the other
day sued his boss for a fortnight’s wages.
He got it. Fourteen shillings was the sum
claimed—ls. 2d. a day for ten hours’ work. The
boss gets much abuse even from papers that justi-
fied Melbourne City Council in sweating its
draymen.

wrote soothingly to her deserted hubby—“ lam
very, very happy, though wicked.” Why, “hap-
piness is wickedness! ”
The age of consent, which a score of persons
declare is going to be the great Parliamentary
question of the near future, stands at 14 in N.S.
Wales, at 16 in England, and at 18 in most of the
United States.

Q. blacks now much prefer “ smokum
charcoal ” (opium) to “ eatum bottle ” (rum).
* * * *
Maoriland Department of Labor has or-
dered that all women and girls employed in fac-
tories must either wear their hair cut short or have
it closely fastened up. This is the result of a
recent accident in a Christchurch boot-factory. A
young girl was working near a moving machine
when her loose hair caught in the belting and she
was hoisted aloft. Then her scalp came right off,
and she dropped. The description of how she
looked afterwards isn’t a nice thing to read.
A Berlin physician attri-
butes the English gout-rate
(fixed at 10 per cent.) to an exces-
sive consumption of meat. Fool-
ish German physician ! Gout is
very prevalent among jobbing-
warehouse porters, cab and ’bus
drivers, and all sorts of men
who pick up a precarious living
in big cities, and are liable to be
treated to drinks. The eternal
“’arf-a-pint of hold hale” —Bur-
ton ale, when they can get it —is
the finest gout-producer in the
world, says local medical exper-
ience. It gives the complaint
to thousands of men who cer-
tainly can’t be suffering from
too much meat. Besides,
Australia isn’t a gout-ridden
country. What do Australian doctors say ?

The University lodge of Freemasons
applied to Melb. University Council the other
day for the use of the Wilson hall. The govern-
ing body refused—but only by a majority of one.

“The way to reform woman’s dress is to
cut 10 inches off the skirt. Think of how many
hundreds of tons of mud-bedraggled drygoods
would then drop from the overweighted hips of
womanhood.”— North American Review.
» * * *
The latest S.A. divorce-case, which kept
Adelaide simmering for a fortnight, is another
instance of the futility of going to law. The
petitioner owned the freehold of an Adelaide pub.
and wanted a divorce. Well, the jury disagreed,
and the law-costs have divorced him from his pub.
“IF NOW, NOT HEREAFTER.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When we were young we used to hear
That whoso liked his pint of beer,
And loved his pipe o’ baccy well,
Was booked, by broad-gauge line, to H 1!
But now (O tempora, O mores !)
We don’t believe such silly stories,
And this new parson’s smoking whim
Shows that ive have converted him !
So let each swagman stow his swag
And haste to smoke the parson’s shag,
And hear him preach, ’mid cheers and laughter,
“Those who smoke now won’t smoke hereafter!”
O.O.O.
MY GIRL BEHIND THE BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My heart’s a-whirl —a handsome girl
As Juno large and ox-eyed!
Her eyes are bright; her teeth are white ;
Her hair’s a plain peroxide ;
Still, spite of that, she is my star,
My Girl Behind the Bar !
When I’ve no “ chinks,” she’ll stand me drinks,
And quite forget to “ slate ” ’em;
If Boss in ire for those enquire,
She’ll say demurely, lovely liar,
“ The rats, sir, must have ate ’em !”
With Boniface she loves to spar,
My Girl Behind the Bar !
All poets good will say I should
Straight wed this buxom beauty,
And swear all day, as poets may,
Such is my bounden duty ;
But I’ve a wife who’s still in life —
From cemeteries far —
So, being wise, won’t bigamise
My Girl Behind the Bar !

Slab from the leading-columns of the ultra-
respectable Melb. Age, referring to a certain his-
torical Sydney Pleasant-Sunday afternoon :
Artists will paint the nude probably so long as men are
men, and women are to be found to sit as models to them.
But, after all, this sort of art is often very much akin to
the salacities of the brothel, and the Artist who cannot
make a name for himself without resorting to it goes
perilously near to bringing his profession into disrepute
with that large and influential class of people who still
cultivate the decencies of life, and have not wholly fore-
sworn the virtues of drapery. Iloni soit qui rnal y pense
may sound very well in the lips of a courtier,' but is
altogether too superfine for the common clay of humanity
to live up to it.
In a word, Titian, Correggio, Reynolds and all the
company of the great paint-spreaders down to
Leighton and Burne-Jones have brought their
profession into disrepute, according to the Age
Grundy!

Sydney, August 19, 1896.
Mv Dear Moorabinda—
Sydney Govt. House revelled in a dinner-party
and at-home last Thursday, when the guests were
not quite so suggestive of a crowd at a pupils’ in-
vitation concert as on a former occasion. Some-
how, whenever I hear of V.R. festivities I always
think of the Sydney woman v at a V.R. shivoo
whom somebody stupidly asked “ if she was enjoy-
ing herself ? ” With a look of withering scorn at
her green interlocutor she severely replied : “One
does not come to Govt. House to enjoy oneself,
Mrs. Blank! ”
Music occasionally amuses herself with violent
contrasts. On boy-pianist Mark Hambourg’s
heels comes four-score De Kontski, whose matinee
at Sydney Town Hall last Wednesday was flatter-
ingly attended by the usual matinee women, who
seem to be at last learning to applaud, and if they
don’t always do so at the right moment—well,
nobody, not even a woman, can do all things at
once. At the same time it hardly flattered the
composer when he played his delightful arrange-
ment of Gounod’s “ Faust ” and paused an instant
after a dewy fantasia on “ Let Me Gaze ” to have
the audience come howling down on him as if it
was the finish. It was such a disconcerting proof
to the artist of how unfamiliar he was as a com-
poser in Australia. The Chevalier,, with his
skull-cap and eerie white hair, only wants a
gaberdine and the devil coming down the chimney
to be the copy of old Faust in the opera.
His playing is full of moods, and his reading
of the Moonlight Sonata was a passionate revela-
tion after Halle’s cold and analytical performance.
I was amused, at the De Kontski matinee, by the
shoals of school-girls escorted thither to get tips
how to play the old masters. It looks well—rows
of green piano-bangers, with stereotyped back-hair
combed smooth like a pet mare’s tail, struggling
desperately to look as if they were appreciating a
sonata, though their personal studies haven’t pro-
bably got far beyond “ The Merry Peasant.”
De Kontski is indeed a marvellous old man at
80 years. He (who studied under Beethoven before
the first Napoleon died !) plays with the manual
dexterity of a boy and arranges professional trips
through Siberia—and talks of what he will do when,
he comes back to Australia later on. On being
introduced to the most utter stranger he rises,
bows profoundly, and wrings his new acquaint-
ance’s hand with the energy of adolescence. And
he is never without a strong cigar in his mouth, at
which he puffs locomotive fashion. “To puff like
that at a cigar of that sort,” said a doctor, the
other day, “ would in nine young men out of ten
produce heart-failure in 20 minutes.”
Mrs. Gilbert Wilson was the only other artist
at the De Kontski concerts. Her voice is pure,
sweet and sympathetic. I liked it—but that
doesn t bind me to approve of the matinee hat she
wore -white and black feathers, like the trim-
mings on a spinster’s hearse. By the way, I must
confess that Beethoven’s only living pupil doesn’t
give his old preceptor much room on the pro-
gramme. Two numbers of L. Von B. were equally
balanced by two of De Kontski and one piece of
Chopin and Mendelssohn !
A misprint occurred in the programme of Satur-
day’s numerously-attended De Kontski concert at
the Town Hall. The Chevalier was put down to'
play an Andante from the fifth symphony, appar-
ently composed by himself. He played, however,
the “ Andante con moto ” from Beethoven’s fifth
symphony—but one of the musical critics says De
Kontski played a melodious Andante from his own
symphony !
A well-known meteorologist carries with him
his bad luck at running the weather even when he
travels incog, on a holiday. He was staying not
long ago in a place where he was personally un-
known, and undertook the weather for the other
boarders—two youths. “ Take your coats to-day,”
he said, “and your umbrellas. It will be cold
and rainy. ” The youths returned late that even-
ing caked in dust and perspiration. “No occasion
for wraps to-day,” was his next ukase, and there
is something so beguiling in another person’s
belief that the once-bitten youths started out
coatless —and got back with something like frost.
bite and influenza. But as the weather-maker
proceeded to advise straw fans and cigarettes as
the proper guise for the next day, one of the
youths struck. “ Look here,” he said, calmly,
“ it’s kind of you to take the trouble, but really
your weather-tips are so consistently wrong that
you might be the Govt, astronomer himself ! ”
At the Sydney Town-hall grand charity concert,
the other afternoon, the most memorable item was
the appearance of Herr Staedtgen, the new Ger-
man baritone, who warbles hotly and fiercely in
the subtle language of the German sausage, and
reaches your hardened emotions by the honesty of
his sentiment and his unselfish determination to
squeeze every possible effect out of each of his
numbers. If the Herr didn’t walk on and off as
stiffly as Lauri’s automaton Hamlet, and accentu-
ate the similarity by wearing a pair of large white
gloves (the worst form at present on the English
concert-stage), he would be an unmixed blessing
to Sydney concert-programmes. Miss Rochester
also made her debut. She has a music-hall way of
jerking the melody at you, and she needs a lot of
sandpapering ; but her mezzo-soprano is very big
and assertive, and is the sort of bold, dashing
voice that one generally finds successfully asso-
ciated with pale pink tights and a scientific wink.
The other debutante, Miss Loveday, evidently
hadn’t quite made up her mind whether she was a
basso-profundo or a falsetto, and gave the audience
a taste of each in her badly-phrased rendering of
“Alas, those Chimes.”
One thing you cannot cavil at in pretty Mrs.
Brown-Potter’s reappearance at Sydney Lyceum—
you cannot say she makes the same fetish of her
wardrobe as heretofore, nor does she star Worth
(of Paris) on her programmes any more. Perhaps
the reason of this is that Mrs. B. P. plays “ La
Tosca ” in the same garments as of yore, so far as
cut and color go, so further comment on who
made ’em would be superfluous. It’s an eloquent
comment on how rakish and eccentric everyday
modes have become that La Tosca’s Grecian
draperies do not look half so impossibly pretty as
they used to. Even her first big hat with the
blossoms hung round the edge of the brim (as I
have seen fishermen hang bits of bait), and a thing
like a lyrebird’s tail stioking out of the top, was
merely a replica of what we shut our eyes to as we
pass it on the Block every day.
Mrs. Potter’s chiefest frock in “ Tosca ” is black
jetted net with a heavy foot border of gold bullion,
pink puff sleeves well off the shoulder, the whole
thing finished off with a rope of pink roses (as if
the lady had tried to hang herself with a floral
cord which had broken at the critical moment) and
ropes of pearls, the richest touch being a curling
black feather arising at the back of the head
like the thing the Mikado wears in the operetta.
Pretty Ida Hamilton (Mrs. B. P. will never get a
more physically-similar understudy) played the
Princess in a pair of dimpled white shoulders
principally, though I believe there was something
of a lilac train and a frock the color of gooseberry-
fool —however, few got beyond the shoulders. In
the 3rd act, Mrs. Potter wore the same artistic
Srey woollen in which she looks so ingenuous—
er cloak of spring green woollen at first
suggesting a component part of the gown.
The “La Tosca” people are all well
frocked in Act 2, and the color scheme
is good, and the only people who hurt me were a
superess in a flowing red robe, as if she was in
training as a cardinal; and the Queen. But the
latter can’t grumble—Queens are always scare-
crows in stageland. I never yet saw majesty
behind the floats but it suggested that somebody
Fad dressed it up in velvet-pile tablecloths.
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society make more
noise at Sydney Town Hall next Wednesday
(26th), in the usual “ grand concert,” with Signor
Hazon in the birdcage, and his well-drilled forces
around him. By the way, it is significant of the
soaring ambition of the S.A.O.S. that their con-
certs are always “grand.” Some day, for a
novelty, they ought to advertise “ a small, mean
concert,” just to show a meek and lowly spirit.
The Sydney University Dramatic Society—another
clever band of amateurs—put up a comedy, “The
Heir at Law,’ at their stock theatre —Sydney
Gaiety—last Monday evening. I was sorry to
miss seeing the piece. One can considerably en-
large one’s acquaintance with the little efforts of
rococo dramatists by carefully keeping a finger on
the S.A.D.S., who are never guilty of staging com-
monplaces.
Good news for the Sydney frockists ! The social
column of a daily newspaper informs us that at a
recent Sydney wedding the bride’s gorgeous white
satin and brocade was made in Shanghai by a
Chinese tailor! Ah Sin, I suppose, perpetrated
this masterpiece from patterns provided—but it
just occurs to me that “John,” as a ladies’ tailor,
“ makee bodicee fitee likee waxee,” would have a
far more thrilling profession, from John’s own
point of view, than that of picking slugs off cauli-
flowers.
Apropos weddings—the announcement of a
Maoriland marriage read as follows in a Dunedin
t(M.L.) newspaper:
MARRIAGE.
Jeffery — Kerns. —On the 2nd. inst., at St. Joseph’s
Cathedral, Dunedin, by the Very Rev. P. Lynch, G.
C. Jeffery to Anna Kerns. R.I.P.
There is one large and prosperous Sydney hard-
ware-mercbant-CMm-dashing-militaire who appar-
ently means to break Methuselah’s record for
longevity. At a recent shivoo in connection with
the firm the tin-poc warrior rose and remarked
that he was happy to see many elders there that
night who came to the firm from school, and he
hoped to see their children and their children’s
children among the army of employees in the
future. “ He hoped to see ” is heroic. It only
shows, though, how little danger a N.S.W. mili-
tary character anticipates from the pursuit of his
gory pastime when he blandly talks about out-
living two generations of peaceful citizens who are
exposed to no more dreadful danger than that of
falling over something in the hardware depart-
ment.
Speaking of the fierce ironmonger who seeks the
bubble reputation, &c., I notice that Colonel
Lassetter got off a huge advertisement at a
recent R.C. meeting at Sydney Town Hall
—though I am sure he didn’t mean any-
thing like that. “ Speaking as a business
man ” (not as a soldier, you observe), “ I have
never had to discharge a member of the R.C.
Church for dishonesty or neglect of duty,” said
this truly sagacious person. Well, what did he
discharge him for? Perhaps he didn t discharge
him at all, for the gallant Colonel appears not to
have specifically stated that he had any R.C.’s
in his employ. Had he not been enthusiastic
and off his guard, he certainly would have con-
cluded by stating that he was now selling brass
bedsteads at 10 per cent, less than any other house
in the city, or have handed down a heap of price-
lists to be circulated in the meeting—for, after
that lovely advt., the Church is bound to rush the
firm for its ironmongery, and there is nothing like
striking the Holy Roman while he is hot.
What sermons might not be preached from the
“society ’’-paper formula, “Miss Smith looked
nice, as usual,” and from the frantic endeavors of
Miss Smith aforesaid to look nice ! A Mrs.
Rheese, of Sydney, had been swimming in the
sun, which afflicted her with some freckles on the
arms and shoulders, and as Mrs. Rheese “ wanted
to go to the theatre that week ” she contracted
with a Sydney toilet co. for the removal of said
freckles, which were “ very few and very small,”
for a fee of three guineas. “Madame P. Davis,”
of the toilet co., who knew “ nothing of drugs, but
had had some lessons in face-massage from a lady
in Victoria,” thereupon proceeded to make Mrs.
Rheese’s shoulders beautiful by means of a per-
chloride of mercury (corrosive-sublimate) lotion
which brought her to death’s door. Mrs. Rheese,
plucked from the tomb by a doctor, sued for £3OO
damages and got £3l. Better co bear your freckles
than chance shedding your skin and teeth.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The girls of the Nat Goodwin Co. are voted
socially a success. Most of them are ‘ real cute ’in
private life, gifted with that power of ‘ back-talk ’
which the benighted Sydney Ducks (all Austra-
lians are ‘ Sydney Ducks ’ to Yankees) have been
taught to expect from fair Columbians, but have
hitherto looked for in vain. They have some new
slang. Johnnies they call ‘ Willieboys ’; ‘ I don’t
start’ means ‘l’m not on.’ What a Melb. girl
gushes over as too sweet for words, the Melican
gal proclaims ‘out of sight.’
“ The Shade of W. Saurin Lyster of yore seemed
to hover over the Russell-st. Hall on Tuesday
night, when all the opera-singers who have ever
come to Melb. and stayed there arose from their
silence and anticipated the past. It was a Dante
evening and so the company all played at being in
Malebolge. Wasn’t that the place where they
were all stuck head-down wards in ice? Things
were cold and frozen as though the ice-cream trade
had eaten into our very vitals. And when Gov.
Brassey and suite and assorted guests were led
forth to refreshments into an adjoining room, the
vice-regal name only seemed to suggest the spoons
which flavor the penny ice of commerce. There
was a funny printer’s error on the programme;
the last item was ‘ Prose in Egitto ; ’ after a ‘ dis-
corso ’ in Italian from Consul Corte, and a
‘ discorso’ in English from Jas. Smith, and a ‘few
remarks ’ from the Gov., the word fitted in well.
An Italian Royalty recently here fathered the
‘ Societa ’; local ‘ Sassiety ’ immediately took
to patronising Dante, rather a grim old personage
to be the presiding genius of a fashionable muffin-
worry, by the way ; they were all there. But one
familiar face was missing. Where, oh where, was
Ugo Catani ?
“The getters-up of charity performances in
Melb. are beginning to inquire : does it pay to ask
for the presence of Lord and Lady Brassey. It
means a guinea bouquet always for the lady, and
the free allotment of several of the best seats, on
the chance of the public coming to view the
honored guests. Lately the public haven’t turned
out in paying proportions. Lord Hopetoun didn’t
like concerts, but when he gave his patronage he
always sent a cheque. Lord Brassey gives his
presence without the cheque.
“ Lady Brassey and the Guv’mentouse party
were cycling around Caulfield the other Saturday,
and on their return journey had the ill-luck to
strike a van-load 'bf junior football barrackers as
they turned into Chapel-street, Prahran. The
opportunity was right into the hands of the
assembled larrikins, and after the humorists of the
party had exhausted their stock of Rabelaisian
wit, the whole thirty or so started with a wild
whoop ‘ Since Mary Ann has learned to ride a
bicycle,’ bawling the classic ditty for a couple of
miles. The perspiring cyclists endeavored to pass
and leave the van, but the driver was as tricky as
scorcher Martin, and carried too many tactics to
permit of such a move. Ultimately the whole
party decided to turn from the smooth tram-track
to a newly-metalled side-street, which they did
with the words of the ‘Mary Ann Cycle’ song
echoing in their flaming ears.
“For the first time on record, the Mayor of
Melb. has been left out of the invitation-list to
the Governor’s ball, and great is the indignation
of the ultra-loyal circle of Melb. City Council.
“ The allegation of smuggling directed by De-
tective Christie at certain ‘ swagger ’ naval officers
is regarded as a horrid shame in Toorak circles,
which have grown more solidly Freetrade than
ever since the charges were made. If these be
upheld, some light may be cast on the mystery of
how the haw tv youths manage to maintain such
style on the £1 15s. a week and keep at which a
paternal Government assesses their worth.
