Miss Hilda Cumming, of Toorak, at present in
England with her sister, Mrs. Rupert Clarke.
Mr. Ritchie, the bridegroom-elect, is the son of a
medico, and was formerly a familiar figure in
Melb. society.
“ America must look to her laurels now that
Australia enters the lists as a purveyer of pro-
fessional beauties. A very handsome matron
went ’Ome not so very long ago with the avowed
intention of ‘catching the Prince of Wales’eye.’
Letters from the Big Smoke testify that the am-
bitious lady’s house and carriage are worthy of
her charms, and that her opera-box is next but
one to Tummy’s own. At such short range, the
bejewelled brunette beauty should certainly score
a bull’s-eye straight away.
“ Melbourne society pets lady doctors, and at a
recent big at-home there were five of them on
show as the lions of the occasion, lined up in a row
beside the hostess. On the other hand, from
grudging male coadjutors the poor girls get more
kicks than ha’pence. In the dining-hall of a cer-
tain medical institute, for instance, the he-sur-
geons, ’tis said, refused to carve for their fair
colleagues, who, in consequence, must advance
upon the joint and operate ‘on their own * or go
dinnerless.
“Governor Brassey still shudders when he hears
of the big florists’ bills paid by the Hopetouns.
For his part he holds that home-grown blossoms
should suffice for ordinary occasions, and he may
often be seen at an unpretentious establishment
choosing the household fruit. Of course he has
an expensive progeny; so much is expected of a
gilded patriarch. The other day, when pretty
Mrs. Freeman-Thomas came of age, she got
£BO,OOO in money and diamonds, her share pro.
tern, of the family spoil. She is the youngest of
the first Lady Brassey’s children, but there’s
another family to be provided for now.”
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
SPORTING NOTIONS .
Apropos the row between the professionals and
the Surrey committee, let The Bulletin put it from the
pros.’ standpoint. Richardson, the great bowler of his
kind in England, being a pro. receives £lO (per match,
not per day) as his salary for big matches, and £5 for
county matches. An alleged amateur like Grace receives,
say, £2O for “expenses.” Further, the pro. is treated as
a pro. : has to dress in a separate room, dine at a separate
table, and in other ways be reminded of his servility,
whilst Grace and the other alleged amateurs who live
on the game are “ esquired” and placed alongside royalty
at dinner. In theory Grace is a doctor practising his
profession ; in reality he is as much a pro. as Richardson,
seeing that he plays for every county engagement for
Gloucester and is always available for big matches. Rich-
ardson and his brother pros., in going on strike, once
more draw attention to the utter hypocrisy of the English
amateur and professional system.
* « * •
Sympathisers with the professional “strikers,”
Richardson, Abel, and Hayward, must have been sorry to
observe that the two bowlers had 39 runs knocked off
them for no wickets. The match was won by the bowlers
who didn’t strike, but in batting the three malcontents
came out well with a score of 71 for four wickets, Richard-
son carrying his bat each time, whereas Peel, Lilley and
Hearne made only 17 among them.
* * • •
Captain Trott was clearly responsible for some
of the 66 runs by which All-England won the test-match.
He allowed Giffen to be punished to the tune of 64 runs
for two wickets when he might have done much better
with M‘Kibbin. Mac’s two wickets for the first innings
cost only 21 runs. Grace didn’t make so much mistake
with Richardson, for the tall fellow was taken off for good
and all when he had given Australia 22 runs and about
eight sundries. All-England, by the way, scored 75 more
off the bat than the other men, who had 14 sundries—as
against three—in their grand total of 163.
* * * *
London Referee struck the weak point in
Prince Ranji’s claim to play for All England by suggesting
the possibility of several 'Ranjis blossoming at one time
for England or Australia. A whole team of Ranjis, for
instance, would be an absurdity in either case. They
wouldn’t represent England or Australia. Therefore one
Ranji playing for either country against the other in a re-
presentative match is an absurdity.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Let us criticise the Australian
Eleven’s record from an English barracker’s standpoint.
The seven strongest teams matched against them have
been Lord Sheffield’s, Midland Counties (which was cer-
tainly stronger than the scratch lot of Players, or North
of England), M.C.C. (twice), and All England (thrice). Of
these seven games Australia has captured one and practi-
cally lost six, although two of them were draws. The
Australian performances in London, up to time of writing,
are three defeats, one draw, which saved defeat, and one
win against Surrey. And they have made three collapses
in London for iB, 53, and 44 respectively.— Rubsino
Faxin.
* * * »
Three batsmen ran a dead heat for highest
aggregate score in the test-match —Darling, Abel and
Jackson, each 47. Nine All-England scores touched
double figures, and three men got “ducks.” For Aus-
tralia, there were five double-figure scores and six
“ ducks.” Peel was the only man who bagged a pair of
birds.
* * * *
The third test-match proved nothing, except
that two English bowlers on a soft wicket were able to
beat the performances of the most successful Australian
bowler —which is a very unusual, if not unprecedented,
occurrence. Perhaps Ail-England won the match through
winning the toss, and starting its first innings on a
wicket that was rather too wet for the bow'lers; but, on
the other hand, it seems that the Australians started their
first innings under fairly easy run-making conditions.
The ground then would remain good for awhile after
being rolled. Anyway, Iredale and Darling scored 76
runs before Iredale ran himself out. From that time for-
ward there was no considerable stand made on either side,
but the Englishmen did not fail so steadily and persist-
ently as the Australians. Here are the figures :
On either side the last men in were able to make a few
runs. Australia suffered two “run-outs,” and one of
these (Iredale’s) probably represented a loss of several pips
to the total. Abel was the only man to bat well at both
attempts.

An English paper says Varney Parkes was
called Varney because Parkes was reading Scott’s
“ Kenilworth ” when “ the baby came.”
Naturalist A. J. Campbell, of Vic. Cus-
toms, possesses eggs of 500 different species of
Australian birds, having completed his fifth cen-
tury a few days ago.
Redmond (a cousin of thePamellite leader),
now on S.A. Advertiser, was the first reporter to
interview Dr. Jameson on the field of Krugersdorp,
after the Rand fiasco.
General Kitchener, sirdar of the Egyptian
forces, is a son of a M.L. settler, 001. Kitchener,
who settled in Otago in ’66. The family still owns
a large station in the Shag Valley.
‘ ‘ The advantages of being a consul in Aus-
tralia,” writes One of Them, “consist in wearing
a gorgeous uniform and being permitted to sub-
scribe for the burial of indigent consuls.”
A Melb. paper pourtrays the 40 men who
constitute the council of the local United Masonic
Grand Lodge. Only Brassey, Clarke, and
Premier Turner are known to the outside world.
How different from aforetime !
Noel Buxton, a younger son of the brewer-
Governor, is the great apostle of the Gothenburg
System in S. A. In the Licensed Victuallers Bill
now before Parliament there is a clause authorising
its adoption at Renmark irrigation settlement.
Prendergast, M.P. (Vic.), resided in
Sydney for some time, employed as a comp, on
Sydney D.T. He was secretary of the Typo.
Society, played the ’cello, and practised boxing,
all excellent preparations for the higher field of
politics.
Frederick Acheson, shot at Louth (N.S.W.)
by a woman of whose land he was taking posses-
sion under legal process, was last week taken to
Sydney Hospital in the hope that the bullet which
had entered his thigh through the groin might be
located. This was accomplished per the beneficent
medium of the Rontgen ray process, operated by
Mr. Schmidlin, the Elizabeth-street electrician.
The result was an entire and gratifying success.
The jiortion of the thigh bone “ shadowgraphed ”
came out clearly, and the bullet was also very
distinct. Dr. Bowker removed it next day with-
out difficulty.
So far as is known, every man
whose head was damaged in the
Brunswick (Melb.) orange-and-green
riot was a Catholic, and most of them
were named O’ something or other.
Yet the Dutch Billiam crowd is still
holding forth about the violence and
brutal intolerance of the other lot—
which other lot is mostly getting its
skull repaired at the hospital.
Apropos Henniker Heaton’s state-
ment that Parkes “ hesitated to speak
to persons unknown to him —that it
was a sheer waste of time.” A cer-
tain Sydneyite remembers travelling
in the Balmain tram in the same com-
partment as the veteran. Another
passenger, after some effort, managed
to attract Parkes’ attention and “passed
the time of day.” “Hidon’t think Hi
know you,” was the response. “ Oh, I
am Mr. X.—l live next door to you.”
“ H indeed,” was the chilling reply.
A pause—then the passenger made
another attempt. “Do you think the
Reid Government will last, Sir Henry ?”
“Hi don’t think they '"ill last for
her or, Mr. X. ! ”
H. H. Champion has taken up
seriously the business of knight erran-
try, ami has already rescued several
damsels in distress. One of these, who
was aim st s a wing, and was refused
help by the father of her illegitimate
ohild, told her woes to H.H., who
promptly put the case into the hands
o a sodoitor, who quite as promptly
got an order against the father, who
even more promptly “ cleared ” to
another province. H.H. then showed
that he was a practical philanthropist
by obtaining employment for the girl,
and she and her child are now com-
paratively comfortable. The Bulletin
admires Champion’s generosity, but
fears that he will find himself every
morning become the involuntary bailee
of at least six bundles, of baby, and that
soon he will have to turn his office into
a foundling-asylum.
The Bartlett who married Baroness Bur-
dett-Coutts fifteen years ago, celebrated his forty-
third birthday recently. And the old lady still
lives, a healthy octogenarian ! Meanwhile the
other girl—if there was one —has probably died of
deferred hope.

can theosophist marriage of the pair affianced
aeons ago is the story that the event was hastened

re-incarnation of their lately-dead Pope, William
Q. Judge himself.
As the Ophir left Sydney the other day,
one of the sights on board was “Jawbone ” Neild,
lately appointed to the command of the St.
George’s Rifles. The gallant officer, by the way,
has found time to depart for England without yet
having attended a drill with his regiment. Never-
theless, the luggage was all branded in large letters
with the poet-warrior's military title. The majah
in the awkward squad !
N.S.W. newspaper church-intelligence is
degenerating more and more : “ Rev. Robert Steel,
of Queanbeyan, has lost his old horse, Mealy, which
in the course of its life caried its reverend master
60,000 miles,” and “ Rev. Edward Davidson, dur-
ing the last 10 years, slept in 530 different beds.
Failing sufficient cash to provide for a
statue of J. B. Patterson, the defunct and practic-
ally forgotten Vic. politician, the committee has
decided to spend the money on a drinking foun-
tain to be set up in Castlemaine. A drinking
fountain in Castlemaine —the place of breweries !
WHAT IT MUST COME TO.
“I don’t know what particular effect the bicycle may have upon the conformation of posterity.
It seems to me it may produce a race of beings of a Z-like shape.”— The Earl of Rosebery.
Counsel’s opinion was lately sought by a
Sydney journalist as to whether an action for
slander or defamatory libel can be successfully
maintained against a local medical man who
referred to him at a public meeting as “a colonial
W. T. Stead.” Counsel is considering the knotty
point.
Missionary Joseph King marvels why no
“ permanent memorial” has been raised in Sydney
to the saintly convict-parson Samuel Marsden.
This is the amiable old gentleman who, when sit-
ting as a magistrate at Parramatta, used to order
men accused of stealing to be flogged until they
confessed.
At the conclusion of Melb. Opera House
performance the other night, a reputed Victorian
millionaire was observed to carefully raise the mat
at the foot of the circle steps and produce half a
cigar which he had frugally hidden there at eight
o’clock. Yet that man only demands one extra
vote for Thrift.

match with Martin.

“ F.” writes from Melbourne :
Dear Bulletin,—A few weeks ago, you allowed a cor-
respondent to splash whitewash over lawyer Geake, the
fraudulent Daylesford trustee who was quietly sneaking
from justice when The Bulletin called attention to his
figure in the distance. Your correspondent described
Geake as a generous, lovable sort of person, incapable of
canting hypocrisy, and I would now like to ask him (your
correspondent) where he picked up his picture of the miss-
ing attorney. Geake, like all “respectable” embezzlers,
from Jabez Balfour downward, was a loathsome hypocrite.
Upon receiving cash for investment, he would invite the
dupe to join him in a prayer for God’s blessing on the
transaction. Clients have knelt beside him in his office.
A widow lady, who entrusted her property to Geake’s
care, tells me that he several times persuaded her against
withdrawing any principal, but he was very punctual with
the interest, a cheque for which was invariably enclosed
along with a tract. Geake was a large distributor of
tracts and a wholesale dealer in Bible texts. It doesn’t
seem kind to hurl the fellow’s mock-piety at him now
that he is down, but plain truth is better than ornamental
fiction, therefore I write to contradict the yarn that Geake
was not a hypocrite. The world cannot be too often in-
formed that every business-man who makes open profes-
sion of piety is a highly probable Barabbas.
Hancock, M.P., Trenwith’s locum-tenens
as Vic. Labor leader, spends Pleasant Sunday
Afternoons preaching against the idea of opening
the National Gallery and the Public Libraries on
the blessed Sabbath. John is in favor of six days
labor for hi* fellow workers, and solitary confine-
ment on the seventh.
The untimely death of Kitz in Melb. the
other day removes the crack Australian wine-
expert of his city. The Kitzes were almost
pioneers in the endeavor to popularise native
wines. They had their reward, to be sure : Kitz
winged his way to the empyrean from one of the
most luxurious homes in the Melb. suburbs.
Your Kitz is the necessary complement of the
Castellas, Fallons, Morrises, and other vignerons,
the beneficent middle-man with whom nobody
quarrels.
Barlow, associate successively to Judges
Kerferd, Higinbotham and Madden, has been
sedulously collecting persiflage, tags, anecdotes,
and reminiscences of the Vic. Bench, Bar, jury-
room, and dock during his long court career.
When he publishes, some interesting matter may
be confidently expected.
Out of the £300,000 netted by the divi-
dendaires of Mt. Morgan last year, that soulless
corporation disgorged in donations the magnificent
sum of £3O. “ The Mountmorganaires,” remarked
a Sydneyite the other day, “ are a noble lot. Their
chief characteristic is that almost every man
Jack of them is a Johnny Woodser.”
Mr. Cheke, one of the Vic. Supreme Court
taxing-officers, is a relative of the late N.S.W.
Judge Cheke and a lineal descendant of the
Cheke who was famous for something or other
(there’s a book about it somewhere) in Bloody
Mary’s reign. (She was some sort of a queen in a
place called England, or some such name.)
Thomas Walker, ex-M.L.A. (N.S.W.), is
on the avid look-out for a seat in Maoriland House
of Representatives. Sydney’s one-time Free-
thought lecturer Collins is now a member of that
advanced body. Also, poor pastor Laseron, who
still has in him an unlocated bullet from Tommy
Walker’s revolver, thinks of appealing to the
Rontgen rays.
M‘Millan, M.P., who introduced a depu-
tation to Premier Reid last week, for the purpose
of pressing on the Government the duty of ex-
tending the railway through the city of Sydney,
is related to the James Macarthur who headed
the first deputation that appealed to the Governor
to direct the laying of the first N.S.W. railway
line, fifty years ago.
Ed. Bulletin, —Be the question “ whether
a sheriff would be received into decent society did
the exigencies of his office at any time demand
that he should himself perform an execution,” it
should be remembered that President Cleveland,
the tin-god of the Australian Freetrade party,
once turned a couple of men off when acting in
sheriff’s capacity.—M.G.
The Duke of York is not coming to Aus-
tralia. After what has of late happened in the
British royal family no sane person really ever
thought he would come. The equerry who was inter-
viewed on the subject by the London correspond-
ent of S.A. Advertiser, remarked, apropos the
printed statement that the Royal couple were
coming out in the fast crusier Blenheim, that that
vessel had only the other day served as Prince
Henry of Battenberg’s hearse, and that it was un-
likely the deceased’s nephew and niece would use
it so soon after for a pleasure cruise. Truly a
delicate distinction to be drawn on behalf of the
Duke, who married his elder brother’s fiancee
only a few months after that brother’s death.
Walter Besant, as an ennobbled Britisher,
seems now to regret that he betrayed a Republican
hoof at prophet Stead’s recent London meeting.
Besant prophesied that Australia (amongst other
cited British dependencies) would become a
“ great nation,” that it won’t “continue for any
great length of time the fiction of dependence ” on
England, and that its (at present merely practical)
Republicanism will announce itself in fact and
law. Per contra, as an English ennobblee, he
assumed that monarchism would strengthen in
England. This means that Mr. Besant couldn’t
help himself. In the abandon of oratory, the
logic of Democratic insight ran away with him;
when he remembered the monarchial handle to his
name, and his acceptance of the Queen’s menial
livery, he was bound to justify his feudal sub-
servience.
The poet- warrior-politician Neild’s some-
what sudden departure for England has more
than roving restlessness as a cause. Jawbone
designs, it is said, a raid on the English Bar,
and hopes to carry back with him to Australia a
wig and gown. It is, putting all things together,
ever so much easier for an Australian to get eal.ed
to the Bar in England than it is in Australia.
Judge Hartley Williams, who retires from
the Vic. Bench next year (and will probably be
succeeded by Equity-barrister Higgins) is in poli-
tics a Radical, and in religion a pronounced
Rationalist, of the type of Chief Justice Higin-
botham and Dr. Pearson. Of his colleagues on
the Bench, four—Hood, A’Beckett, Hodges and
Holroyd —are Anglican, and Madden, C.J., is by
education a Roman Catholic, .and by practice
nothing in particular.
A Sydney historical writer has obtained
through a London correspondent documentary
proof that an immediate ancestress of Governor
Bligh went mad. It is not generally known that
one of Bligh’s descendants became insane, and it
was a common statement, referring to the Gover-
nor, in Sydney during the period immediately
preceding the Rum Rebellion, “Oh, he’s mad !
An interesting side-light on a stormy epoch of
Australian history.
Leaving one night in the “ seventies ’ the
cottage in Hunter-street, Sydney, where were held
the meetings of the debating society in _ which so
many N.S.W. public men developed their gaseous
faculties, G. H. Reid said to a companion: “I
have never read five books in my life.” Later m
the conversation he remarked : “ I would like to
be Premier of the colony—that is my ambition.”
His companion threw at him sententiously Car-
lyle’s generalisation that “ One’s wishes are pre-
sentiments of one’s achievements.” That man
evidently confirmed the aspirations of G.H.R.—
who has still the same incapacity for reading books.
“Robertson, who sent the letter the
other day to Madden, C.J., protesting against
that much-over-ruled dignitary adjudicating on
the Commercial Bank reconstruction scheme on
the ground that he is not a ‘ gold-expert ’ is,”
writes a Bulletin contributor, “ quite an original
in his way. He was an inspector in Vic. State
schools at a snug salary, but, finding that his
continual protests against the wooden methods of
the department were fruitless, he resigned for
conscience sake, and tried to get a living by start-
ing at Essendon a school which he runs on the
lines laid down by the most advanced German
professors of pedagogy. He has a magnificent
library of several thousand volumes on philosophy,
literature and currency, double-banked round the
schoolroom. His great hobby is currency, about
which he has probably thought and read more
than any other man. To him it is not a mere
branch of social science, it is a religion, nay, more ;
many religions, properly considered, are only
divinely - ordained compensations for defecti '•<*
A SNAG.
BARBEE: “ You've got a little dandruff, sir! ”
THE EMINENT BIOLOGIST: “ Ach, it is nodings!
Simply some epithelial eggsudation, bossibly
gombined mit a leetle varicose zeburossity
worn dem gapillary vollecules! ”
currency-systems. For instance, the doctrine of
self-abnegation so prominent in Buddhism, and so
much talked about in Christianity, is to him an
instinctive recognition by conscience-smitten man
of unjust gains obtained by an all-pervading
defective currency. It is even said that he was
with difficulty dissuaded by his friends from
naming one of his children ‘ Currency. ’ He sees
in reformed currency a cure for evils usually
thought (and once thought so by him) only curable
by revolutionary methods. His system is, roughly,
to recognise as the basis of currency not only gold,
but all articles which have what he calls an ‘ inter-
national value.’ He has also developed sliding
scales and automatic regulators for the proper
working of his system. The Melb. papers refuse
to insert his letters, the Banking Commission
ignored him—so that he has now tried, with at
least the success of publicity, a new method. One
wonders whether this quiet, neuralgia-tortured,
Essendon master of arts, neglected and laughed
at now, will one day, with his intense moral
earnestness, be recognised as the Moses or the
Mahomet of a new departure in civilisation.’

POLITICAL
POINTS
Sydney’s Mayor, Ives, says that Australia
began to go down when assisted-immigration was
stopped.
Hughes, M.L.A., speaking on N.S.W.
Factories Bill, said: “ Every Chinaman should
lee deemed a factory, and be compelled to consume
his own smoke.”
By some extraordinary blunder—or worse
—the annual report of the Australasian Federa-
tion League did not contain a single reference to
Parkes, over whom the League was wont to gush
as the “ Father of Federation.”
“ Some time ago,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent, “ two men, who were then suing for
wages in a western N.S.W. Police-court, deliber-
ately swore that they were dummy homestead
lessees, they made no bones about it; they gave
date and place, and named their employers. No
notice was taken by the authorities. The present
Ministry was not in power. ”
A- hint. When the
Maoris of the King
country are holding a Par-
liamentary session, the
Upper House is only al-
lowed one meal a day till
it puts through bills sent
up by the Lower House.
N.S. Wales’Post and
Telegraph Department
revenue for 189.) was
<£115,373 short of working
expenses, and <£211,233
short of working expenses
and interest on borrowed
money. The curious fea-
ture about the Post-office
is that no one worries about
the stupendous deficit. The
railway shortage sat on
everybody’s soul till at
last the country imported
the best expert it could
find, and paid him a big
salary for the express pur-
pose of putting the service
on a business footing and
making it pay its way if
possible. And meanwhile
any miserable kind of
politician any Cook or
Kidd or Dan O’Connor—
is allowed to run the other
department, and nobody
seems in the least inter-
ested about the leakage.
The Post-office wants quite
as much as the railways
to be bossed by a capable
expert permanently em-
ployed, with instructions
to run things on business
lines and make the dread-
ful institution pay its way
or kill somebody in the
attempt.
It has been proposed by some of Sydney’s
forward women’s-righters that an agitation shall
be started to make invalid married men’s agree-
ments relating to property, unless said agreements
are countersigned by the wife.
Hancock declares that he has asserted all
along that the Vic. railways savings could have
been effected only by allowing stock and perma-
nent-way to fall into disrepair. But he didn’t
assert it at the top of his voice.
Why are the Hindoos, Sikhs, Lascars,
Levantines, and all the brindle-colored crowd that
poke about Australia with a basket of goods and
a threatening scowl always called Assyrians ?
A Maoriland departmental report suggests that it
is because “ they came down like a wolf on the
fold.” Which is the nearest approach to an
intentional joke this paper ever saw in a depart-
mental report.
Legislation carried in N.S.W. Assembly in
the small hours of the morning goes to the Upper
House with the mark of the Beast on it. Every
hour after 11 p.m. the influence of the Labor-
party increases, so that by 3 or 4 a.m., instead of
being one-sixth of the total voting power, they
become one-third, and sometimes one-half. Which
fact prejudices the Council against all Bills put
through the Assembly after midnight.
N.S.W. Postmaster-Gen. Cook said, in a
recent address on “ sweating,” that men wearing
garments made by sweated labor are wearing the
blood and bone of their starving fellow-creatures.
And Affleck, M.L.A., remarks just here that
Cook’s department gets the coats worn by the
postmen made at Is. lid. each, and the price cf
lining, thread, and buttons has to come out of
this Is..lid. Either Afffeck, M.L.A., is very ui -
reliable, or P.M.G. Cook, of the God-fearh
; Ministry, is the last man in N.S-.W. who shouh.
talk about sweating.
DearBuLLETiN, —Despite thegood qualitie B
of Vale, who has just resigned his honorary port-
folio in the Turner Ministry, he is most unpopular
in the House. He is a Bore, and has a fixed
conviction that he is the only fit and proper
person to be Premier. The man utterly un-
popular in Parliament can do nothing in Parlia-
ment, no matter how well he may manage to work
his constituents. —Z.
Pickaxe : ‘ ‘ The Vic. Labor-party is vir-
tually without a leader. Hancock doesn’t do.
For one thing, he is not whole-souled with Labor,
and his versatility in preparing to start a news-
paper, which should emphatically be not a Labor
organ, is thought “ a little too much.” He is a
balancer, an opportunist, and too much of the
Farceur, whereas Labor is desperately serious,
being apt to get hungry. ”
Bananaland Leg. Assembly has raised
members’ salaries to £3OO per annum, also enacting
that said salaries can only be interfered with on a
two-thirds majority. A bare majority (be it noted)
sufficed to raise salaries so far, but a bare majority
cannot again reduce them. The other civil ser-
vants (for an M.L. A. is but a civil servant) want
to know why tteeir salaries cannot be secured in
fche same highly-satisfactory manner.
Foster Rogers, M.L.A. for South Yarra,
quoted The Bulletin’s article on Australia’s rule-
of-thumb financiers in his speech on the Turner
Budget. “The Bulletin,” said Rogers, “has
some good things in it, and some that are [not
quite so good.” Well, so has the Bible, the Koran,
the Dhammapada, the Vedas, the Talmud, and
other sacred works. The Bulletin only desires
to stand on a level with them so far as general
excellence is concerned.
PORTRAIT OF THE REAL REID SURPLUS .
Under a wise provision in the Maoriland
Old-Age Pension scheme, no pensioner is to receive
his allowance while absent from the country.
Maoriland doesn’t want to make any more ab-
sentees. Also, the pension stops while the pen-
sioner is in gaol, the State annexing the 10s. a
week to keep the aged criminal.

The M.L. lory party’s vote of censure on
the beddon Government was defeated by a stu-
pendous majority. Even the people who objected
to beddon borrowing one million couldn’t very
well throw him out when the alternative was put-
ting in Bussell, who not long since advocated bor-
rowmg twenty millions. And the speotacle of the
zu-miliion borrowing man moving a vote of cen-
sure on the other man for borrowing one million
was a lovely hunk of humor.
In reference to the cry that Bogan district
(N.S.W.) squatters are eaten out of lease and land
by kangaroos, a Bulletin correspondent points
out that at least some lessees deliberately preserve
kangaroos, and charge shooters, say, <£l per week
for clearing their paddocks of the “ noxious ver-
min,” whose presence, it is suggested, should
knock |d. an acre off the rent.
“ The Premier is a
professional politician, de-
pending on politics for a
living, and it is only on
rare occasions that men
of this class risk office for
the sake of principle.’
The lofty Duke who writes
thus scornfully of profes-
sional politicians in the
leading columns of the
Australian Star should
be laid across Dibbs’ knee
and given “ what for ”
with both hands. In fact,
the entire body of effec-
tive N.S.W. Protectionist
members should submit
him to a battery of whole-
some slaps.
Caldwell’s S.A. Par-
liamentary Privilege Act
Amendment Bill provides
that the utterances of any
M.P. shall not be privi-
leged in respect of the law
of defamation. Such a law,
while tending to make an
M.P. more careful and as
responsible for his utter-
ances as a newspaper
editor, a speaker at a pub-
lic meeting, or even the
man in the street, in ordi-
nary cases, would, in many
special cases, gag him
when he should be allowed
his mouth. Newspaper
men know many unsavory
things to be absolute facts,
and yet know frequently
the almost impossibility
of being able, or even
likely, to prove them in a
court of law. Members of
Parliament must also know
many such things. Fur-
ther, if an M.P. is to be
debarred from “privi-
lege” in “the House,”
why not a solicitor in a
court of law? Caldwell’s
proposal is utterly inex-
pedient, and anyhow will
not be carried. The habi-
tual, gratuitous slanderer
is not often elected to Par-
liament.
Nearly 5000 Tasmanian children never
attend school. The whole population is 160,000,
so the percentage of unlettered is high. Education
is not “ free and compulsory,” so the private semi-
denominational system is successfully teaching
the Tassies less than they know already.
Premier Reid, a year or two ago : “I shall
always oppose a land-tax until we have a proper
system of Local Government.” And where is
that Local Government Bill now ? Apparently it
has dropped so utterly out of all knowledge that
things are practically where they were 20 years
back.
Dr. Renwick’s principal argument in
N.S.W. Leg. Council against Dr. Cullen’s Bill to
reform the method of election and tenure of office
of members of Sydney University Senate was that
the Senate has. not asked for the Bill. The argu-
nient contributed largely to successfully shelve the
proposal to reform. When, then, in the near
future, the promised Bill reforming the same Leg.
Council reaches that logically-behaving body, it is
P resume d that some Renwick of the time
will move to shelve it on similar grounds. Cer-
tainly the Leg. Council will not have asked for
such a Bill.
The N.S.W. Government has accepted,
not the lowest, but the second lowest tender for
the new Gundagai bridge, because it came from a
local contractor—the lowest tender came from
Melbourne. Wherefore the public is to pay more
than is necessary for its bridge in order to bolster
n<^u , - tiie said industry is to be
subsidised at the expense of the taxpayer, instead
Btf mdmg alone in the “ bracing air of Free-
tiade and the various horrors of Protection
which the Government lives by denouncing are
surreptitiously let loose on the land. Why can’t
Keid and Co. have the courage of their alleged
opinions ? &
Premier Reid, dealing with Cope-
land’s championship of a Monroe doc-
trine for Australia, committed himself
to the opinion that Australia took
possession of Maoriland, thus going
outside her boundaries at the very start.
This view is incorrect. Maoriland, as
New Zealand, was gazetted a British
dominion in 1787 in virtue of the
sovereignty established by Captain
Cook, and never was any more a posses-
sion of Australia than, is Australia of
British New Guinea.
Premier Reid, being questioned in N.S.W.
Assembly concerning Jack Want’s promised “re*
port on Japan,” &c., promised to have “ a search ”
made for the document :
N. : “Australia is strewn with the human
wrecks of ardent enthusiasts in political life •
Freetraders, Protectionists, National Bankists"
Socialists, Christians, men who have dashed into
the machine and retired mangled because of their
poverty. Politics are only meant for the individual
with lots of money ; and cost him nothing. From
him that hath not is taken even that he hath.”
FLORAL TRIBUTES.
JOUNNIE : “ You might give me your roses, doncherknow."
HEBE: “ I can’t. I'm wearing them for a gentleman. He sends them
to me in the morning so that I can take them from my dress
and give them to him at luncheon-time when the bar it
crowded.”
N.S.W. ex-Treas. See objects to the con-
struction of electric trams in George-street, Sydney,
because they will deprive farmers of work growing
feed for ’bus-horses. The same-objection applies
to all the tramways and railways in the country,
and to John See's own steamers ; they all deprive
the farmer of work in growing feed for the horses
and bullocks that might have humped goods and
travellers all over N.S.W. Therefore Mr. See
should abjure railways and trams and steamers,
and go home per buliock-dray, and live up to his
own great theory.
Search high, search low, search far, search near •
Search places likely, places queer;
Search pigeon-holes, or small, or big;
Search Jack, his pockets, boots, and wig •
Search Crown Law Offices, the lot; ’
Search Want, his chambers, and his yacht;
Search fishing-baskets, luncheon-bags,
Gun-cases, lockers, four-hand drags,
The private clubs, more private bowers,
Where Johnny passes.quiet hours ;
And when you’ve searched, and searched in vain
Bull’s-eye the search on Jacky’s brain ;
And seize the store th’ enamoured chap
Retains regarding Japs and Jap—
The honeyed tour, the heathen Christmas,
The almond-optical strabismus
That lures the fancy’s finger-tips on,
That Mem’ry ever licks its lips on !
Let Freetrade tariffs balance short,
Let stark stagnation blight “ the court,"
Flaccidity usurp all sport,
But let us have that Jap. report.
D.

other tropical products, and convenient to Mills
in course of erection under the Sugar Guarantee
Act, is prepared to treat with intending Investors
on Easy a erms.
The lands offered compare advantageously with
those of Bundaberg or Mackay for important
reasons. Frost is unknown, damage from grub
nil, and the climate unsurpassed for the purposes
of such culture. To persons of small means this

sent offering in the colony.

The Morning Post said of Australia v. The
Players: “The colonial Eleven deserve the warmest
praise for the manner in which they utilised their oppor-
tunities. At times the cricket became a little slow to
watch, and, when they had the bowlers at their mercy,
one could have wished that the batsmen had played a
freer game, but the method adopted in Australia of plac-
ing no limit as to the time over which a match shall
extend has caused most of the batsmen now with us to
wait for their opportunities and run no risks.”
* * * *
Shrewsbury was left out of the Gentlemen v.
Players match because he asked for time to consider
whether the ordinary terms were good enough. The
Nottingham v eteran is regarded by the powers that be as
the root of the discontent that is growing up among the
pros.
* * * *
Nice point of cricket etiquette over this year’s
Oxford v. Cambridge. Cambridge wanted to prevent
Oxford from following on, and managed to give Oxford
the 12 runs needed by wide balls. A maze of discussion
ensues as to whether this is the square thing.

C.M.G. : “ Every success of Jones’ is followed
by murmurs in the English press re the unfairness of his
deli/enes. If the Australians win the next test with
conspicuous assistance from the Adelaide scorcher, his
unfairness is sure to be unanimously and emphatically
asserted at’Ome. But they have bought over “Jonah”
with all faults, and once he becomes a British possession
all doubts will be set at rest according to the venerable
J.B. maxim, 4 What is (ours)is best.*”

Cricketer Midwinter’s only child—a boy, aged
12 died in Melb. Children’s Hospital the other day, having
been a patient sufferer there for eight years.

Melb. Bicycle Club gives .£550 for two days’
racing—Nov. 28, Dec. 5.

At a Melb. cyclists’ Church-parade, on Sunday
week, tliere were 1000 cyclists and 4000 spectators. The
collection panned out ±lO, or nearly Id. per head ! But
then, after buying a bike, the average man lias not even a
halfpenny.
* * » *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League meeting last Satur-
day at Sydney Agricultu al Ground drew a good crowd, de-
spite the wet and biting cold. The honors and cash of the
occasion go to W. Davison, of Hillgrove, N.S.W. This
young man frem the country won the Hampden Wheel
Race (±5O), 2 mi es, and the First Class Handicap (£10),
miles. In his heat of the Winter Stakes (he should
win the final next Saturday) he led his field and made the
pace against a strong wind, and in the straight he sprinted
away from Chapman (N.S.W.), Clinton and Don Walker
(Vic.). In the Hampden final he led the last three laps ;
and at the finish broke all his men up with liis faster pace
and won in champion style. He won the First Class
yards. His handicaps for the Hampden and
First Class were respectively 185 and 130 yards, but they
do not detract from his performance.
The great Bill Martin received a gruelling.
His only place was third in the First Class Handicap
The sturdy Yank seems to have rather lost his head ; tie
hasn’t been riding a winning race lately. He “slung”
the Humber people for the Stearns, and the Stearns for
the Dux, and it is said he is now quarrelling with his last
love. The entanglement of business seems to have
entered into his riding; he didn’t show any of his splendid
pace or decision this time.
xt le new American, ran third in the
N.S.W. Championship, and didn't do anything to raise
hair with surprise. He has matched himself for ±lOO
against Martin.
Ken Lewis won the N.S. Wales Chamion-
ship G mile £10), also his heat, by gamely brilliant
riding. In the final Martin started his old-time long
sprint m the last lap, but was cut down in the straight
and beaten home by Lewis, O’Brien (N.S.W.), and
McDoneil. v
Don Walker (Vie.) showed a Zimmerman-down-
lng-spnnt in his heat for the N.S.W. Championship.
Yankee McDoneil was winning but in the last 20yds.
Walker pedalled home quicker than the Yank could think.
Porta was certain that he, not Middleton, ran
second to Walker in his heat of the N.S.W. Champion-
ship. When Middleton’s number went up for second
place Porta, came out and protested. “ Oxcuse me,
gentlemen, Porta second by half-yard ” ; and he held out
ms light arm and then, with his ieft hand, notched upon
it how good the half-yard was.
Meadham (N.S.W.) rode with great judgment
and pluck in his heat of the Hampden Wheel Race, beat-
ing Walne (Q.) and Ken Lewis (M.L.).
Marsehner, the German trick cyclist, brought
down the house with sensational feats, which crave the
notion thatjie wore a pair of invisible wings.
r l he Victorian League has decided not to sanc-
tion outside promoters, except in a few instances. The
league runs only one meeting a year itself, and its dog-in-
the-manger policy will probably be reversed by the new
council.
* * * *
The match between Martin and M'Donell is to
be run on novel lines. The men start together, but ride
in opposite directions. This will make the contest dull
from a spectacular point of view, but the dulness will be
compensated for by the possibility of a smash every time
the men meet, and the gentle public dearly loves its
* * * *
The alleged world’s record put up by cyclist
I arsons at Chicago turns out to be 1 min. 54 l-sth sec.,
standing start, or § sec. slower than Gibbons-Brooks’
English best. The Yankee recognised record, by J. S.
Johnson, stands at 1 min. 50 sec., and a dozen’Murkan
riders have put up miles between this and the English
time. In any case, there would be little incentive held
out to the Australian to beat Johnson’s time, considering*
that both men are members of the same trade team)
Whethei 1 arsons can beat it or not is, of course, oues-
tionable, and what has to be reckoned out is the capa-
bility, on a perfect track* with perfect pacing, of a man
who, with indifferent both, turned off the distance in
1 min. 58 2-sth sec. Does this equal more or less than
1 nun. 50sec. ?
* * * *
The really wonderful part of Parsons’ perform-
ance at Chicago was not his turning-off the mile in 1.54 1-5
hut the time in which he did the last quarter of it—2s 1-5
se °- J! 1 . 0 wmWh flying-start quarter stands at 22 3-5 sec.
—by Windle, of Cleveland, Ohio, who did the distance
when fresh, while Parsons had already pedalled 1320yds.
at top speed. As it was, Parsons’ time is 1 2-5 sec. better
than any Englander’s.

The winner of the Half-mile Championship at
Sydney League’s meeting was largely starred as “ Ken
Lewis, of Maoriland, ’ apparently to impress the public
with the width of the field which supplied the entries.
As a matter of fact, Lewis is a Melb. native who spent
three or four years in Maoriland, returning to his native
cabbage-garden about two years ago. Another alleged
Maorilander is Either, born in England, and with the
last five years divided between Melb. and Sydney
The newest French bicycle is made chiefly of
aluminium, and is “several pounds lighter and just as
strong as the ordinary machine.”

An idea for cycle-meet promoters : A handicap
Resurrection race, for all the old-style bone-shaker bikes.
It would bring to light some weird machines and cause
fun.
* * * •
The Rand wielc-Wallaroo argument settler was
the Sydney football game of the winter. Both sides took
it in turns to score and run over each other, and uncer-
tainty prevailed to the last. Consequently the yelling
crowd, including a feminine battalion of cart-wheel hats,
was hoarse long before gas-light. Rands, were five ahead
until Gardiner organised a working bee ; a try was scored,
and a goal kicked in a borrowed boot. Result : 16 all,
and everyone satisfied.
* * * •
Saturday saw the downfall of the two leading
senior Melt), football-clubs. Essendon’s game against 8.
Melb., before the larg-est crowd of the season, was full of
incident and good play. The scores in the first and third
quarters were even, but South’s doggedness and good con-
dition brought them home winners by 6g. 6b: to 3g.
Melb. v. Collingwood was a hard tussle. After
.much preliminary good football, Melb. led by 3g. to 1 at
half-time. From that out it seemed anyone’s match
until nearing the finish the “red-legs” got two more
goals, and finished up with 6g. Bb. to Coiling wood’s 4g. 3b.
South, Essen don, and Collingwood now stand
equal at the head of the premiership list. To-day (22nd)
South play Collingwood, and Essendon Melb. South and
Essendon should win.
North Melbourne beat Richmond 19g. 12b. to
4g. 4b.—season’s record. Larkin kicked 6 goals : another
record. In the other matches, Port Melb. beat Williams-
town sg. to 2g. Footscray beat Carlton 7g. to 4g., and
Fitzroy beat Geelong lOg. to sg.
* * * *
Though less interest is taken in Melb. football
than for twenty years past, the present season is really
the best-contested on record. The defeat of Essendon by
South and Collingwood by Melb. places the three first-
named clubs on an equality, a thing which has not oc-
curred at this time of the season within the memory of
the oldest barraeker. In ’B7 South and Geelong were
level until tneir second meeting at the beginning of Sep-
tember placed the Pivotians on top, but no other club
stood within oooee of either. Between ’B7 and to-day one
team has invariably topped the list with a fair lead, and
the premiership has, by the middle of August, always
been a settled fact.
* * * »
S.A. v. Victoria lacrosse match, in Adelaide,
proved a runaway victory for S.A. by 13 goals to nil,
though Victoria had almost her best, team. Despite
counter attractions there were nearly 4000 spectators, an
illustration of the strides the Canadian game is making in
Adelaide. How long before a representative Australian
lacrosse team visits Eng. and Canada?
* * * *
In Adelaide lacrosse seems already to have un-
dermined football. Senior football teams find great diffi-
culty in securing recruits, and as for the public—there
were 500 people present at the match, Norwood v. South,
on Aug. 1. At same match last year, about 5000 !
* * * *
Westralia will send East an eight-oared crew to
compete in the next Interprovincial contest. At the pre-
sent time the West has the cream of Australia’s amateur
rowing men to pick from, but all are merely transferred
Easterners, and no native Westralian has a “ possible ” of
a seat in the boat.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Stanbury's objections to row-
ing Gaudaur at Prillia were groundless. Situated on Lake
Simcoe, one of the loveliest sheets of fresh water in
America, without tides or currents, it is but two hours’
railway journey from Toronto (Iranian’s home) and four
from Niagara Falls, and is in every way an ideal spot for
a rowing contest. The “ red Indians ” exist in Stanbury’s
mind, while the only ice he could get in the boating
season would he the lump the bar-tender might put in his
cocktail. And Gaudaur is not a ’Murkan, but a French-
Canadian, born and raised near Montreal, and if the
“loyal ” protests of Canada go for anything, as much a
“colonial” as Stanbury. Let us be exact.—One Who
Knows.
* * * *
A sporting syndicate has cabled ’Ome a big sum
for investment on Stanbury in his match with Gaudaur.
The Harding match, regarded as somewhat uncertain,
elicited little local speculation. Sydney sports dearly love
“ morals.”
* * * *
Hawkesbury “ townies ” of sculler Towns are
allegedly willing to put up any amount for a go at Stan-
bury if their man beats Neilsen on 29th inst. This is the
sort of enthusiasm that gives a fillip to a good sport. The
young aspirant should be a better paddler a year or two
hence. Peter Kemp is fining him down, but racing for
cash is admirable schooling.
* * * *
The stroke of the Maoriland Four which lately
visited Sydney writes to Tiie Bulletin “ A paragraph
in your paper states that a rumor is current in Sydney
that the M.L. crew were drugged a few days before com-
peting in the Interprovincial Fours. I wish to give such a
report an emphatic denial. No crew was ever, or could
be ever, treated with greater kindness and consideration
than were the M.L. crew by all the Sydney residents with
whom they came in contact. 1 deeply regret that such a
report should have been spread about. George B.
Richardson.” [Very likely but, all the same, certain
members of that crew believed, or affected to believe, that
poison had been put into a meal taken by them at a Svdney
caravanserai. It was agreed that, as the thing could not
he absolutely proved, nothing should be said about the
matter ; but “ that which is known to more than one is
no longer a secret.”—Ed. B.]

Larrikin’s victory in Vic. Club Steeple Saturday

* * * *
One swell Sydney bookie has now stepped down
to a silver connection. Spot cash, though small, is often
better than a dubious fiver “on the nod.” The incidental
cronkness is the only thing against a silver biz. If an
honest tote-man happened along, Sydney would give him
a fortune in no time.
♦ * * *
The art of bringing horses together per light
and heavy lead-bags is rarely exemplified so strikingly as
in the Northern J.C. (Maitland) Flying Handicap last
week. Tom, Yellow Plush, and Syndicate dead-heated.
The trio ran off, Tom and Yellow Plush tieing again. The
crowd began to get interested when the pair went out a
third time, Tom winning by half-a-head—so the judge
said. Had the jock, on the ultimate loser dropped his cap
in one of the heats, he might have just got home.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 6Goft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ;3, Stationery; 4, Music ;5, Eancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures.; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
Why, The Bulletin again asks, has the
public not been officially informed of the cause
why the old Sydney Harbor ferry-steamer Bright-
side, alias Emu, which is now running once more,
lately sank at her moorings? By the way, the
Brightside, for some reason not an over-popular
boat, now has her name painted up in old English
characters which are particularly hard to decipher.
Eno’s “Fruit Salt.”—A lady writes : —“ I
think that you will be glad to hear that I find
your ‘ Fruit Salt ’ a most valuable remedy, and I
can assure you I recommend it to all my friends
and the result is always satisfactory. Everything,
medicine or food, ceased to act properly for at
least three months before I commenced taking it,
the little food I could take generally punished me
or returned. My life was one of great suffering,
so that I must have succumbed before long. To
me and our family it has been a great earthly
blessing. I feel I cannot say too much for it.”
Sold by all chemists and stores.*
“M.L.A.” to The Bulletin :
M‘Millan’s personal dislike of Reid betrayed him into
Weak and imprudent exaggeration. The public will know
how to judge of his sincerity when they consider that in-
stead of justifying his furious charges of deceit and cor-
ruption by voting against the Govt., he retired from the
chamber immediately after his speech, and is now content
to support an administration which he has publicly and in
the plainest terms accused 'ff serious offences. —D.
As regards his vote, M'Millan’s attitude is per-
fectly logical. A man may not vote with the
Government because he reckons it too bad to be
supported, and not vote with the Opposition
because he reckons it no better. Government by
Contradiction naturally produces deadlocks such
as this.

A TERRIBLE CASE OF
ECZEMA
CURED BY
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mrs. E. Wyatt, of Port Hoad, West
Hindmarsh, So. Australia, writes of
the sad condition of her little daugh-
ter, whose portrait she also sends:
Wi
“My daughter was afflicted with
Eczema of the most aggravated
type. The disease first appeared in
eruptions on her head, then her hair
began to fall out, and in spite of
the best medical advice and treat-
ment she grew steadily worse. The
sores were full of matter and were
extremely offensive. Her eyes be-
came affected, and she was, in truth,
in a terrible state. My neighbors
were very sympathetic and took
great interest in the case. They
persuaded me to try Ayer’s Sarsa-
parilla, and I am most thankful to
be able to say that this wonderful
medicine coihpletely restored my
daughter’s health. She has now as
good a head of hair as anyone could
wish, her eyes are perfectly well,
and she is a fine girl of eight years
with every prospect of growing up
to be a strong and healthy woman.”

“I am ever dreaming that the old aque-
ducts of the water of life may yet be repaired.”—
Stead.
Mdlle. de Pougy, the beautiful queen of
Parisian demi-mondaines, attempted to commit
suicide the other week. The girl had seemingly
fallen in love with an unreciproeative young
“swell,” and the “horror of her life” began to
pall upon her. A bad look-out for Mdlle. If she
has taken a first dose of poison before her admirers
are dropping off she will get very tired of herself
presently.
The cheerful Butler, now doing ten years’
hard labor for nothing in particular, has owned up
to the charge of highway robbery on which he
was acquitted by a Melb. jury. Two old men
derived particular satisfaction from Butler’s burst
of candour. Mr. M‘Phee’s watch was returned to
the bosom of its lawful owner, and Judge Hol-
royd’s terribly stiff sentence seemed indirectly justi-
fied (a la Windeyer-Dean). Had it been milder
the Crown Law department would not have cared
to guarantee Butler’s safety from a second trial
on the burglary charge.
There died at Hobart, August sth, lex.A
McGregor, M.L.C., aged 75, an important figure
in Tas. shipping circles. He started life as a
shipwright, embarked his savings in a whaling-
vessel, and by some lucky cruises laid the founda-
tions of his fortune. Eventually he became one of
the largest owners and merchants in Tas.—run-
ning quite a fleet of whalers as well as English
and interprovincial traders. Elected to the Legis.
Council in ’BO, as member for Hobart, he retained
his seat till a month or two ago, when failing
health made him resign. He dabbled in mining
and made a lot of money out of the sale of the
Anchor Tin Mine to English investors ; had he
lived he would shortly have figured as defendant
in a suit brought against him by the original
holders of the mine, who do not consider their
side of the transaction satisfactory. It was this
deal which led up to Premier Braddon’s castiga-
tion by local and English press for allowing his
name to appear on the English prospectus as a
director.

J. Garrard, of the N.S.W. God-fearing
Ministry, appeared along with Quong Tart at a
Salvation Army shivoo in Sydney the other night
(what Mandarin Tart wanted there doesn’t ap-
pear, but then Brother Tart is more or less every-
where) and spoke a piece :
He expressed his gratification at being allowed to pre-
side. His sympathies were with them in their work. He
was not a stranger to these proceedings in view of the
fact that he came from a good old Methodist stock.
(Cheers.) And by the grace of God he had been enabled
to do something for the Master during the 25 years’ ser-
vice since he became what was called a “ converted ” man.
(“ Glory,” “ Amen,” and applause.) Some people thought
that it was a disgrace to acknowledge that they had
passed from darkness to light, but he was not built that
way. (“Praise be to God!” “Hallelujah.”) With all
humility, but at the same time with great gratification
and pleasure, he bore tribute to the saving grace of the
Lord Jesus Christ. (Volleys of cheers.) Australia had to
thank God for such an organisation as the Salvation
Army, and for the splendid work it was doing in its
social aspect, and he believed that the Army had dealt
with this social question better than any other section of
Christ’s church.
And then Brother Garrard waved his wild, red
jersey on the Australian atmosphere, and the big-
drum vote (though, of course, he didn’t seek it)
was safe for next election. Hallelujah ! Also
Mahershalalhashbaz ! Likewise Jeroboam !!

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your I
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.

(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”
A Lady’s Wish Fulfilled.

Abridged Prospers of tf>« MOUNT LYELL 60LDEN FLUX SUPPLY COMPANY, MoftQt Lyell, TaSipapia.
TO BE REGISTERED AS A NO-LIABILITY COMPANY.
CAPITA!,: ... ... £35,000, in 70,000 Shares of 70s. Each. All Fully Paid-up .
50,000 Shares fully paid up to 10s. per Share are now offered to the public at 2s. each; payable—ls. on application; Is. on allotment; out of the proceeds of which £2400 pet is placed to the credit of the company for working
ra ital, which is considered more than sufficient for all purposes. 10,000 Shares held in reserve for future issue if any further capital should be required, and 30,000 Shares go to the vendors in part payment for the property. No Calls.
All Shares fully paid up. THE VENDORS PAY ALL BROKERAGE AND FLOTATION EXPENSES. DIRECTORS and AUDITORS, &c., to be Appointed at First Meeting of Shareholders,
This company is being formed for the purpose of supplying the Mount Lyell Company with a flux (silica) containing
gold, the pres ;nce of which in same would result in a considerable extra profit per ton being made on all ore treated
by them, as, vide Dr. Peter’s report, a silica, i.e., flux, is absolutely necessary for smelting the Mount Lyell ores ; and
the flux, or silica, now being obtained and used by them is of no intrinsic value it follows that their being supplied
with a flux containing even a fair percentage of gold would result, as stated above, in a substantial extra profit on
their smelting operations. The advantage, therefore, to the Mount Lyell Company in obtaining a flux of this kind is
very obvious, and is, perhaps, better illustrated by reference to the Tarrawingee Flux Company in its relation to the
Broken Mill mines. This property is exceptionally well situated for most economical working, &c., inasmuch as it
is only twenty chains from the Mount Lyell Company’s railway, and a tramway can be made to the line at a very
nominal cost. The Mount Lyell Company are prepared to take any quantity of flux of this description, and the
b nefit d'Tived by this company in supplying flux to the Mount Lyell mine in this manner is very great, as it entirely
obviates the necessity of the erection of crushing plant and other costly appliances, with their attendant expenses,
and, a > will be seen by Mr. G. D. Gibson’s report, the Mount Lyell Company are prepared to purchase auriferous quartz
on the au; of its GOLD CONTENTS, and, under these circumstances, a very small percentage of gold would enable
this company to pay regular dividends. The Tarrawingee Flux Company have paid 37 dividends, amounting in the
aggregate to ill(i.),0 10 (one hundred and sixty thousand pounds sterling). It will also be seen by the appended reports
that no costly machinery will be required, as there is ample water-power available for all purposes, thus reducing the
cost of working the mine an 1 raising the quartz to a minimum. Apart from the special profits to be derived from its
connection with the Mount Lyell mine, it will be seen by the appended paragraph from the Hobart Mkrcury that if
taken as a gold-mine and worked in the ordinary way it would be a good investment, and more especially on taking
i ito account that it can be worked entirely by water-power. The amount, viz., J 52400 net, to be placed to credit of
the company is ample, and it is not anticipated that the 10,000 shares held in reserve will require to be diposed of.
The vendors, as showing their bona-fides and confidence in the property, have applied for 3000 out of the 30,000
shares now olfered to the public. These shares are well worthy of the special attention of all SHAREHOLDERS in the
MOUNT LYELL MINE, as the benefit derived by them from a company so closely situated to the smelters and being
m a position to supply golden flux of this nature is very great. From reports made by Mr. G. D. Gibson, M.A.1.M.E.,
and Mr. Colin McDonald, it will be seen that a good deal of work has already been done, and it only now requires a
shaft to be sunk to raise the quartz.
EXTRACT from REPORT by G. D. GIBSON, Esq., M.A.I.M.E.
Taking a dishful of lode stuff from up and down the face, I found, on panning it off, that the clay and fines,
separate from the rubble, gave a fair prospect of fine gold, as also did the rubble after being roughly pounded and
washed, only that the gold was heavier. Combined, the result may be considered highly satisfactory, especially as a
good deal of gold would, no doubt, be contained in the coarser tailings and fragments rejected. Another sample dish
taken from the crosscut gave very similar results, but in this case the quartz was more solid, and in some of the stones
the gold is apparent to the eye.
THE WHOLE WIDTH of the FORMATION is NOT LESS THAN EIGHT (8) FEET. There has arisen of late a
very special local reason why the development of even a low grade quartz lode in this district is become a matter of
importance. The Mount Lyell Mining Company require silica as a flux for the sulphide ores, and are prepared to pur-
chase auriferous quartz on the value of its gold contents—the gold being of value to them on account of enriching
the bullion and the quartz as flux. Now, should this property develop a sufficiently large body of quartz, even though
it be poor in quality, it will be placed in a particularly fortunate position owing to its close proximity to the Mount
Lyell Co.’s railway, which runs within 20 chains of the southern boundary, from which point an easy connection can
be made on a falling gradient, and the stone delivered directly into the company’s trucks, the expense of carriage being
thus practically nil. It is rarely that owners of gold-mines meet with such a happy method of disposing of their
quartz, all the difficulties and anxieties of treatment being thus at once disposed of. Apart from these special con-
siderations, the merits of the property itself quite justify the expenditure of capital in its exploitation and develop-
ment, while with the above advantages added it does the more so.
EXTRACT from REPORT of Mr. COLIN M'DONALD, Mine Manager. - . *
A considerable amount of work has been done on the property. A drive has been put into the hill 170 ft., and
crosscut 60ft. At 110 ft. in the drive the reef was struck, showing a good body of stone carrying gold. A winze has
been sunk 20ft., some very fine specimens of gold-bearing stone being obtained. The facilities for working the mine
are exceptional. Having secured the right of a permanent supply of water, it would only necessitate the cutting of a
race a few chains to bring it to the machinery-site at a very small cost. A good supply of suitable timber for mining
purposes is on the property. The mine is within 20 chains of the Mount Lyell railway, and a "tramway with a good
grade could be put in at a trifling cost. In conclusion, I may state that I have every confidence in the mine proving a
good investment.
PARAGRAPH from the “ HOBART MERCURY,” Aug. 7, 1896.
We have received particulars of assays made by Officer and Co, of quartz taken from a claim situate within 20
chains of the Mount Lyell Company’s railway. The discovery of this quartz is considered to be very important, as the
Mount Lyell Company require silica as a flux for their sulphide ores, and it is understood that the company will be
prepared to purchase the quartz on the value of the gold contents. The assays were of quantities of quartz treated
with a pestle and mortar, and are as follows No. 1,360 z. lldwt. 17.6 gr. of gold per ton ; No. 2,590 z. Odwt. 12.8 gr.
of gold per ton ; No. 3, 19dwt. 14.4 gr. per ton ; No. 4,19 gr per ton ; No. 6, 16dwt. 17.4 gr. per ton ; No. 6, lOoz. 12dwt.
12.8 gr. per ton ; No. 7,17 oz. 16dwt. 21.7 gr. per ton.
P.S. —Above tests have been made from stone taken from the mine since Mr. Gibson’s report was written on this
property. Tests 1,2, 3 and 7 were from stone showing gold. Tests 4,5, and 6 were from stone NOT showing gold.

THIS Company is being formed to acquire Gold Mining Lease No. 474 x, comprising 24 acres, and
situated near the Red Lake, 13 miles E.N.E. of White Feather, and North of and adjoining the
well-known General Gordon Extended Mine.
The property has been inspected by Mr. G. R. Fearby, M.E., and Captain Glass, Manager of the
General Gordon Mine, and their reports are appended.
According to these reports the lode formation is identical with that of the General Gordon, and
outcrops on the property ; the parallel formation in the West Block of the General Gordon Extended
Gold Mining Company is continued into the General Gordon Junction ; two blocks to the South-West
there is a formation PROVED TO BE 100 FEET WIDE, and this passes through the centre of the
General Gordon Junction. The formation of these lodes is a highly ferruginous chlorite schist, con-
taining numerous quartz leaders carrying gold.
The Red Lake is believed to have a permanent supply of water in the driest seasons suitable for
milling and reachable in easy sinking. At present the lake is a vast sheet of water many miles in
length, and of an average depth of about six feet. There is abundance of mining timber, and the
question of transport of machinery and mine supplies is largely answered by the fact that the mine is
within 25 miles of Kalgoorlie, the present terminus of the railway.
<£6250 will be placed to the credit of the working capital, less brokerage and flotation expenses.
An Agreement dated the sth day of August, 1896, has been signed between Randolph Bedford, of
Adelaide, and William Arthur Kingsborough on behalf of the proposed Company, and can be inspected
at the Solicitors’ Offices, Pirie Street, Adelaide.
Upon the formation of the Company a meeting of the allottees of shares will be held for the pur-
pose of passing the Memorandum and Articles of Association, and for the Eleotion of Directors, etc.
Dated at Adelaide this sth day of August, 1896.
Report by Mr. G- R. Fearby, M.E.
Received by Telegram, 6th July, 1896.
“ Have inspected block 474, of 24 acres, known as the General Gordon Junction. The mine
adjoins the General Gordon Extended on the north and the Gordon Consols on the east.
“ The position is well pegged—THßEE HUNDRED AND THIRTY YARDS ON THE
COURSE OF THE LODE. Property also contains portion of General Gordon main lode. Develop-
ments are costeen pits and trenchings. In two costeens gold found, formation assaying loz. to the
ton. Now sinking a shaft in north-east corner of block on the main lode, not yet sufficiently deep.
“Two blocks south-west there is a LODE 100 FEET WIDE; good prospects. The outcrop
of this lode is in your direction, AND PASSES THROUGH ABOUT THE CENTRE OF TOUR
PROPERTY.
“ I consider the PROPERTY A VERY VALUABLE ONE r well worthy further development.
Abundance of water and timber.”
Report by (Japt. Glass, Mine Manager of the General Gordon Gold-mining Go.
Received by Wire, August 4, 1896.
“ Acting under instructions I have visited and carefully examined the mine known as the General
Gordon Junction, and beg to submit my report on same.
“ The area of the property is 24 acres, and the number of lease 474. The property is situated
west of and adjoining the General Gordon Consols, with the General Gordon Extended (block 2394 e)
adjoining on the southern boundary.
“ On the property No. 1 shaft has been sunk to a depth of 38ft., and meeting the water your
manager decided to open out and drive to intersect the lode. This drive will require to go 30ft.
further to cut the lode formation now showing on the surface. The drive is now in 22ft. south-west.
Another crosscut was put in north-west and suspended at a distance of 12ft. The labor is now beiijg ;
mostly used in pushing the south-west drive to cut the lode. This FORMATION OUTCROPS ON
THE SURFACE AND GIVES GOOD PROSPECTS BY DOLLYING.
“ No. 2 Shaft has been sunk to a depth of thirty-five feet, about one and a-half chains west of No.
1 Shaft. There is here a SECOND LODE FORMATION, six feet wide, CARRYING GOOD
GOLD.
“ A Costeen has been opened some 3 chains in length which CLEARLY SHOWS TWO LODE
FORMATIONS RUNNING PARALLEL TO EACH OTHER AND GOLD IS BEING
OBTAINED IN BOTH.
“ In my opinion, if this Costeen was extended east and west, other lodes would be met with, the
whole of the block being exceptionally favorable.
“THE GENERAL GORDON JUNCTION IS IN A DIRECT LINE WITH THE
GENERAL GORDON ; the strike of lode being south-east and north-west. Like the other mines in
the neighborhood, you have everything necessary for economical mining—water and an unlimited
supply of timber.
“ In concluding this report, I can CONFIDENTLY RECOMMEND THIS PROPERTY as a
SOUND MINING VENTURE.”
(Signed) WM. GLASS, Manager General Gordon Mine.

He was a leading member of the church,
and was in love with a sweet little girl who sang
in the choir and had a class at Sunday-school.
His love was returned. He had not always been
a good young man. There was a time when he
sowed his “ wild oats,” and on one occasion in too
prolific soil, which cost him 7s. 6d. a week. As he
gazed on the innocent little face he made up his
mind to tell her of his wickedness in the past,
and ask her forgiveness. When he told his story
she readily forgave him, saying she knew it was
the girl’s fault. “ Oh, darling,” he cried I could
be thoroughly happy but for the hole that 7s. ocl.
makes in my income.” “Never mind, dear, she
said, with a sweet little blush, “ I have an income
of 7s. 6d. a week.”
How Providence watches over the drunk.
The other day, at Heathcote (Vic.), a miner much
disguised in liquor started for home on a baturday
night, and wasn’t heard of again until late on
Tuesday. Most people were beginning to assume
that he had spontaneously combusted on the road,
and that his boots had blown away, till he was
found hollering for assistance at the bottom of a
40-foot shaft. This shows how the wind of
adversity is tempered to the shorn drunk. He
dropped 13 yards, and landed in a beery heap
without the slightest injury. There didn t happen
to be Bft. of mud at the bottom of that particular
shaft. There was no snake there. He didn t fall
on two dead sheep and die a horrible death through
blood-poisoning and effluvia. The shaft was old
and rotten, but it didn’t cave in with tne shock of
his sudden arrival. He didn’t die of thirst, for
rain came on and a thin stream trickled down for
drinking purposes. Also, he didn’t even get
rheumatism, and he was accidentally discovered
in three days,, whereas a sober, upright citizen
would probably not have been found for three
months.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Buck: “I call it our tight place because
there were three of us, riding across country to a
party at a neighbouring station. We were all
dressed in store clothes and each had a boiled
shirt. It had been threatening ram all the
morning, but we expected to reach the homestead
before it came. But, ere half the journey was
over, the rain began to fall in the heavy drops
that foretell a big shower. The genius of the
party proposed that we make for a certain hollow
tree, strip and unsaddle, plant the clothes, and
stand the shower out, holding the prads, and,
when it was all over, pull out the dry clothes. This
brilliant idea was carried out to the letter, to. the
accompaniment of heavy thunder and lightning.
Then, holding the horses, we awaited the end of
the storm under the shelter of the big gum. But
presently it started to hail, and, ere you could say
Bulletin, down it came in torrents. The homes
reared and plunged with the sting of the big
stones, and dragged us all out in bare pelts from
the shelter of the tree. Then— the proverbial cat
on hot bricks couldn’t have got a place with us.
The tree was soon a full hundred yards away, and
that tree was the only shelter for half a mile
or more. Try our hardest we couldn’t get
the horses to it. Suddenly—crack !—light
ning, struck the tree, and down it came in a
million pieces with a crash lost in the deafening
peal of thunder. Thoughts of what might have
happened made us white and speechless; hut the
restless prads soon brought us to our senses. We
were covered from head to foot with red and blue
spots —walking plum-duffs. When the storm
eased down, stiff and sore, we waded through the
d4bris, and fished out of the stump of that old
gum the soddened and blackened mass which was
once our clothes. The saddles were done for.
We didn’t go to that party.”

TO MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
_Patent Battery Plates,
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from

Ed. Bulletin,— Be crows’ nests. Surely
no ornithologist is responsible for the bush saying
that a crow’s nest has never been found ? In Tas-
mania, at least, they are as commonly found as
the magpie’s, though they certainly choose trees
more difficult of access. The Horton College boys
always had full clutches of crows’ eggs in their col-
lections. Perhaps there is a confusion of crow
with black cockatoo —that has always been the
roc’s egg amongst collectors.— Van Demon.
