A Tied House .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ You must do a snug business here, Mrs.
Firking,” said the customer, helping himself
liberally to the counter-lunch. “ It’s a good idea
of yours starting the bar-lunch in the suburbs,
same as in the city. It’ll make business.”
“Yes,” replied Mrs. Firking. “There ain’t
much in it, though, when all’s said and done.”
“Ah !” genially laughed her patron, “that’s
always the way with business-people—never satis-
fied even when they’re coining money.”
“ Coining money—God help us ! ” murmured
the landlady under her breath. Then, smiling :
“ I see you know all about it, Mr. Spencer; I
wonder you don’t go in for a pub. yourself.”
And as he placed a threepenny-piece on the
counter as payment for his beer and his luncheon,
he said he had thought about it. It wasn’t so
difficult, was it, to start with a brewer’s aid ?
“ As easy as winking,” rejoined Mrs. Firking.
* * * *
He was her twentieth caller, was Spencer,
and it was already half-past eleven o’clock. Six
shillings and sixpence in the till, and a shilling’s-
worth of food gone from the bar-lunch. Then
there was big Murphy, and Sam the butcher, and
little Johnson, the barber from across the road.
They would all come in presently for their three-
penny beers—long ones—and each would make a
heavy meal.
“Dashed good institooshun, this, ain’t it?” she
had heard Johnson say. “ I used to pay a bob
for my dinner before without the drink, and now
I gets pretty well as good a feed and the beer
thrown in for a thrum ! ” Most of her customers
thought with Johnson. There was one blessing,
however. After the men had picked over the
lunch-spread, she could always get enough for a
meal for herself and the girl out of the fragments.
Half-past eleven, and about twelve-fifteen the
traveller of the great brewery would drop in. He
would want some money on account, that was
certain, and all she could muster was three-pound-
ten—a mere drop in the bucket of her indebted-
ness. Then there was the overdue account of the
one wine and spirit merchant with which the
brewery would allow her to deal. He would be
pressing her next week—and the rate-collector
was due for calling again also—and then the grocer
was snarling, and the children’s school-fees.
Thank heaven ! the good sisters at least would
wait, if no one else would ; though even it was
difficult to make them and Father Clancy under-
stand that she, the landlady of the snug little
public, doing such a nice corner-trade, and with
two lodges meeting in her house, was not paying
her way, and could not contribute to this school
fund or that church subscription.
Twelve-fifteen —and the natty sulky of the great
brewery’s representative drove up to the door.
“Mornin’, Mrs. Firkin’, how’s trade- got some-
thing for me, I hope. I see the painters are put-
ting an advertisement on your back wall. Who’s
it for ? ”
“The Dailv squelcher has offered me four
pounds a year ”
“Oh, you can’t do that, Mrs. Firking, without
the firm’s permission. I don’t know how I came
to overlook it —of course, I see"how it catches the
eyes of the train people We must have our own
name up there.”
“But,” in desperation, exclaimed Mrs. Firking,
“ the firm won’t pay four pounds a year for its
use. And you’ve got the front, and the side-walls,
and the bare windows now ! ”
“Oh, that’s all in the agreement, you know.
However, I’ll tell you what we’ll do : we ll pay the
sign-writer for his lost time. There, that’s
fair ; now, isn’t it ? And now, what cash are
you going to favor me with to-day? ’Twon’t
break the sulky down, I hope ? ”
Mrs. Firking smiled as in duty bound at this
pleasantry, and tendered three-pounds-ten. “ It’s
all I have ; every penny. ”
The commercial’s jocularity dropped to the point
of vanishment.
“ That all ? Look hete, Mrs. F. ; that won’t do,
you know. I’ll get sacked over your account yet.
Why, you must be letting the trade go away to
nothing ! And we hear you’re getting behind with
your tradesmen, and the rent-account is getting
into arrears, too. Come, now, you must send me
out at least a fiver—not a penny less. If you can’t
—well, I’m really afraid the firm will have to come
down on you. And on me, too. They’ll say I
have been too lenient.”
“I can’t give you more.” The woman was
within an inch of tears.
The commercial, doing but true service to his
masters, became insistent to the verge of brutality.
He must have five pounds, or
Mrs. Firking went up-stairs, and, returning,
threw down another sovereign. “ There ! take
that from my little ones’ money-box. No, you
needn’t look as if I gave it them. It’s a schnapps-
coupon pound. God knows, nothing much in luck
comes in my way now, and the children hadn’t
had a shilling in their box for months. ”
“ Four-pound-ten,” said the commercial, un-
moved. “ Ten shillings more, please, Mrs. F.”
“ I have nothing more ! ” The woman was
sullen.
“ Oh, yes, you have ! You’ve got some tram-
tickets and stamps in that cigar-box, haven’t you ?
Make up five bob more with them, and I’ll take
that bottle of M'Sandy’s whisky there for the
balance. Is the capsule sound ?
“That’s the only bottle of good whisky I have
unopened in the place. The stuff you send ’ll get
me into trouble some day. Besides, that bottle
cost me six-and-three, and you’d only give me
equal to five shillin’. And I ain’t paid for it yet,
neither.”
Protest notwithstanding she handed the bottle
down at last. Giving her a receipt, by cash and
sundries, for the five pounds, he fell back into the
persuasive.
“Now, look here, Mrs. Firking, I think, and
the firm think, you’re not cut out for this business.
You owe us a matter of five hundred pounds—
that’s three hundred of the original purchase and
interest and current account since. Hadn’t you
better give it up ? You see the trade’s going to
the dogs.”
“ And what’ll I do for the two hundred I gave
you to come in?” moaned the woman. “All I
and the children had in the world ! ”
“ Oh, well, that’s gone, you know. At the worst
you know you’ve had your living out of the hotel
for 15 months, and that’s something.”
“ What of all my hard work, and the worry, and
the long hours ? Ain’t they worth my bite and
sup ? ”
“Anyhow, the firm know a man who’s ready to
come in. Now, if you’ll go out without any fuss
I’m authorised to say they’ll let off, say, half of
the five hundred. And that’s treating you real
liberal.”
“ And what of my other creditors —not much
’tis I owe besides the brewery—what am I to do
for a home for my children ? ”
“That’s no concern of the firm’s, Mrs. P. You
came into the business open-eyed ’
“ God forgive you the lie ! ” Then the woman
broke down.
And a fortnight later an advt. appeared in
the Squelcher of another “ Eligible hotel property.
Suburb, snug bar trade, two lodges meeting in
rooms. Ingoing, J 260 0; cash, =£2so, easy terms
for balance. Satisfactory reasons given by present
licensee for leaving.”
Tied houses are so-called because they are tied
round some poor devil’s neok.
The Dowager Duchess of Newcastle re-
sides in a small house at Whitechapel, and devotes
herself to charitable work among the poorest
Catholic members of the “ submerged tenth.” A
theme for any moralist who knows anything about
the lady’s two dead husbands and the life she led
between them. Poor old Duoh. ! —not that she is
very old, either.
Recent mention of a workman being cast
into the molten metal of one of the Murray bridge
iron supports, recalls a somewhat similar occur-
rence, when the N.S.W. Blue-Mountain Zigzag
was being built. Two navvies were undermining
the side of a hill there to cause a slip, when the
whole mass came down on them. The stuff
fell just where it was wanted, so the men were not
dug out; and every train that passes rumbles a
requiem for their souls.
A great oratorical break was lately made
at a high-toned Sydney function. The eloquent
responder, mounted on old sherry—“the winged
horse of Parnassus that hurries the bard to the
skies” —beamed around him, and exclaimed : “If
the sons and daughters of our first families were
whipped naked down Pitt-street, as their ancestors
were ! ” But he was docile, and accepted the
tug from behind, so that very few are competed to
say they heard that politician make a fool of
himself.
Up Youlahmine way, recently, the “boys ”
stuck up a Syrian hawker for all he had. After-
jtrds, the “ Assyrian came down like a wolf on
fold ” and laid an information. But their
pals squared it privately with the foreigner for a
tenner, so, when the case came on, nothing could
be dragged out of him but “ me don’t know, no
understand.” The result was he got 48 hours for
“contimpt.” But the story, unluckily for the
man from Syria, does not end just here. When
released he discovered, by experiment, that the
tenner was “cronk”—it nearly got him run in
again.
A report mentions that on the Great
M'Kinley Day in ’Murka, the Chief M'Kinley
Boss, amid the general orgies, didn’t know how he
went to bed —“ whether he was afoot or on horse-
back.” Let Envy speak :
“Upon thy belly shalt thou go!” I'he
abject flunkeyism of the middle-class Briton was
never better exemplified than by the fact that he
wouldn’t have read a line of Blackmore’s “ Lorna
Doone ” if a certain edition of the work hadn t
happened to coincide with the Lorne-Louise mar-
riage ; whereupon—supposing in “Lorna” somi
reference to “Lome” —the collective British
Jeames rushed that is, crawled —for the book,
and financially “made” it.
Dear Bulletin, —Does the bush brutalise
women? Outside a pub., on the borders of tha
Never-Never, an unfortunate greyhound had been
run over, but managed to crawl to where two fine
strapping damsels sat. The poor dog was in a soul-
stirring state, yelping piteously in its agony, and
should have cudgelled up sympathy in the most
callous heart. But the women merely looked on
awhile as interested spectators, and then “shooed
the tortured brute from off the premises, laughing
over the awkward movements displayed by its
broken limbs. They laughed—and I now know
what it is to hear hell in a laugh. —A Disgusted
Swagger.
Dear Bulletin, — Be your paragraphist
who sleeps best without pillows, a not-sufficiently-
noted phase of the insomnia-question is that,
except in a very general sense, there is no specific
remedy for this tremendous handicap upon mental
efficiency. Thus while one man sleeps upon coffee
and keeps awake upon tea, another exactly re-
verses these conditions. Also, while a moderate
amount of liquor will send A off and keep B
broad-awake, a heavy dose of the same may make
of A a log and of B a bundle of tingling sensibili-
ties. You might sleep best with much cold, I
with much heat, and yet a third be wakeful only
under either extreme. The only certain cure for
insomnia would be a more or less complete nervous
rehabilitation. Combine with a high degree of
nervous stability a tolerable exemption from dis-
tractions, and position will make little difference—
sleep will follow a given period of wakefulness as
inevitably as the night the day. The Chinaman —
who has no discoverable nerves—can slumber
peacefully standing on his head, with his legs tied
round his neck ! — Speculum.
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos Mark Twain’s
theory of mental telegraphy. I heard a piece of
news the other day, supposed to be secret, and,
following the usual rule in such cases, I communi-
cated it to a friend in Sydney, requesting him (as
is also usual) to keep it to himself, and I wound
up my letter with “ mum’s the word.” Two days
later I received a letter from my Sydney friend
informing me of the same bit of news, and,
stranger still, he ended his letter with the same
exhortation to be silent. The two letters had
crossed, and, on comparing notes, we found that
both were written and both were received on the
same days. Again: having nothing to do one
Saturday afternoon, I determined to telephone a
friend and ask him to ride with me to the race-
course. But ere I had time to ask to be connected
with my friend’s hotel I heard a ring at the
’phone, and, on answering it, found it to be from
the friend in question, who proceeded to ask me
the very same thing that I was about to ask him.
What made it all the more curious wa3 that
neither of us had been to the racecourse for some
time, and had never ridden there together before,
and that the race-meeting was an off one, with no
special interest to either of us. Both these events
may have been only coincidences; but I prefer to
explain them by Mark Twain’s theory.—Specta-

Job Fryer,
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“No, there ain’t much goes on in our
street as I don’t know on. I’ve got ’em all set, I
have. I know their histries and fakements, most
of ’em, a sight better nor they know theirselves.
They can’t flummox me ! ”
That explained it. I had often, since I came to
live in “our street,” wondered why this fat,
pasty-faced Job Pryer, of the large inquisitorial
nose, and the stealthy, scrutinising eyes, haunted
a seat in one corner of his verandah —haunted it
every day and all day, and at varied hours of the
night. While a vestige of daylight remained he
deserted his post only to snatch urgent interview's
with his food, frequently returning with his
mouth full and hunks of hot pudding in both
hands. If anyone passed while he was thus laden,
ho became so absorbed in his or her movements
that he would stoke and scald his ample mouth
with the pudding aforesaid. His sentry-box, so
to speak, commanded a view of the terrace he re-
sided in, the terrace on the opposite side of the
way, and, indeed, of the entire street. He owned
his own house and four others in our street. He
- toiled not, neither did he spin. He collected his
vrents and the scandal, veracious or otherwise—
“not seldom otherwise—of our streets for a living.
* ♦ * *
Job Pryer was upwards of 50, and far
from pretty. He had a yellow, greasy, creased
look about him, as if he had been a long time
folded up on a pawnbroker’s shelf. How he capti-
vated and succeeded in marrying his handsome
young wife is a problem I have never solved. She
was exceedingly nice to look at, with lovely dark
eyes and hair, pearly teeth, pouting red lips, and
a soul - satisfying figure. But she was more
demure than the demurest old-time Quakeress,
more nun-like than the devoutest nun. Numbers
of bold, bad young men in our street, arrayed in
blue serge suits, straw hats, and garden-beds in
their buttonholes, cast killing glances at Mrs.
Pryer, or, with the idea of “breaking her up,”
extended their chests and displayed their purple
and fine linen to her gaze as they paraded before
the window at which she often sat. But no
shadow of recognition, let alone encouragement,
escaped those pellucid, modest eyes. She
gazed over them, past them, as one who essays to
penetrate the dim vistas of eternity. She very
seldom went out. Job did not believe in “wimmin
gaddin’all over the bloomin’ shop.” He told me
so. “I’ve seen too much of that in our street,”
he said; “not that there’s any fear of her. I
could trust her with a ’ole regiment, and the
colonel thrown in. Blesh yer ! she’s been brought
up proper, she ’as—religious and moral, and all
that kind of think. Never wants to go out flyin’
round, she don’t; too fond of ’er ’ome and dooty
and old man ! ”
* * * *
“ Bub, good lor ! ” he continued, “ there’s
donahs in our very street, as they calls respectable,
reg’lar out-and-outers, and butter wouldn’t melt
in their mouths. Oh, no ! There’s a little woman
now as lives three doors from you, on your right;
’usband’s a commercial, he is. Gomes ’ome unex-
pected Friday night fortnight and finds a young
chap there. Folks ’bout here say as ’twas her
brother. Brother, me heye. I knows a ’ang’d
sight better. But her ’usband, poor fool, comes
out with him, friendly and laughin’, at 25 to 11,
by my watch, and they goes to the pub. together.
Nice game she carries on.”
I said I was sorry for that.
“Did ye ’ear the flamin’ row as there was at
number seven on Wednesday night—or rather
Thursday morning ?”
“ I didn’t hear any particular disturbance, only
Mr. Brown knocking two or three times. He’d
been to his lodge and forgot to take his latch-key,
and they were all asleep.”
“ Been to his lodge ! Bah ! Been to a lodge
with a chimley-pot to it. Been to the pub.,
a-flirtin’ with that ikey barmaid! That’s where
he’d been, and that’s where half the men in our
street pass their ev’nin’s. And why did they keep
him knockin’ ‘t Because someone ’ad to be let out
of the back door, that’s why. There’s nothin’ as
escapes Job Pryer.”
Just then a tall, slim, dignified lady, my next-
door neighbor, passed.
“Looks meek an’ mild an’sankeyfied, don’t she
now? Know where she’s agoin’? No? Well,
she’s agoin’ to buy a bottle of square-face gin, and
she’ll soak it to-night to her own cheek. Buys
four bottles a week, an’ ’er ’usban’ in theguv’ment
hemploy, ’oldin’ his ’ed ’igh, I can tell you. No,
there’s no carryin’s on in our street as I don’t
know—not one.”
Here Mr. Jones’ front door was opened, and
Mr. and Mrs. Jones—probably the most truly
affectionate and irreproachable couple in Sydney—
came down the steps.
“ Look at them! Just look at ’em ! Turtle-
doves, people calls’em. An’they fights like cat
and dog. Jones, he’s just got rid of a swollen
face, where she busted him in the jaw las’ Tuesday
week. ”
“ Oh, come, Mr. Pryer, that was toothache. I
went with Mr. Jones to the dentist while he had
the tooth drawn.”
“ Toothache ! I daresay, with your toothache !
They went to the thayatre Monday night, an’ he
kep’ lookin’ at a girl in the dress-succle. I ’eard
’em talkin’ about it as they passed my ’ouse.
Toothache ! Broom-’andle ache, more like ! ”
* * * *
Mr. Pryer had no family and very few
visitors. “ I don’t want folks a-foolin’ round my
’ouse, and a-pokin’ their noses into what don’t
consarn ’em,” he explained. An elderly hay bag
or two visited Mrs. Pryer once in a way. But
Job had a particular crony whom he always wel-
comed warmly —a mahogany-faced party of 65 at
least, whose voluminous epigastric protuberance
was cased in white waistcoat and festooned with
a watch-chain which looked like linked sausages of
precious metal. This patriarch walked slowly and
ponderously, and owned a peculiarly massive
double chin and a remarkably libidinous and roll-
ing eye.
“ One of the best informedest an’ most genteel-
est old blokes in .Sydney, is Mr. Sain Tucker,”
said Job to me. “ Been heverywheres and seen
hevery think, and that free-’earted as never was.
Got the mopuses, too, and terraces, ’as Tucker.
There’s only one think agen him—he can’t abide
the female sect —reg’lar woman-’ater, that’s what
he is ; he told me so. He’s just civil, so’s you can
say civil, to my missus. ‘1 looks on her,’ he says
to me, ‘ as a chit of a girl—a mere child,’ he says.
She’ates him. Me and she’s ’ad words about it
more than oncet; only I up and makes her be civil
to him when he comes to the ’ouse. I’m a man as
rules royal in my own ’ome, I can tell you,
mister.”
* Jf * *
Landlord Pryer became a tritie oppressive.
He eternally bailed me up as I passed, to favor
me with the more than criminal biographies of the
inhabitants—male, female, adult, and juvenile—of
our street. If I had had faith, even as a grain of
mustard-seed, in Job and his biographies, I must
have concluded that I had pitched my tent in the
most abandoned and infamous locality in all
Sydney. Moreover, I had the consolation of
knowing, through both the milkman and baker,
that Job Pryer represented me as being a pecu-
liarly virulent type of ruffian. He gave me to un-
derstand that there were not more than three
chaste females—of whom Mrs. Pryer was un-
doubtedly one, and her nice-looking servant.
Susan, was another —and only two honest men—
and Mr. Pryer was one of these —in our street.
“ It takes me to cipher ’em up,” he remarked.
“Talk about a heye like a ’awk ; I’ve got the
’awk heye, if you like. I’d a made a prime police-
man, or one of these ’ere private liinquiries, I
would.”
* * * *
There was something in connection with
the Pryer manage which gave me pain: On several
occasions—when Pryer and Tucker, in company,
had left the former’s house to adjourn to the
neighboring pub., where Pryer supplemented the
information he gathered in our street —I noticed
Tucker lag behind his host and slyly wave signs or
signals towards that host’s house. More than once
I saw a female, whose face I could not distinguish,
respond to the signals. Now, Pryer’s domestic,
Susan, Was a remarkably plump and well-favored
young person ; but if, as seemed probable, that
“reg’lar woman-’ater,” Tucker, was carrying-on
with the chaste Susan, it certainly seemed to me
that Job was mistaken, both in his jidus Achates
and his modest serving-maid. A friend to whom
I mentioned the matter pointed out, however, that
senile old gentlemen frequently affect towards
young females a manner at once playful and pater-
nal. That might be ; still I failed to understand
why the venerable Tucker invariably sneaked be-
hind Job’s back to bestow fatherly, or grand-
fatherly, salutation upon Susan.
* * * *
One evening I met Job a few hundred
yards from his own house, his face turned city-
wards. “I’m a hoddfellow,” he explained, “an’
I attends oncet a month, reg’lar, to pay my book
up. Sha’n’t be ’ome till eleven. The missis ain’t
well, and she’s gone to bed. Susan’s gone to a
prayer-meeting. Ain’t religious myself, but re-
ligion’s good for females. Talkin’ about that, I’ve
a story to tell you ’bout a woman in our street an’
her parson—my heyes, that is a story, that is ;
Tell you to-morrow, when you’re goin' in to town.
No time now.”
As I passed on, he shouted after me—“ They
can’t flummox me —nothin’ hescapes my heye ! ”
* * * *
I met someone else after this, and it was
quite half-an-hour before I arrived in our street.
When I came to Pryer’s house, it struck me as
strange that the lower room should be lighted,
for Job had a thrifty mind and wasted not gas,
and Mrs. Job had, so her husband had said, gone
to bed. The window was open. The blinds were
up. They had evidently been left up in error.
The lower room is really two rooms divided by
folding doors. Those doors were partially open,
and the light was burning in the back room.
There were only two persons present—Mrs. Pryer
and—“ the best informdest an’ most genteelest old
bloke in Sydney.” The former, looking ravish-
ingly beautiful, though not painfully demure,
wore a charming toilette of a loose and open
nature. The latter embraced and kissed the lady
with an energy somewhat remarkable in so pat-
riarchal a person.
* • * *
Even as I gazed, shocked and astounded
by so much treachery, I heard, without hearing, a
strident voice quite close to me say, “ Well, good
night; I forgot my book. I’ve come back for it.
I shall be late. I’ll tell you that yarn ter morrow.
Ive got ’em set. Nothin’ hescapes me ! ”
• * * *
Then there was an awful, a stentorian, an
apoplectic breathing at my left ear, and suddenly
someone fell in a heap at my feet, grabbing wildly
for the railings. I called a warning through the
window, stooped, and picked up—Job Fryer !
J. EVISON.
N.S.AV. Foreign-trade Government pro-
poses to be willing to give local manufacturers
preference over foreign manufacturers and im-
porters of foreign goods in respect of Government'
tenders. Commendable from a Protectionist

“In another hundred years the average
Australian will be a tall, coarse, strong-jawed,,
greedy, pushing, talented man, excelling in swim-
ming and horsemanship. His religion will be a
form of Presbyterianism, his national policy a
democracy tempered by the rate of exchange.
His wife will be a thin, narrow woman very fond
of dress and idleness, caring little for her children,
and without sufficient brain-power to sin with
zest.”— Marcus Clarke.
“At present,” says an English paper,
“ the skins of Australian kangaroos are much
prized ; a skin is worth more than an average
sheep—carcase, wool and all—for which reason
squatters are giving up sheep-farming for kangaroo
raising.” Yes, and bunyips are now being grown
in millions for their ivory tails, which are worth
more than a 10001 b. bullock, and the production of
emu’s milk has in five years increased 8000 per
cent. It is now the staple basis of Victorian
cheese.
Tobacco lias got to go in several American
universities. Boston university is returning fees
and giving clearance tickets to all students who
will not abjure “My Lady Nicotine.” Ohio
Wesleyan and other universities propose to do the
same next year. Statistics given are curious. In
1801, of a class of 147 at Yale, 77 non-smokers,
excelled 70 smokers in increase of weight by 10'4
per cent , of height by 24 per cent., of chest-girth
by 26 '7 per cent. At Amherst College the in-
crease-superiority of non-users of tobacco in any
form was for the four years’course—weight, 24 per
cent., height, 37 per cent., chest-girth, 42 percent.
At both Yale and Amhurst non-smokers showed
a superiority of increase in lung capacity of over
75 per cent. Unluckily the respective brains
couldn’t be measured.
